PUBLISHED AT SAN FRANCISCO, CAL., U.S.A.
The Argonaut publishes bright weekty letters from
New York, Paris, apd London, from skilled correspondents
who are in touch with, the intense life of those great cities.
It publishes frequent letters from other points in the
old world, and from the older world of Asia come occa-
sional letters from India, China, and Japan. Its short
stories.are famous throughout the world. It is hard to
describe their peculiar tinge-, but an idea may be gathered
from this, our standing injunction to our contributors :
“ Whatever you are, never be commonplace.” The re-
sult is that the Argonaut short stories have an individu-
ality of their own. The Argonaut endeavors to present a
weekly review of the literary movement. Brief notes on
books, rather than lengthy reviews, together with notes
upon authors and journalists, will keep the reader well
up with the literary gossip of the day.

THE RED PAGE.
Upstairs in the Brain.
In the 1200 pp. vol. of the Australasian Associa-
tion for the Advancement of Science there is
a paper on “Fact and Idea,” which by dint
of being most obscure looks almost as if it
might be very profound, by force of going
nowhere with great dignity almost looks as if it
might be going somewhere, and by reason of shed-
ding a large amount of darkness over things-in-
general almost persuades one that there must be
a luminous idea in the region from which so
much gloom has been abstracted. That is
to say, it is a metaphysioal paper, and there-
fore for the most part irrational anc} absurd.
For it is at last beginning to be understood that
metaphysics is an exploded science ; and that all
the metaphysicians from Aristotle to Kant, and
past Kant, have been “ hooting up a dead limb of
the tree of knowledge,” darkening counsel and
confusing understanding in precisely the fashion
of the groping essayist in the 1200-page volume
aforesaid. At the close of the nineteenth century
there is left but one primal science in the realm of
human knowledge—the science of Physics in
the widest sense, the science of things
natural, things as they are; and the
science of things dreamt of, dogmas invented,
baseless theories sent flying like stringless kites
into the domain of the imagination—the science of
Metaphysics —is in truth, if not in fact, as dead as
the. legendary cock that crowed to warn the
legendary Peter.
* • • m
All these centuries the metaphysicians have
been pokiiig the smoke ; the progress of physiology
has at last taught people—some people—to poke
the fire. Instead of reasoning from effects, it is
becoming more and more common to reason from
causes. And instead of losing himself in a maze
of words like “will,” “idea,” “concept,” “voli-
tion, ’ “ emotion, ” and so on, the scientist nowa-
days—the sensible scientist—scrutinises the mole-
cules of the brain, and learns what makes “ will,”
“emotion,” and the rest. The whole world of
psychology has practically been concentrated in the
microcosm of the skull. The germ of a brain re-
ceived from one’s parents, played on by all sights,
sounds, tastes, odours which reach it, works as
mechanically at any given moment as an engine
fashioned by men’s hands. There is no pin-hole
for chance to enter ; no break in the chain of law.
And from a complete knowledge of any brain and
its environment all dreams, actions, passions, can
more certainly, be predicated than from the steam-
power and burden, the shape and displacement of a
vessel, can be predicated its speed in a known water-
formingling and manglingterms and
definitions m relation to xis over. Every day
brings us more knowledge of the x in the cere-
brum, and long before the earth bursts or freezes
good brains will be cultivated like good oysters.
Unluckily, neither Kant nor Aristotle, nor a*ny
personal ghost of them, will be there to see.
* * * •
In a recent number of Mind , W. McDougall de-
velops further the theory of the nervous system
and its development and centralisation in the
oerebrum. The figure here given in illustration is
not so cryptic as it looks. Roughly speaking,
McDougall, regarding the brain and spinal cord as
a reservoir of impressions inherited from ancestors
or acquired by the individual, suggests that the
central nerve-cells may be ranged practically in
masses one above the other. Roughly speaking, he
divides the house of the brain into storeys. On
the ground-floor of the spinal cord are accumu-
lated the very-old cells with the very-old impres-
sions transmitted in the progress of evolution from
the very beginning of life. In the cerebellum
first-floor one . might say there are held
the cells of impressions stored for us by
the experience of more immediate ances-
tors. And in the second and third floors of
the cerebrum and cortex there are the cells that
we ourselves have trained to duty, and the reserve
stock of cells on which we rely to grapple
with fresh impressions. This is very loosely
phrased, but it will serve to suggest a leading idea
of McDougall’s theory. And the lines in the
drawing show imaginary nerve-paths by which
sensation and motion are communicated from one
cell to another and to the external organs—the
periphery.
* * * *
' McDougall suggests in his drawing the inter-
dependence of the nerve-centres. The simple
automatic reactions are suggested by the
lines from S to M—sensory nerves to motor
nerves— on the first level. Perhaps half
the actions of our lives are performed on
this automatic level, with an immense saving
of wear and tear. Were our ears, for example,
not_ trained to pass insignificant sounds without
nofioe, the din of life would be insupportable.
Talking in a railway train, the rattlemf the cars
goes unheeded. The'bounds are faithfully re-
ceived, but they pass dff by the waste-pipe of the
automatic centres, without distracting the conscious
centres employed in thinking of tire conversation.
The complexity of sensations on the third storey,
where most of the conscious work is done, is
suggested by the more numerous nerve-paths
in the drawing. Aifd the educational lesson
of the whole is thaft during the youth of
every individual as many spheres of know-
ledge and action ,as possible should be' brought
under the oontrol of the automatic centres, so
that se can think and do those spheres with the
least possible expenditure of energy, reserving sis
nervous force for new ideas and unexpected situa-
tions. In the same way as a pianist can play and
sing at the same time, it is conceivable that a
well-endowed human being may compose a
poem, read a newspaper, resolve a chess problem,
invent an improvement in gold-saving appliances!
converse with his wife about frocks, and do half-
a-dozen other things—all at the same instant, and
each with a different area of his brain.
. ‘ Rolf Boldrewood ” has deliberately fallen on
evil days. Five years ago The Bulletin told
him that be was taking advantage of a confiding
public by trading on his reputation, and writing
fluent rubbish instead of taking pains. The truth
has slowly filtered down to English and American
critics, and his last two or three books have been
deservedly characterised as dull and flatulent
Says a recent Athenceum —
It is difficult to know what to say about the stories
“ A Romance of Canvas Town,” &c., by Rolf Boldre
wood (Macmillan & Co.). They are so elementary in con
struction that they almost defy criticism. In none o
them is there the slightest suggestion of inventiveness
they are incidents which probably happened, told with
some detail, but with very little point. But none the
less, as a social study, the book is interesting for it
obvious insight into life in the Australian bush. Adven
tures at the gold diggings or out sheep farming, the ditfi
culties of domestic service, and such like matters are tolc
with the air of a man who has experienced what he writes
about, and are brought out clearly before the reader. li,
is just the same quality, although on a slightly different
topic, that was displayed in “ Robbery Under Arms.”
That is mild in comparison with some recent
notices. Even the author of “ When the Mopoke
Calls,” who was puffed by his publishers as “a
nephew of ‘ Rolf Boldrewood ’ ” —whatever that
had to do with his quality — does not escape un-
scathed from the shower of dispraise ; and has
lost, rather than gained, by his advertised relation-
ship.
The development of the great English language
is fascinating to watch. Take an American re-
port of a baseball-match, verbatim :
Fifth Inning.— Brown netted two corners. Smith and
Jones nipped Robinson, but Thompson crossed the trocha.
Johnson caught Green’s liner. White banged a home-run.
Walker was grazed by a hot one and took his base.
M Carthy caught Weber’s boost. Two runs.
Second Half.—A grounder to Walker was the cause of
M Carthy’s demise. Walker and Green sent Smith to the
bench. Hill and Yale settled Brooks. No runs.
Sixth Inning.— Mills lifted an easy fly. Smith and
Johnson disagreed over Wilson’s high and he took two
bags. Richardson accounted for Wilkinson’s Steepler.
Bronson flied to Jones. No runs.
Second Half.—O’Rourke singled. Jones promenaded.
Hill pulled in M‘Carthy’s rise. Smith forced Jones
ORourke caught at third. Brooks knocked a homer
scoring Jones and Smith. Wilkinson walked. Smith
singled and took one more on error. Wilkinson tallied
Bronson stole third. Walker captured M‘Carthy’s lofty
foul. Three runs.
Spenser’s exemplar—
Dan Chaucer, well of English undefiled
On fame’s eternal bead-roll worthy to be filed—
should have lived hereafter, in order to write
“The Baseball Pitcher’s Tale.” What a heroic
record, with the slang to fill it with colour and
music !

Kipling’s satirical “praise” of Guy Boothbv
runs thus— J
Mr. Guy Boothby has come to great honours now. His
name is large upon hoardings, his books sell like hot
cakes, and he keeps a level head through it all. I’ve met
him several times in England, and he added to mv
already large respect for him. y
And Boothby’s publishers naively print this as
complimentary 1
Nathaniel Hawthorne.
Siok with a surfeit of new books, it is relief and
gloom at once to turn to Hawthorne. Latter-dav
literature deals so largely with the activities oi
life, the march of science, social problems anc
the parade of old thoughts in a new guise (skil-
fully arranged with intent to deceive), that the
world'grows tired of the multitudinous voices
though they be very varied in register and tone’
It is like walking m a market-place where huck-
sters cry their goods. And it is like turning
aside from the market-place to open Hawthorne-
like entering some grey cathedral where no sound
ol man s feet is anywhere, and the light from
great windows, made red and violet in transmis-
sion through the painted panes, reveals invisible
dust strangely glorified. Solemnity shadows one,
and though he may be in the world he is not of it
A supreme spell takes him in thrall, and grave-
eyed thoughts that have long hided in cloistral
corners steal out and look him in the eyes.
Q . Hawthorne .charms us with his, remoteness
Strange' to say, 'the endearing charm of Kipling
lies in his nearness. They occupy opposite poles
of enchantment. It would be hard to find two
other writers so altogether unlike in theme and
atmosphere. Kipling is intensely modern. He
stands on the bridge of the world and feels the
shudder and .the throb of the gigantic mind-
engine that is driving it onward— -sunward or
niglitward, who will say ? His must be new blood
fresh-made by nature.
On the other hand, and for Hawthorne, the
century displayed its treasures in vain. He had
only cold eyes for them. Men wrought and
thought, and bought and soldi even at his elbows
but his mission was not with them or their doings
Within himself was a sufficient world peopled
strangely. He drew the shadows from their lurk-
ing and gave them a theatre. That theatre was
not always the Past, but sometimes something
antiquely resembling it. Because he was so lonely
m the midst of life, so markedly out of date, so
vaguely apart, it is a wonder and a dream almost
to think on . him. His must have been very
ancient blood indeed, set running long ago when
the race was in the cradle.
Look into your own heart and write is good
advice, but difficult to follow. We are so strangely
fashioned that these eyes of ours have a truant’s
habits and wander where they list. Thus it is
tha,t nine of us out of every ten fail in inward
vision. The tenth is sometimes a Shakespeare or
a Hawthorne.
When Hawthorne sat down to write and turned
his eyes inward it was a grim and gloomy drama
that greeted them. Sm paced the scene with Ke-
morse in pursuit. Seldom is the red act or the
hot embrace presented as in Shakespeare. The
curtain lifts on the after-effect, the coming of Con-
science leading in, with one hand, the desire •
and, with the other, the fear of Expiation. So it
is m the 4 Scarlet Letter, ” the woman with the
c< A 55 on her garb and the child in her arms—the
punishment and the outcome of sin. Nowhere do
we get a picture of the love that went first—went
before the sin—sweetening existence even for a
little while.
Some say that Hawthorne made a novel where
he might have been content with a short story,
and gave to short-story themes sufficient for
majestic expansion. This is only saying that he
was not always a master of suggestion. Kipling’s
“End of the Passage” is more deeply laden with
intensity than anything Hawthorne has done.
Still, to say that his stories are unsuggestive is
not to deny suggestion to his style. It is replete
with side-effects. His stories are like corridors
twilit and paved with polished marble, down
which he leads us gravely pacing, and with a sense
of awe upon us. Here to our right his hand goes
out and a door swings wide, revealing a room full
of faded sunlight. There to the left a second
door grows ajar, and we catch a glimpse of a for-
gotten coffin heaped over with withered wreaths ;
and then, perhaps, we become seized and teased
with a desire to plumb the depths of hinted
mystery, and pause, and gaze, and go a-graping
till our eyes tire and our minds sink back abashed
with defeat. It was more than the coffin and the
faded wreath that the master intended we should
see; and the impressiveness and the exaltation
with which he displayed them cry out to kindred
feelings within us. This is how high imaginative
writing, such as Hawthorne’s, may make a Via
Dolorosa a way of roses, or the rosy way thorny,
and as you will.
Perhaps the sweetest writing of the language is
that in “Daniel Swan.” Certainly, if it is not
the sweetest, it is sweet as any other. It is a
clear pool, where blue and painted- skies lie
mirrored. Stevenson’s best seems turbid in
its neighborhood. Its diction is simplicity and
grace closely wedded. Its conception is familiar as
a dream, and yet as distant. It was gathered from
the frontier of the Communicable, far as the fet-
tered mind can stray.
The test of Art is m the persistence of the artist’s
work. In proportion to the amount of soul he
projects into the canvas soshall the result prosper,
even though Time make constant war on it.
Exquisite witness to Hawthorne’s soul and skill
and dramatic power is borne by the long galaxy of
scenes and faces that cling to the walls of our
memory. Put your mind in service and try how
many you can remember. There, for instance, is
Dimmesdale, sick with inward travail and very
pale, standing on the platform in the red, meteoric
light, his sin made manifest, as though God sat in
judgment. And there, again, in the Minister’s
Black Veil, see the Minister bending over the
dead maiden and—
“As he stooped, the veil hung straight down
from his forehead, so that if her eyelids
had not . been closed for ever, the dead
maiden might have seen his face. Could Mr.
Hooper have been so fearful of her glance that he
hastily caught back the veil ? A person who
watched the interview between the dead and the
living scrupled not to affirm that, at the instant
when the c-lerg.yman’s features'were disclosed, the
corpse had-slightly shuddered, rustling fhe shroud
and muslin cap, though ■'the countenance retained
the composure of death.”
And last, there is 'that great canvas in the
“ Unpardonable Sin,” with Ethan Brend on'the
hillside, pain in Ins heart and on his face the in-
tense light of burning marble.
Whatever place Hawthorne may occupy amongst
writers, he hr'certainly unique in English litera-
ture. And because he is original, graceful, and
intense, it is well to read him twice over at the
least. Butfstill, when all is said and done, there
are some needs he does not satisfy. The heart
cries out for heart-food—permanently hungry for
the milk of human kindness—groping for the
truth that makes the whole world kin, and desir-
ing over and above psychological introspection,
however deep and fine, a lighter communion, a
talk with ordinary individuals like Laura, and
Pen, and Olive, and Ethel, and all the other sim-
ple boys and girls and men and women that
Thackeray, good man, has given us.
Roderic Quinn,
Mixed Verses.

ET MI SIT SATUBITATEM* IN ANI-
MAS EOBUM.”
Pis. ovi. IS,
*Tis well, ’tis well, in the lowest hell,
And I laugh at the lick of the fire ;
The flame roars up in its brimstone cup,
And it lives what lies on the pyre ;
They’ve done their worst for the man they cursed,
They’ve made damnation dire ;
But they can’t undo the joy I knew
When I knew my Heart’s Desire.
There’s one will scream and one blaspheme,
As they writhe in the molten mire,
And curse the earth that gave them birth,
And damn their dam and their sire;
It pleases the devils to watch their revels,
But I wake the devils to ire
When I burst into rhyme at the thought of tile
time
When I found my Heart’s Desire.
They wrench my back on a red-hot raok,
They comb my nerves with wire,
They poison with pain the blood of my brain,
Till the devils of devilry tire ;
They spit from above on the name of my Love,
They call my Love a liar,
But they can’t take away the dream of the day
When I won my Heart’s Desire.
So here in the hell it is all very well
And I’m snug as a beast in a byre,
The devils are beat by my thought of my sweet,
And a fig for the heavenly choir :
And blood scalds skin where the flame bites in :
But I like the warmth of the fire,
For it burns me through with the heat I knew
When I held my Heart’s Desire.
Oh Heart’s Desire, who art not by my side,
Whose Love, so sweetly lived, so strangely died,
Thro’ faltering, falseness, failure, and the fire
Be still for ever all my Heart’s Desire.
*Arridet nobis lectio Hebraica, maciem.

THE FUNERAL OF THE LATE MRS.
HODGSON.
Mrs. ’Odgson worn’t nutthink—did nutthink—
said nutthink
Anywise remawkable so fur as I can tell;
’Usband, ’e were on the line, on a trine, sime as
mine,
Left it fur the fried-fish, and ain’t doin’ over well
She’d a femily of four—well, thur’s ’eaps of us ’as
more;
Thur were nutthink in ’er fice ter cause ’er pride,
O, Mrs. ’Odgson looked nutthink —’ad nutthink—
was nutthink,
But wasn’t theer a kick-up when she died !
E’d ’is frien’s in, ite or nine—did it fine—sherry
wine,
Plite o’ best mixed biscuits, and extrevygance in
kike,
Crorss come from ’er brother Willy—harum lily—
knock yer silly,
Which fur" ’arf-a-thick-un I’d bin sad ter ’ave ter
mike.
An’ the childring ’ad black mittings, an’ thecorfin
plited fittings,
And a verse was on the mournin’-card supplied ;
Though Mrs. ’Odgson saw nutthink—’eard nut-
think—knew nutthink,
They was very cormpliment’ry when she died.
Mournin’ coaches bein’ wide, they cud ride six
inside,
The ’earse ’ad plumes upon it, all were clawsy as
cud be,
I watched ’ey ridin’ orf, like a torf—mide me corf
Ter think with all I’d done they’d never thought
o’ awskin’ me!
Though I’d lent ’em things o’ mine, sich as cork-
screw fur the wine,
They’d gort too grand ter offer me a ride,
O, Mrs. ’Odgson looked nutthink—’ad nutthink—
was nutthink,
But she was the Queen o’ Sheber when she died.
Barry Pain.
IN CITY STREETS, 1898.
Yonder in the heather there’s a bed for sleeping,
Drink for one athirst, ripe blackberries to eat ;
Yonder in the sun the merry hares go leaping,
And the pool is clear for travel-wearied feet.
Sorely throb my feet, a-tramping London high-
ways,
(Ah ! the springy moss upon a northern moor !)
Through the endless streets, the gloomy squares
and byways,
Homeless in the City, poor among the poor !
London streets are gold—ah, give me leaves
a-glinting
Midst grey dykes and hedges in the autumn sun !
London water’s wine, poured out for all un-
stinting—
God ! for the little brooks that tumble as they
run !
Oh, my heart is fain to hear the soft wind blowing
Soughing through the fir-tops up on northern fells!
Oh, my eye’s an ache to see the brown burns
flowing
Through the peaty soil and tinkling heather-bells.
Ada Smith.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
“ In the Australian Bush and on the Coast
of the Coral Sea ” (Macmillans, London), is the
outcome of naturalist Richard Semon’s two years’
sojourn in Australia, Papua, and the M!oluccas,
and embodies such of his observations and im-
pressions as could not well have been fitted into
his more strictly scientific book upon the same
basis—now being published in parts. This less
formal work—pleasantly written and liberally
illustrated—will be of all the more interest
to Australians in that the author s cen-
tral zoologic purpose has not prevented him
from keeping his eyes about him generally,
with the result that Australia and Australians
come in for some very qandid though good-na.tured
criticism. To students of local natural history
this book will be of special value, as definitely
setting at rest certain vexed- questions in connec-
tion with Marsupialia and Monotremata.
The Free Minstrel.
« Now Hugo the minstrel was a proud man, and he
wandered through the Midlands, Kinging and seelang
favor of no man.” — Old Chronicle.
As a freeman I lived, as a freeman I boro me,
I never bent knee to the pomp of the proud.
And the guerdon I prized was the blue heaven
o’er me,
And I loved the stiff neck that no man ever
bowed;
No lands could I boast as my acres paternal,
No lackeys beset me, a supple-lmeed throng,
I was but as a bird in her freedom supernal—
No fortune was mine but a morsel of song !
And had I but done as I saw them all doing,
God wot!—l had never gone empty away ;
For Dives is deaf to no sycophant’s sueing
And he bends a glad ear to the adulant lay.
No voice could I venture to add to the clamour
Of clients who swept him in glory along,
And I sang as I laughed at his .glitter and
glamour —
“No fortune be mine but a morsel of song ! ”
The love of bright eyes that enthrals us and cap-
tures —
O sweet was your charm that I dared to discard !
For chains were ye weaving of roses and raptures
To fetter the wings of a penniless bard.
Yea, deep was the spell, but I rose and I reft
me —
(Forgive me, dear heart, if I did you a wrong);
’Twas the only resource that my Destiny left me
No fortune was mine but a morsel of song !
No fortune was mine but a musical measure
That sometimes ran o’er in a jubilant sea,
For the gods ever kind had entrusted a treasure
That burst from my breast in a song for the Free ;
And my melodies change at the will of the Sender
Now rising aloft in a strain for the strong,
Now dying adown in a love-lilt so tender —
No fortune is mine but a morsel of song !
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.
Villanelle.
They spoke of Love : the shadows fell
Upon the hills, and on the town ;
And Love was ill, or Love was well.
They followed Love—the wedding-bell;
The marriage gift; the bridal gown ;
They spoke of Love : the shadows fell.
And dallying, they turned to tell
Of grey old age, and tresses brown—
And Love was ill, or Love was well:
Or softly spake, with heart a-swell,
Of old regrets they could not drown—
They spoke of Love: the shadows fell.
They followed Love—the subtle spell
Through heart and soul, and up and down ;
And Love was ill, or Love was well.
They followed, from the jaws of hell
And on, and up to Heaven’s crown—
They spoke of Love : the shadows fell,
And Love was ill, or Love was well.
Brisbane.
FRANCIS KENNA.
A Little Song.
Last night I heard a song you used to sing,
And like a dart it pierced me through and through,
For every note held memories of you ;
And in my ears the little plaintive thing
Rang all the evening—gas-lamps burning low
Changed into starlight, and the merry flow
Of conversation into murmuring
Of winds and waves, and sounds of long ago.
Dear little song! Dear love, throughout long
years,
Cold stars have shone alike on smiles and tears.
In my sad heart my love was buried deep,
And other loves have danced upon its grave ;
I thought it dead, but from its heavy sleep
The little song aroused it, and it gave
One startled cry—then the dear eyes of yore
Opened and gazed into my own once more,
Half smilingly, yet half-inclined to weep.
Dear eyes ! What fancy brought yours here to-
night ?
As blue as cornflowers, sweet, and starry bright !
And every fond word uttered then again
Arose, all trembling from the mist of years,
And fluttered towards me, echoing in my ears,
Twining itself about the simple strain ;
Vowing by all those hours of happiness,
Each lingering glance, each kiss and fond caress,
No more to sleep—but ever clear remain.
Dear tender words ! O, memories bright and fair,
All whispering in the sweet, pathetic air !
AIRLIE.

IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
I through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and life
was an old man, Jeremiah and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Hoodie was heard as never
was heard before or since.

SATURDAY, APRIL 22, 1899.
The Pawnbroker of Salvation.
The crowd shouts ; and “ General ” Booth
advances to his platform-table. He talks,
jests, prays, deprecating and imprecating
alternately. His feet shuffle on the floor ;
his arms are flung out with the gesture of a
Jonah cursing Nineveh. He comes for-
ward, goes back ; is now on this side, now
on the other ; but it is when he is behind
his table, with body bent, face lowered, and
chin thrust forward, that you get the charac-
teristic impression of him. The O-glasses
on his eyes glitter in the light ; the O-cavity
of his mouth hangs between them, suspended
by that formidable Nose of all the world ;
and the audience may, if it chooses, interpret
the historic three-ball hieroglyph and under-
stand the man—the Pawnbroker of Salva-
tion.
* * * *
Salvation !—on earth and in “ heaven ” it
is charity—the charity of good deeds, the
charity of innocence sacrificed. Pawnbrok-
ing ! —the extortion of shekels ; the grinding
of the faces of the poor ; the uttermost
abyss of Business. The Salvation Army is
a charitable business, a business charity.
Luxurious living for the few at the top ; the
dry bread of self-denial for the toiling thou-
sands at the bottom. Free meals for the
starving—in exchange for clothes washed
and wood chopped ; homes for the homeless
—if they conform to the dogmas, sing the
hymns, and pray the prayers of the Army ;
and always the Business idea kept in view—
nothing for nothing, food and shelter in re-
turn for the expenditure of physical energy,
or the loss of mental self-respect. Every-
where and continually wealth being piled
up ; the sources of charity exploited to the
last doit and crumb ; a profit made on gene-
rosity, and sixty per cent, interest demanded
from every gift. Strike out the charity,
which gives and asks nothing in return ; and
leave the Business. The Salvation Army, as
an organisation, is a Business. “ General ”
Booth is justified of his Nose.
• • * •
A remarkable man, no doubt; with pro-
d gious energy, and organising talent, and
Ideas. He has his reward in that Power
which is worth to an ambitious man all the
virtues, all the vices. But it is Power
obtained at the expense of thousands who
starve and sweat in the shadow at the root
of the tree, that he may sit in the sunshine
at the top. The credit of the poor, sallow,
narrow-brained fanatic who slaves for him,
suffused by the glow of Faith, is not his
credit; the sacrifice of the female “cap-
tain ” or “ adjutant ” scouring the streets
to succor the weak and afflicted, living on
a crust‘that she may remit the uttermost
farthing exacted by “ head-quarters,” is not
his sacrifice ; the labor and prayers of enthu-
siasts reproving “vice,” aiding the “fallen,”
denouncing the “wicked”—these are not
“General” Booth’s labor and prayers. The
good that there is in the Army belongs not
to the Army, not to the “General,” but to
the humble soldier who sees the good, and
feels the good, and does the good. He is
the honest Fly whose entrails the huge
Spider sucks.
* • * *
The Salvation Army is a Business ; and
the business idea overshadows all else. No
comprehensive statement of its assets has ever
been published ; but they must be millions
sterling. Everything is sacrificed to the
Main Chance. In the chief cities of
Australia we see the Salvation carts col-
lecting, collecting, collecting—meat from
butchers, bread from bakers, contributions
in cash or in kind, contributions in clothes,
broken victuals, bottles, old bones, any-
thing. What can be sold is sold ; what
cannot, is eaten and worn and used by the
“officers” in the homes, and the poor
wretches who tenant them. Every branch
to be self-supporting; every branch to for-
ward to “ head-quarters ” its monthly dole ;
every branch to increase that dole by the
last penny that the unpaid labor of the
“savers” and the “saved” can produce.
The man who goes to a Salvation “ home ”
in Sydney gets work and food. If he stays
there a month, he gets—work and food.
When he departs, he is rarely richer for his
labor : he has existed for a month, that is
all. And, in nine cases out of ten,
his work has been worth twenty times
the value of the broken victuals given
to him between long prayers. The
“charity” of the Salvation Army is
the business-charity of starvation wages or
no wages at all. Its “religion” is the
business-religion of “head-quarters”—slave,
sell, starve, and send the Cash to “head-
quarters.” Its “god” is the Almighty
business man—“ General ” Booth.
* * * *
In the Australian country the Salvation
spectacle is even more saddening. Doubt-
less some of the “ officers ” of the Army are
mere fanatics, under the sway of religious
or sexual impulse ; bawling, banging, and
raving to no good end. But many—the
majority, maybe—are good, earnest men and
women, whose lives are patterns of true
piety, and whose bodies are daily thrown
under the wheels as the immense Jugger-
naut-car of the Army moves triumphantly
forward. Many of these poor creatures get
no wages ; or a shilling or two of pittance.
They have to “ live on the enemy,” get the
wherewithal to sustain life from charitable
contributions, and at the same time carry
on the charitable work which is the
Army's ostensible justification, and send
money to “ head-quarters.” In the country
towns humanity’s astonishing spirit of self-
abnegation is taken advantage of to the
fullest extent. The War Cry stimulates
the “soldiers” by publishing rival lists of
papers sold and moneys received by “ head-
quarters.” Some of the “ captains ” display
wonderful ingenuity in raising funds. And,
every now and again, upon a town which has
been “ organised ” into the payment of regu-
lar contributions, there descends a “Com-
missioner” or a “Major” announcing a
special week of self-denial and prayer. The
fountains of charity are drained to the last
drop; and the “Major” returns with beat
of drum and a big cheque to “ head-
quarters,” while the miserable “soldiers”
left behind wonder vaguely how they will
make their usual remittance, and sell their
War Crys, and subsist, in the desert where
the “head-quarters” locusts have eaten
every green blade of pence and ha’pence.
“ General ” Booth boasts that his
“colonies” are self-supporting. No won-
der ; when he gets his labor free. He
boasts that the Army prospers. No wonder;
since it is the sweating of flesh-and-blood
reduced to a science.
♦ * * *
How is it done ? By the erethism and
altruism of mankind, and by the Nose of the
“ General.” The Nose of the “ General ”is
its own interpreter, and needs no explana-
tion. The enthusiasm of the “soldiers”
depends upon many things. The Army sup-
plies them with excitement—that Hospital-
Saturday joy for which so many bored
women crave ; it gives them an occupation,
a career, and a sanctified aim. There is
always the heavenly bunch of carrots to lure
the donkey onward. Thousands of persons
—especially of women are everywhere
willing to live a life of mean labor now, that
they may ecstatically embrace the Spirit and
the Bride hereafter. Then there are the
flags, and drums, and shouting—the con-
sciousness of being a public personage—the
gratification of vanity in “saving a soul,”
or delivering an address, or achieving
the greatest sale of the War Crys,
or remitting the largest, or the third
largest, or the tenth largest contribution
to “head-quarters.” Then “civilisation”
has modestly suppressed the public exhibi-
tion of those rooted instincts which formerly
found vent in festivals of Aphrodite, or in
love-feasts, or in kermesses, and a dozen
similar orgies. The Salvation Army diverts
these impulses into religious channels, as
has been done by many “moral” organisa-
tions before it; but it gives them a field for
display more satisfying to weak intellects
than any other mode of religion is giving.
The Army soldieress, dancing and tambourin-
ing, is the modern Bacchante, and looks it.
“General” Booth uses a plough which
seems new, but he is merely ploughing the
same old human ground in the same old
furrow.
* * * •
Does the Army do any permanent good ?
Practically none. Doubtless many indivi-
duals have been temporarily bettered by
contact with its self-denying enthusiasts ;
but the system of the Army is always
more palliative than preventive. While
it draws from the service of the community
many men and women who might become
useful citizens, and wastes their time in pro-
menading the streets and collecting for
“ head-quarters,’’’ it gives the community no
equivalent of men and women turned from
evil lives to good. To come in close relation
with its methods as a recipient of relief is to
be mentally and physically deteriorated. And
this deterioration will never stop while
“ General ” Booth is allowed to teach, even
encouraged to teach, that the practice of
“ charity ” consists in building “barracks,”
and amassing funds, and acquiring landed
property, while paying no wages, or the
meanest wages, for labor, and doing little or
nothing to find independent places for its
proteges—-or victims—in the social machine.
Sydney against Australia.
Sydney against New South Wales, Sydney
against Australia :—that is the present and
final stage of the Federal conflict. It is a
straight-out, clean-cut issue. When the
Federal vote comes to be taken, New South
Wales electors will in fact be asked, “Do you
vote for a continuance and aggravation of the
Sydney tyranny ? Do you vote for a continu-
ance and extension of the Sydney monopoly ?
Or do you vote for the free development of
New South Wales ? Do you vote for Aus-
tralia ? ”
• * • m
This article is written for all Australians.
It is written for the New South Wales country
voters. And it is written for the Sydney city
voters who have sense enough to see that the
continual piling-up of bricks and mortar in one
spot of New South W 7 ales at the expense of the
whole province is a manace to the province and
the nation, that the continual spending of public
money in one spot at the expense of the whole
province is a menace to the province and the
nation, that the continual gathering of popula-
tion to one spot at the expense of the whole
province is a menace to the province and the
nation.
* * * *
The men on the land—and the men in the
Sydney streets; the struggling digger and
farmer and grazier—and the Sydney landlord ;
the men who are the backbone of the country,
and who fight drought and flood to win tlie
wealth of the country—and the Sydney civil-
servant who is a necessary or unnecessary
parasite on their industry ; —-which will you
vote for ? In N.S. Wales, and at the coming
contest, Federation is summed up in that.
* * * *
The anti-Federal agitation was begun in
Sydney ; it is sustained in Sydney ; and, please
Australia ! it will die in Sydney. The
centre and essence of the anti-Federal
agitation is the struggle to maintain and
extend the Sydney supremacy—at the cost of
N.S.W. country districts, at the cost of Aus-
tralia. Just as a cancer lives and thrives in
the body, sending out rootlets in all directions,
and drawing to itself all the strength of the
organism, so, in great part, does Sydney live
and thrive at the expense of the N.S.W.
coastal districts, at the expense of the centre,
at the expense of the West. It is a bitter jest
against the bush township that you can fire a
gun down its main street without danger to
anybody—because, by an unwise policy, the
industry and energy which should make that
township prosper have been sucked to Sydney.
There is a bitter jest against the country news-
paper—“ the country rag ” —and why ? because
the big Sydney paper has supplanted it, thanks
to free carriage by the State which charges
every other tradesman. There is a bitter jest
against the men who “go on the land,” and
why ? because Sydney has been so far subsi-
dised at the expense of the country districts
that to “go on the land ” is often to go into
hell.
* * * *
Sydney as a port will always take first place
in Australia ; but there is no warrant for the
extravagant expenditure of public money on
Sydney ; for draining 31 per cent, of the whole
population to Sydney ; for erecting enormous
Government buildings, filled with an enormous
staff of clerks, receiving and spending enormous
sums in wages, in Sydney. New South Wales
has lost the use of the population of Sydney as
servants of the community. They are masters
of the community ; they make and unmake
Ministries ; —instead of the country ruling
the capital, the capital rules the coun-
try. Every year, as the aggrandisement
of Sydney continues, the outlying districts are
proportionately impoverished. The more people
there are collected in Sydney, the more mouths
there are to feed, the more public money is dis-
pensed to help feed them. The backblocks are
defrauded in two ways. They are defrauded
of the money which should be spent, not in
feeding an unproductive population, but in as-
sisting the pioneers of productive industry.
They are also defrauded of the men who should
be the pioneers of industry, but who find that
employment is obtained under pleasanter con-
ditions in Sydney.
* * * *
Many good artisans, hence many valuable
citizens, are collected in and about Sydney, but
they do not all add value to their work ; many
of them add only price. And few or none of
them are primary producers ; that is, few or
none of them belong to the class on whom, in
the last resort, the strength of a nation de-
pends. While Australia has so much natural
wealth at disposal—while N.S. Wales has
minerals to dig, land to till—we want above all
things that as large a section as possible of the
population should be actual producers of wealth
—men who make grass grow and corn wave
where none grew before—not merely men who
take the raw product and enhance its commer-
cial worth. These have their place, too ; but
it is not the first, the most essential, place.
* * * *
The N.S.W. pyramid is tottering on its apex.
The population of Sydney—one town alone—is
417,000; of the whole of the rest of the pro-
vince, 906,000. The province is depending for
its life upon a comparative handful of pro-
ducers—and upon loan-moneys, with the inter-
est and the capital charged to posterity.
* * * *
That state of things cannot last. But the
nest of anti-Federalists in Sydney wish not
only to maintain, but to extend, the policy of
starving the country, starving the producers,
to bloat Sydney with men and money. New
South Wales does not want the Federal
capital at Sydney: Sydney is already far
too huge for the health and safety of the
province. New South Wales would be more
stable, more prosperous, with the popu-
lation of Sydney distributed over a dozen
towns, over a hundred districts. Sydney
is a great belly, which, according to old
iEsop’s fable, is starving the members, is steal-
ing from the country the blood and money
needed to make the country thrive.
* * * *
It is time to end all this. If the Federal
capital diverts enterprise and money and popu-
lation from Sydney, so much the better. New
South Wales comes first. Australia comes
first. And every man in N.S. W. who has the
interests of the province at heart, every man
in Australia who has the interests of the nation
at heart, will vote in the coming contest against
Sydney—for N.S. Wales and Australia !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Our Oriental Express.
When the Commonwealth gets on its legs—and
the eagerness of prospective Ministers and
Judges will now, no doubt, consummate Fede-
ration in record time—the traditional brilliancy
and scintillating genius of Australian P.M.G’s
will at last have a fair field of operation. The
Commonwealth will, of course, in taking over
the postal services, make the mail-contracts
with the steamship companies. And in this
connection the possibility presents itself of
radical changes in the mail-routes, which may
reduce the present time of journey to Europe
by one-half. A divergence of the transconti-
nental line from Port Darwin through Queens-
land (so as to pass through good country and
to tap the termini of the great trunk-lines from
the east coast) would not materially lengthen
the journey, even from Adelaide, and would
bring the Northern Territory into direct rail-
way communication with the chief towns of
Australia. Linked by such a railway to the
masses of our population, its splendid harbor
opening out direct to the swarming East with
its enormously expanding trade, Port Darwin
must become the great entrepot for European
and Eastern mails and passengers, and bids
fair to rival Sydney, at the other extremity
of the line, as a strategic and commercial
centre. By an express steamship service, such
as now runs between Brindisi and Port
Said, the distance between Port Darwin and
the new Siberian railway terminus should be
covered in a day or so over the week. The
Russian engineers anticipate the opening of
the railway next year and promise to send
passengers across the two continents in eight
days—making little more than a fortnight
from our northern port. In the south of Asia
schemes are already mooted for connecting the
Indian system through Persia with the railways
under construction from Constantinople towards
the Persian Gulf, and the Burmese railways are
creeping down the Malay Peninsula to Singa-
pore. Give eight days for the railway journey
from Singapore to Calais via Calcutta and
Constantinople and five days for the water
journey ; we can luxuriously travel by a short,
smooth ocean route from Port Darwin to
Singapore, skirting the matchless scenery of
those “ summer isles of Eden,” and finish by a
railway trip which packs the wonders of the
world into a week. Two days will reach every
important Australian centre from Port Darwin.'
So we will get our London news in a fortnight,
and can take our choice of looking in on either
the Czar or the Sultan for the Christmas holi-
days. These considerations will serve to show
the value to the Commonwealth of the Northern
Territory. Its transfer from the control of
S.A. to that of the Commonwealth, so far from
being the burden on the national finances which
desperate anti-Federalists predict, is certain to
endow the nation with an asset of enormous
value. We may be devoutly thankful that
Northern Australia has so far escaped the
chartered companies and gridiron syndicates
whose greedy maw has several times opened for
the gulp.
A Victorian Draco.
The sentences recently passed by Judge Gaunt
at Melb. Gen. Sessions once more demonstrate
the extreme variability in the incidence of
punishment, according to the personality of
Judges. It is surely a gross scandal on Justice
that an offence which at the previous Ci iminal
Sessions had been adjudged by the then pre-
siding Judge as expiable by 6 months’ “ hard,”
should be next month punished by a sentence
twelve times as severe. Judge Gaunt’s liver, or


gall, or something, made him ladle out to 10
prisoners (housebreaking, horse-stealing and
grievous bodily harm) an aggregate of 46 years
hard labor, trimmed with 64 weeks of
“solitary” and, in the case of the “bodily
harm,” with a solatium of 15 lashes thrown in.
This last—bar the flogging —was perhaps a step
in the desirable direction of punishing
offences against the person as severely
as those against property; but in several
of the other cases the severity of the
sentences moved the prisoners to astonished
protest from the dock; one—who got five
years, with nine weeks’ solitary, for stealing a
few articles of clothing—remarking that a man
would not get any more “ for putting a woman
in a boot-trunk.” His Honor, on the other
hand, displayed some consideration for a rheu-
matism-crippled old man, convicted of horse-
stealing—observing that the man was fitter for
benevolent asylum than eaol ; but nevertheless
inflicting next day—after receiving a medical re-
port on thecase —thetriflingsentence of lOyears !
In answer to another prisoner’s question, the
Judge said that he was wellawarethat“solitary”
meant also bread-and-water. “ But in all my
long experience,” he added, “ I have never
known it do a man in perfect health any harm.”
Which is sweetly consoling to a wretch who has
to undergo nine weeks’ starvation of body and
mind—a week at a time. Also, it is satisfac-
tory to learn that the “ long experience” of a
well-fee’d and well-fed lawyer qualifies his
opinion to outweigh that of medical and scien-
tific experts the world over. Even without the
starvation diet, a single week’s solitary is a
punishment which few men can undergo with-
out more or less permanent damage ; and thus
to prescribe repeated doses of it is to run a
great risk of manufacturing permanent idiots.
There is a crying need for such a systematisa-
tion and regulation of the sentence-administra-
tion plan as shall prevent an atrabilious public
servant from thus venting his gall upon the
wretches delivered into his judicial hands.
But Draco dies hard in Australia. The Trail
of the System is over us still !
The Lamenting Lyne.
When Anti-Australian Lyne was in office, he
was—from the point of Public Principle —as
dodgy a politician as they make. It is, there-
fore, quite the proper political caper that Lyne
should be moved to indignant tears —and a
letter to Sydney Telegraph —by the latest
Federal move of Premier Reid. To appease
the blind wrath of the Anti-Australian little
Sydney party, and, as it were, to take
the rough edge off the clause in the
Federal Enabling Bill which enacts that
the Federal Parliament shall meet tem-
porarily in Melbourne “ until it meets at
the seat of Govt.” in N.S. Wales, Premier
Reid, the other day, pointed out that, in spite
of that clause, the Commonwealth would pro-
bably be administered in N.S. Wales, and
therefore in Sydney, from the very outset.
That is likely enough. That the Federal Par-
liament sits in Melbourne temporarily does not
mean that the departmental work is to be
performed there. As it is to be permanently
done in N.S. Wales, Premier Reid is, probably,
right in thinking that some of it—perhaps a
large part of it—will be done in N.S. Wales
from the beginning, and, therefore, will be
done at Sydney. Details are still so much in
the clouds that even Premier Reid couldn’t
commit himself definitely ; but if convenience
and economy are to be studied, his sugges-
tion is sure to have a good deal of founda-
tion. Even if it has not, the anti-
Australian indignation is just as baseless as the
suggestion. There are two things made certain
by the Bill—N.S. Wales is to have the capital,
and Parliament is to sit in Melbourne while the
capital is being got ready. Beyond that all is
conjecture ; and the Australian conjecture is
just as good as the Anti-Australian, and ap-
parently a little better. Even if the
worst comes, and the Sydney sponge sucks up
no more wealth and population than it does at
present, there may be a consolation in reserve
for the Sydney party. The Governor-General
can live where he pleases ; and, of course, he
will live more or less in Sydney. He would
never think of dwelling all the time among
“ thieves and robbers.”
Left Stinking.
At South Melbourne Court, last week, one
more attempt—probably the last of the series
to make the Law do its duty in the smellful
Dungey-Bromley case was promptly sup-
pressed by a brazen police-magistrate named
Keogh. The appeal this time was for a proper
judicial consideration of the evidence as re-
gards Mrs. Bromley only, all hope of achieving
a verdict against policeman Dungey having
been abandoned by the private prosecution.
The Law, in the persons of magistrate
Panton and Judge Hodges, had made such
a bold, unblushing demonstration in favor
of its minion Dhngey, that the prosecutor
decided to leave him sniggering under the
Law’s wing, and proceed against his female
accomplice, Bromley. The woman, as every-
body knows by now, did shamelessly bait with
her body the “trap” which Dungey planned
for the capture of two alleged abortionists, and,
seeing t hat it was a crime for woman Bromley
to incite a criminal act, she could not possibly
escape a verdict of guilty except through a
gross miscarriage of justice. Keogh, P. M.,
and his attendant honorary magistrates effected
the miscarriage, and Mrs. Bromley, The Bul-
letin supposes, is finally relieved of the trouble-
some consequences of her crime. Keogh,
P.M., refused to administer the Law that
he is paid to administer. The woman’s
legal guilt was laid before him, but he brushed
it aside, as Panton, P.M., had done before
him, and practically spat in the face of Justice
when he said, not that the woman was inno-
cent of the crime charged against her, but that
“ the bench were unanimously of opinion that
the case should be dismissed.” Three men,
thus far, stand convicted by public common
sense of deliberate wrong-doing on behalf of
Dora Bromley —or, rather, on behalf of the
Crown Law Department which sanctioned the
scandalous Dungey-Bromley trap. Panton,
P.M., Judge Hodges, and Keogh, P.M., can
be denounced as a trio of unjust stewards,
and The Bulletin cordially denounces them.
Their intentions may have been honorable: the
immorality of their action is beyond doubt.
They may pretend to find justification for it in
the silence— mostly approving silence—of the
people whose laws they are sworn to administer
without regard to distinction of persons ; but
the fact that all people, Victorian or other-
wise, are mostly fools is none the less regret-
table because Panton, Hodges and Keogh
have thought fit to impose upon their ignor-
ance and stupidity.

Bull’s Paw on N.S.W. Mines.
The most debatable of the provisions which
N.S.W. Chamber of Mines has asked the Minis-
ter to incorporate in his new mining code, pro-
mised first thing next session, is the abolition
of the old-established labor-covenants in leases.
The Chamber, accepting the advice of several
J. Bull visitors, asks that things shall be all
right if the holder spends on land held under
gold-lease “ at least <£lso annually for each five
acres, or fraction of five acres (that is to say,
.£2250 for such area during the first 15 years of
the lease ”), and as to land held under mineral
lease “£5 per acre during the first three years
of the term, and during the remainder of the
term at least £5 per acre per annum.” This in
lieu of the continuous employment of two, three
or four men, as the case may be. Also, “ the
excess expenditure in any one year above the
amount required shall be carried to the credit
of, and go in reduction of, the necessary ex-
penditure for succeeding years of such 15 years’
lease.” Further, it is proposed that holders of
current leases may convert their holdings into
leases with the new conditions; and if any
leaseholder “ can prove an expenditure in
excess of that prescribed by the new conditions
he shall be entitled to be credited with any
such excess.” So mining co’s in a district with
a wage-reduction in view could crowd on men,
and in three months comply with the condi-
tions for three years ; they could then tell the
men to call round when they felt hungry. For
their part, the workmen would have plenty of
time to refresh their memories regarding a
time when many a lessee was unable to carry
out his threat of a lock-out on account of his
covenant to continuously employ a certain
number of workmen. And, anyhow, apart
from all considerations of strikes and lock-outs
and wage-cuts, Labor’s worst affliction', or one
of its worst, is an uneven market; and the con-
ditions which it is proposed to substitute for
the old ones are particularly well calculated to
produce unevenness. For share-market pur-
poses the new idea is most beautiful; but the
stock-jobber is hardly the most important
purpose in the mining industry yet, in spite of
the No-Liability Act and some others.
Sixpenny Kerosene.
Police-magistrate Panton bluntly asserts that
“ the main cause of the prevalence of drunken-
ness (in Victoria) is the want of proper super-
vision over public-houses. There are Govern-
ment inspectors of liquor, but a better class of
men is needed, and the inspection of liquor
should be more persistent.” As a matter of
fact, the Government takes next to no measures
whatever to protect the stomachs of hotel-
drinkers from the slow-poisoning policy of
rapacious licensed victuallers. The Excise
officers who visit hotels at rare intervals to
sample the stock are chiefly concerned in look-
ing after the interests of two or three agents
for certain makes of brandy and schnapps
-—and a whisky or two occasionally. If
the bottles that bear these particular brands of
spirits are found to contain a spurious article,
a prosecution of the pub-keeper will not im-
probably result, on behalf of the firms in-
terested. The Excise officers receive an annual
fee for this service, and are expected to give
their private employers some return for their
money. The Government, seemingly, is quite in-
different as to the wholesomeness, or otherwise,
of hotel drinks in general. At many of the
small, disreputable pubs, there isn’t a bottle of
unpoisoned liquor on the premises, and an un-
wary stranger, walking casually into one of
these licensed hells for a glass of whisky, stands
a chance of losing consciousness right away.
He is lucky if he is able to reel forth into the
street and hang on to the nearest lamp-
post until he feels capable of seeing him-
self home. Certain theatre bars have a
reputation for selling “ whiskies ” that
are criminal libels on the characters of
their alleged brands. In one case, Surgeon
Fitzgerald —being served with a dose of kero-
senic stimulant between the acts—caused the
yellow hair of the Flossie behind the bar to
stand on end, “like quills upon the fretful porcu-
pine,” by threatening to expose the awful
wickedness of the business she was aiding and
abetting. The Government knows about these
scandals. Members of the Government will
stand before a bar and candidly comment on
the atrocious fraudulence of the stuff they are
paying sixpence for, yet the years roll on and
never a legislator rises from his soft seat in
Parliament to propose an honest supervision of
the liquor-traffic. One hears Parliamentary
outcries against patent-medicines, because the
medical faculty, which objects to “ illegiti-
mate ” doctoring, is largely represented in the
’Ouse, but inasmuch as poisonous whisky sends
hundreds of patients to the consulting-room,
the faculty has nothing in particular to say
against that.
An Anti-Federal Bogey.
The most comical anti-Federalist this paper has
met with so far is one “T.M.,” who says he
has discovered something too terrible for words,
and is actually allowed to fill a column of
S.M. Herald over it without editorial remon-
strance :
It being too serious a matter to vote blindfolded a
second time, I determined to examine the Bill section by
section ; and I must confess the more I read it the more
clearly do I see that there are quite a number of its
clauses impracticable, contradictory, and unjust, and
others through which there will not, I think, be much
difficulty in driving the proverbial coach and horses.
Everyone seems to suppose that as South Australia has
female suffrage, only half the number of votes recorded
there will be counted in an election there for members of
the Federal Parliament, until, if it should so unfortunately
happen, all the other colonies have a similar disad vantage;
bur. on a close examination of the Bill I find that t his is
by no means so, for immediately the Commonwealth
Bill becomes law the full number of votes cast there,
male and female, will have to be reckoned. South
Australia, therefore, will have double the votingstrength
of any other State, which other sections in the Bill prove
beyond question. It is only when, if at any time it may
be proposed in the Federal Parliament to alter the Con-
stitution of the Commonwealth in other matters, that
only half her total number of votes will count (see section
127 and its heading). Another (section 30) says: “The
qualification of electors of members of the House of Re-
presentatives shall be in each State that which is pre-
scribed by the law of the State ” ; and as by the law of
South Australia females arc entitled to vote, they cannot
be deprived of it, or the suffrage there in any way
restricted. Not only so, but section 41 gives this’ right
in the most absolute form and for ever, for it reads :
“No adult person who has a right to vote shall be pre-
vented by any law of the Commonwealth from voting at
elections for either House.’’ So that it actually becomes
part of the contract with South Australia that no law,
even of the Commonwealth, may ever halve the count
there, except in an election solely for the purpose of
altering the Constitution ; in that case, and in that alone,
will only half the total votes count.
Only by inexcusable negligence could such sections as
these have got inserted in the Bill, and the most chari-
table construction I can put upon this neglect of duty by
our Federal Ten is, that they must have been dozing to
have allowed South Australia to have stolen such amarch
upon them by the insertion of so monstrously unfair an
exception.
According to the Commonwealth Bill the
number of representatives for each province is
to be fixed according to population, so S.A. will
get just the same number of representatives in
the Federal Parliament whether it has male
suffrage, or male and female suffrage, or dog
suffrage, or no suffrage at all. And when a
referendum is on, so that the vote of all
Australia has to be taken, it is expressly stipu-
lated by clause 127 that the woman-vote of S.A.
won’t count unless all the States have a woman-
vote also. But what harasses the anguished
“T.M.”is that when S.A. is electing its own
members —the same seven Representatives and
six Senators that it would have in any case—it
will, by reason of the woman suffrage, elect
them by twice as large a vote as it otherwise
would. And a Federal member from S.A. will
represent 106,000 voters (men and women),
while a Federal member from N.S.W. will re-
present only 53,000 (men only). And the
frantic anti-Billite who fills a column in the
Herald thinks that somehow the one member
returned by 53,000 men and 53,000 women will
have twice as much voting power in the Com-
monwealth Parliament as the one member re-
turned by 53,000 men only. “ T.R.” examined
the Bill “ section by section ” to dig out this
strange lunacy. Truly, Australia is mostly
governed by One-Fool-One-Vote.
On a Poetic Isle.
The beautiful poetic theory about the evils of
city life and the good results to be expected
from a simple natural existence face to face
with Nature, receives a bad knock from a
recent official report on the state of Pitcairn
Island. The Pitcairn Islanders lead the simple,
natural existence in all its beauty. There is
no dusty city thereabouts ; no big red-brick
factories, no looms, no ’buses or tram-cars, no
beer, policemen, theatres, dog-fights or boxing-
saloons, no ribald push rolling home on
Saturday nights, no tea, coffee, horse-races,
welshers, totes, tarts, Flossies, oyster-suppers
—no nothing. It is practically the simplicity
of the early chapters of Genesis over again—
plenty of food, a fair green isle, a fair blue sea,
pure air, no worry, and the stillness of an
eternal Sabbath in the air. The restfulness
and peace which eat into your bones are all
there. And Captain Dyke, of H.M. ship
Comus, reports that this happy, primitive com-
munity is fast qualifying for an idiot-asylum.
There have been three deaths in eighteen
months, and two of them were murders—
two murders in eighteen months among 149
people is a rather steep percentage. The
people have no literature, and refuse to accept
any. “ They do absolutely nothing all day ”
save loaf and look with a cow-like eye at the
blue and green immensity of things. The
island swarms with cocks and hens, and the
inhabitants live on them and fish and vege-
tables, and the battle of existence is practically
non-existent; anybody who can run fast enough
to overtake an egg can make some kind of
living at Pitcairn. The look in the faces of all
the grown-up inhabitants is one of tired
vacancy ; they are weak in intellect, with no
conventional morals worth speaking of, and
the general tendency seems to be a drift
towards unanimous inbecility. Questions have
to be put even to the more intelligent of the
adults in the simplest language, and in a
slow, impressive fashion to evoke an answer,
and even then the questions have to be
about fish, or hens, or vegetables, or some other
easily-understandable subject. The children,
going a step further than their elders, appear
to be developing a gibberish language of their
own. Through some curious process of evolu-
tion or decadence nearly all the women shed
their front teeth at an early age. The only ex-
citement is a slow, long-enduring and seem-
ingly harmless feud between the two principal
families —otherwise they are only a bow'ed and
gradual community much given to prayer. If
the world would agree to leave Pitcairn abso-
lutely alone for a century, and then a deputa-
tion of scientists would call there to see how the
community had mooned along in the interval,
it would be an interesting experiment in evolu-
tion.
Dear Bulletin. —The beautiful working of
N.S.W. Health Regulations! In a country
town recently scarlet fever was epidemic, and
as soon as a fresh case was reported to the
police the latter served upon the head teacher
of the public school a notice requiring that
none of the children of that family be allowed
to attend school until another notice should
have been received, advising that the house,
clothing, &e., had been properly disinfected.
But the children thus kept from school never-
theless freely mingled in their pastimes with
the children it was intended to protect, and
also attended with them the different Sunday-
schools ; and, on attention of police being
directed to latter fact, they sorrowfully ob-
served that “a Sunday-school is not a school
within the meaning of the Act.” They could
do nothing about the Sunday-school, and neither
the parents nor sin-shifter w'ould do anything.
—T.J.
Telegraph talks (April 12) of “the
Anti-Hill party, the party that has set itself
resolutely against having thisparticular measure
rammed down the throat of the country.” Said
“ramming down” consists in offering the
J ill to the country to take or leave, just as it
chooses, and just as every other Bill, policy,
Ministry, or candidate whatever is offered to
the country. The electors vote on the whole
Hill just as they vote on the whole John
tUYNEs. Every individual elector can’t tinker
the Bill to his own notions any more than
every individual elector of Wellington can tin-
ker John Haynes. Plenty Wellington electors
may object that John Haynes’s manly figure is
too Japanese-junk-like and wide in the beam
tor Grecian beauty, but they’re not allowed to
hack nine inches off his waist and vote for the
rest of him—John Haynes is “ rammed down
their throats” all standing. “You’re ill,”
say the Federalists to the country; “ here's
your medicine : take it or leave it, but please
yourself.” And “You’re ill,” say the Anti-
Billites; “ but we’ll take jolly good care you
don t get the offer of medicine ! We love you so
much that we’ll smash the bottle beforehand.”

At Beaufort (Vic.) the local schoolmaster was
recently charged by a small girl-scholar with
having indecently assaulted, her and another
little girl in the school, before the whole class.
Other witnesses, however, deposed that the
12-year-old prosecutrix was an habitual liar,
besides being very dull and stupid, and some of
her class-mates testified positively that there
had been no such conduct as described. Also
the sweet innocent herself, in cross-examina-
tion, admitted that she would say anything to
do the teacher harm, as she didn’t like him !
And, finally, the other girl utterly denied that
she had been treated with any indecency,
whereupon accused was discharged without
being called upon for any defence. The unfor-
tunate schoolmaster will probably continue to
teach precocious and prurient girls as long as
his luck lasts ; when that fails he will probably
go to gaol on some other such trumped-up
charge. The Indecent Small Female is a very
serious menace to mankind in Australia.

Dear Bulletin.—ln offering a prize of j 67 10s.
last year for the best exhibit of dried fruit
N.S.W. Govt, stipulated that there should be
ten distinct varieties, each to represent half-a-
ton. To do this it would have been necessary
to treat about 40 tons of green fruit, at a cost
of £l5O, besides another £5O to £7O for subse-
quent treatment, and as no one in his senses
would attempt to fulfil such conditions, there
was no entry ; with result that this year there
is no prize offered. Contrast this with Govt,
treatment of the dairying industry, which year
after year has a £5O prize awarded for an
exhibit that costs about £5, and with no stipu-
lation about any stock of same quality behind.
This, too, in spite of the fact that, as there is
no import trade in butter to be competed with,
the industry is well established and able to
take care of itself without Govt, assistance.
With Federation pending, this should be a real
live question with any chap that wants to
“ save the country ” anywhere off the stump.—
J. Sutton.
Concerning N.S.W. Postmaster-General Var-
ney Parkes’ new parcels-insurance system :
Ed. Bulletin.—P.M.G. Parkes has solaced himself for
his defeat by the holy how lers over the Sunday work-
question by the introduction of a parcels-insurance
system, regulated by a number of cumbrous and clumsily-
drawn rules printed on the back of the “ Certificate of
Insurance.” Now, as, under the N.S.W. system of treat-
ing parcels in the same way as registered letters, the
loss of the former is almost unknown, this new' idea of
extorting revenue is both ridiculous and unfair. Of the
sapience of the rules let No. 13 serve as an example.
This provides that “no compensation shall be given
where the loss arises from flood, tempest, shipwreck,
earthquake, war, or other causes beyond control.”
Which simply means that the “compulsory insurance”
protects sender only against the dishonesty of postal
officials. Another item : “ A fee of 6d. will be collected
on every uninsured parcel received from the U.K. which
is found to contain jewellery of any description.”
That is after the parcel has safely made its long
journey. P.M.G. Parkes actually fines the re-
ceiver 6d. because the sender did not insure it against
loss by dishonest postal officials. Another rule sets forth
that “ over-insurance is a bar to compensation ” ; so that
for a postmaster to get a fair estimate of the value of,
say, a parcel of lady’s underwear which sender wishes to
insure for £lO but which he, the postmaster, considers is
but worth £5, he must separately examine and appraise
every article in the parcel. At this rate, the services
of an expert valuator will be needed in each P.O.
Also, many persons who insure their parcels will
find that on receipt at other end the most valuable portion
of the contents has vanished, and, as only a personal
examination of the parcel and a checking of the invoice
by the receiving postmaster can block mysterious disap-
pearance of valuables which were never enclosed, this
precious insurance system opens up a particularly invit-
ing door to dishonesty.—Bargo.
The N.S.W. anti-Federal platform:-
1. That the Federal capital shall be Sydney.
2. That all contracts for Federal buildings, &c., shall be
carried out by Sydney firms.
3. That all Federal positions shall be filled by Sydneyites,
and no other than a Sydneyite shall carry on any business
within the Federal confines.
4. That N.S. Wales i.e., Sydney shall have a repre-
sentation in the Federal Parliament equal to that of all
the other provinces combined.
5. That any Sydneyite who doesn’t like w'ork shall be
comfortably supported out of the Federal revenue.
6. That N.S. Wales shall be allowed to divert or lock the
Australian rivers wherever she pleases, and throw the key
overboard if she desires.
7. That the Braddon clause shall be removed, and a fresh
scheme of taxation, under which N.S. Wales shall have
the right to contribute as much or as little as she pleases,
shall be forthwith drafted by Damchieago Dibbs and John
Haynes.
8. That, if these resolutions be not at once accepted by
the other small and inconsiderable provinces, then the
landlords of Sydney and their claqueurs pledge themselves
to remove, with All their goods and chattels, from Aus-
tralia, which shall for ever after be accursed.
Maoriland’s extended municipal franchise
system comes into operation after. April 14.
There are to be three qualifications for enrol-
ment, as against one previously—ratepayers’,
freeholders’, and residential occupants’. The
freeholder must possess land of a capital value
of ,£25, but he can’t exercise a ratepayer’s and
a freeholder’s vote too. The residential occu-
pant must be a three-months resident of the
borough, and pay a rental of at least £lO
per year for private house, warehouse, office or
shop. If a husband is entitled to a vote, his
wife may cast one on the same qualification,
and vice versa. But only ratepayers and free-
holders can vote on questions of borrowing.
This broadened franchise will probably revolu-
tionise civic government locally, and it badly
needed readjustment.

The present production of “Othello” is
so good that it ought to stand for another fort-
night. Rignold has seldom been seen in a finer
character than that of the Moor of Venice. His
conception of the part is on all-fours with Cole-
ridge’s, who insists that mere, vulgar jealousy was
not the point in Othello’s passion. It was rather
an agony that the creature, whom he had believed
angelic, with whom he had garnered up his heart,
should be proved impure and worthless. It was,
says S.T.C., the struggle not to love her. It was
a moral indignation and regret that virtue
should so fall, combined with a feeling of out-
raged honor, tfiat drove him to Desdemona’s slay-
ing and his own suicide. “ There is no ferocity
in Othello ; his mind is majestic and composed.”
By the way, Rignold over-stains his Moorish
countenance. Coleridge considers that Othello
must not be conceived as a negro; but a high and
chivalrous Moorish chief. The Moors were ori-
ginally Arabs, a Semitic race ; and Rignold’s
almost-woolly wig is incorrect. Besides, why does
he stab, after smothering, Desdemona ? The text
gives no warranty for this. Rignold should never
play melodrama ; Diver should never play any-
thing else. The lago of the latter is foppish and
jerky, and his villainy too obvious—a bandbox
Mephistopheles—despite its facile cleverness. His
finesse would not deceive anybody. lago was a
large man of imposing presence, and a bluff, hearty
manner; a soldier, all apparent frankness and
easy good nature. lago is a comedy part, Tither-
adge would play it almost ideally.
* • * •
Theatre-goers who know their Shakespeare,
grumble about excisions, forgetting that without
them it would be impossible to stage a single
Shakespearian play. Whoever produces Eliza-
bethan drama must edit largely. Rignold may,
perhaps, be blamed somewhat for the trend of the
editing that carves at the other characters rather
than his own. But what is the use of being a
potentate if you cannot do what you like ? "The
Sydney press doesn’t rapturise over Rignold as it
did over Wilson Barrett, but ■ artistically Barrett
isn’t in the same histrionic street with the local
man. One thing, moreover, it doesn’t waste
commendation upon the splendid scenery, to wit,
from the brush of artist Phil Goatcher.
**■*■«■
When Miln was playing some ten years
ago at Melbourne Opera House “Othello” was
produced with a novel attraction. One night
“G.C.M.” played the title-rdle, and Dam pier
played lago. Next night Miln played lago, and
Dampier the Moor. The scheme worked splen-
didly, and ensured double bookings by everybody
interested in either actor. The crowd rolled up
eagerly to see if “ the other fellow ” was able to
down its own man in one or the other part. Of
course, Diver as Othello is not an alluring propo-
sition.
** * *
Napoleon Bonaparte is still impersonating
Robert Brough at the Sydney Royal, and shirking
the responsibility of paying his antediluvian laun-
dry-account. ‘ ‘ Madame Sans-Gene ” ought about
to sound the knell of the First Empire boom.
When the dramatist worries a hero into the
depths of a clothes-basket, and clear-starches his
frayed linen to the fitful moaningsof the clarionet,
it is about time to fold up the Sunday shirt of his
glory and put it away for gala use in the historic
locker. No emperor is a hero to his blanchisseuse.
Talking about clothes-baskets, the threatened pro-
duction of “ The Merry Wives of Windsor, ” at the
Criterion, will raise memories of the great Brough-
aparte, for there is no end of dirty linen and
washerwomen repartee in the Shakespearian
comedy. Anyhow, Madame Sans-Gene takes a
well-earned rest from the labors of the washtub
and the malapropisms of a laundry-built Duchess,
on Friday, and the 22nd will witness the produc-
tion at the Royal of “ Lord and Lady Algy.” So
far, the Brough season in Sydney looks as if it
were going to hum. The houses have been so
good that Brough considers his opening fortnight
has brought along the record two weeks’ business
of his managerial career.
*# * *
The Tivoli has burgeoned forth with a new
relay of artists. Perhaps the foremost of the
fresh-comers is Mr. Manuel Wilson, a contor-
tionist with novelties of muscle torture. His
characteristic is speed, the eye being unable to
follow the rapidity of his movements with any
degree of certitude. His turn is performed on a
white cloth, which gives it a cameo-like aspect.
The three Keziahs are ladder-trick artistes, neat
and clean in their performance, though perhaps
not very novel. Then there are Miss Edith
Conley and Geo. W. Carew, serio-comic singers,
sketeh artists and dancers. They are good of
their kind, and fairly replace Bellman and Moore,
who introduced to Sydney the refined musical
sketch, sans high-kicking and knock-about vul-
garities.
* * *- *
The Woman of Destiny has continued to
wave her knife wildly in the limelight at the
Lyceum. But the days of her eloquent madness
are numbered. The Union Jack and the reaping-
machine and the red sunsets and the blue seas and
the realistic steamer and all the pomp and circum-
stance of the glorious British Empire retire to
make room for “Man’s Enemy” which, we sup-
pose, is a euphemistic method of nominating the
“cursed drink”; and which, no doubt, will be
somewhat similar to “ Our Guardian Angel ”
only more so.
£j£
“Round the World in Eighty Days”
ceased from its cyclonic sprinting on the 15th
inst., and the Sydney Opera House will re-open
on the 29th with musical comedies. By the way,
the owners of the earth-circuit piece had better
hustle round and get as much as possible out of it
while the title is decently modern in its applica-
tion.- With the invention of steamers that put
up records of 40 miles an hour already on the
bounding main, the day is not far distant when
the achievement of Jules Verne’s hero will be a
very small affair. The present century is doing
all it knows how to beat Puck’s fastest—4o
minutes.

Fitzgerald’s Circus is still profitably in the
same place, and a great joy fills the heart of the
“ Zoologiseher ” gardens ; likewise the tiger has
not yet gastronomised his steed, and the elephant
and the lion lie down with the bicycle ; also, a
large peace broods over the only bunyip in cap-
tivity, and the total menagerie has not yet shown
any sign of tuberculosis, or any other form of
measles known to medical men.

“The Geisha” retires on Friday from Her
Majesty’s. From first to last the opera has drawn
this city in large quantities. On Saturday the
Firm put up “ lolanthe ” for six nights only. The
opera season will then close, and the Knight-
Ferrar co. will come in on its heels.
