** * *
“ The Belle of New York ” gets along
better than was expected at Melb. Princess’s.
At last week’s matinee Albert Whelan took the
principal-man part, wherein poor Oscar Girard,
the one visible humorist of the imported crowd,
appears to be playing himself to a standstill.
Whelan was a satisfactory substitute for the sick
man, and his appearance in place of somebody else
would amount to a decided change for the better.
Meanwhile, the lamentable new arrival, who
figures as Mugg, is enjoying robust health, also the
impersonator of the Polite Lunatic steadfastly
refrains from falling ill, and the exponent of
mixed-ale pugilism goes on unchecked. The irony
of fate is very noticeable in this matter of “The
Belle of New York.” An accident, “fortunately
unattended by serious consequences,” might as
well happen to give the Automatic Twins leave of
absence from the bill, but it doesn’t. The unpre-
cedented mummers in question are still there.
Likewise the somewhat Napoleonic stranger who
struggles with the character of an interesting
young prodigal, and the unsesthetic “Queen of
Comic Opera,” and the female contortionist in
petticoats. They are all there, as before. The
present writer drops into the Princess’s occasion-
ally to approve of Miss Hepner and Girard, but
when he finds the others all there he stifles a sob
and leaves early)
** * #
“Woman and Wine” has not yet slack-
ened its prosperity at Melb. Royal. The title
turns out to be a powerful attraction, and the
drama’s honest endeavor to shock the public is
much appreciated. Very few dramas fulfil the
promises of their headlines, but this is all that the
title suggests, or even more so ; in fact, Bland
Holt would have been justified in billing it as
“Sin and Wickedness,” not to say “Booze and
Bloodshed. ” Hence the large attendances. An
ornate feature of this tale of dissipation is the
plump tenor, who prowls gingerly around
a cafe with a knife in his costume, and
mentions that the weapon has slain several
faithless women in its time. Maddened by
Miss Watson and drink, Walter Baker buys
this fatal toy from the plump vocalist, which ex-
plains why Walter is arrested on suspicion when
his best girl discovers Miss W.’s corpse under a
heap of roses. Bland Holt’s dramas are usually
rich in moral lessons, but this is the first drama
to point out the foolishness of buying an instru-
ment of vengeance in a public restaurant.
** * *
It now appears from a letter written by
Allan and Co. to Melb. Age that Amy Castles
didn’t get half the profits of her cleverly-worked
boom at the Town Hall, but she was handsomely
treated nevertheless. “After the success of the
concerts Mrs. Doubleday informed Miss Castles
that she would receive a cheque for 75 guineas,
and this, with outlays made on Miss Castle’s be-
half, is equivalent to her receiving fully £lOO for
the two concerts.” Any number of girl-warblers
would like to be “ discovered ” on the same terms,
of course, and lots of enterprising Mrs. Double-
days are pn the look-out for another “ finest voice
in the world.” Consequently there will be some
sore disappointments presently. The musical
critics have rashly committed themselves to the
glorification of Amy Castles, and if another Amy,
with an equally round voice, and a much more
promising physique, were to be exploited next
month, she would find the public grown indifferent
and the press notices rather chilly.
The current agony at the Haleck consists
largely of Augustus Glover in the felon’s dock—a
small dock, with only room, enough for one in it.
oome of the witnesses incline towards Augustus,
but others are dead against him, and you get
warning of these from the look of despair in the
prisoner s eyes, arms and body, as he beholds them
entering the witness-box. Augustus escapes the
gallows at the finish. The beasts who bore false
witness are shoved into the dock instead of the
thunderous innocent aforesaid, and the 12 respect-
able jurymen find ’em guilty inside of 11 p.m.
Ihe change of bill for next Saturday was not
announced at time of writing, but it will doubt-
less happen with the requisite Haleckian awful-
ness.
** * *
Dante, late of Sydney, introduced himself
to Melbourne on Saturday at St. George’s Hall,
where the light wasn’t as good as it will be made
this week. When the new light is thrown on
Dante s pretty little show, the fame of that curly-
headed artist will spread rapidly in Melbourne, no
doubt, for in a hard, professional way of speaking,
he is All Right. Dante is an entertainer in the
first place, and a distinctly excellent conjuror
afterwards. The magic of his smile goes a long
way, also his legs are rather lovely, and he
is a gay deceiver in all respects. Whilst
Dante makes a favorable impression on the girls,
his assistant goddess, Mdlle. Edmunda, casts a
soothing spell over other fellows in the audience.
Hie show starts well, goes well, and ends well. It
is neatly put upon the stage, and it deserves a
more festive home than St. George’s Hall, but
seeing that the Palace Theatre couldn’t be carted
across from Sydney for the purpose, St. George’s
Hall will have to do for Dante, and the public
must take this fresh young wizard where they can
find him.
** * *
Much-travelled Pianist Friedenthal gave
his first recital on Sat., April 15, before an audi-
ence which comfortably filled the Town Hall.
The gathering was thickly salted with local musi-
cians of all cliques—Zelman, senior, Father Ryan,
Herr Hartung, Mrs. Palmer, Mona M‘Burney,
&c., &c. Hero-worshipping damsels, interested
m the man rather than the musician, will be more
numerous next time. The “expert” tone of his
reception must have gratified an artist who ignores
the gallery, so to speak, dispenses with sensational
mannerisms, and, in his own words, wants
nothing of his audience “ except that they love
music.” Nevertheless, judging by the well-
groomed and much-cherished aspect of Mein
Herr’s dark tresses, he possibly won’t be com-
jiletely out of sympathy with those hearers (not a
few probably) who oyer and above said modest
sine qua non, set their superfluous affection on
Music s interpreter, in the person of slim, dark,
sympathetic Albert Friedenthal. A more mature
and self-sustained artist than Mark Hambourg,
our present visitor, unlike the Polish boy afore-
said, does not frenzy excitable listeners by ebulli-
tions of youthful impetuosity, flame, sound and
fury.
» * » *
Friedenthal - fire, like forked electric-
flashes, perturbs some natures less than Ham-
bourg sheet-lightning with thunder-claps and a
brimstone whiff. Serenade (Sohubert-Liszt) was,
maybe, the gem of Saturday’s programme. Frie-
denthal’s own little Gavotte got an encore, though
frowned upon by the cognoscenti as a sop to the
Philistines. Said skittish composition is destined
to go the round of the local drawing-rooms with
its freakish finger-gymnastics. Performer trills
simultaneously with the nails of the thumb and
second finger and en route strikes notes in the
ordinary way with the forefinger. Second Frie-
denthal concert, billed for Tuesday, April 18, will
have happened when this paper is printed.
At Poverty Point.
“ The ‘ long arm of the Law ’ is nothing,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ to the lengthy arm
of Love. George Bull, formerly of the Sydney press,
marries Madge Rossell, the popular danseuse, in Septem-
ber. Miss Rossell was in Australia with the last Gaiety
Co., will be remembered for her dancing as Flo Fan-
shavve in ‘ In Town,’ and met her future guardian, who
was‘copy-jerking’for the Australian Star. But the
track of veracious affection never helps the running for
the Matrimonial Stakes. They parted ‘out thar,’ en-
gaged and met ‘ over he ar,’ disengaged—a dark dividing
cloud, some years wide, of misunderstanding expanded
by the haughty pride But now—Bow-bells, orange-
blossoms, horseshoes, curtain.”
“The new Alhainbrkballet, ‘The Red Shoes,”’ writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “is the finest
that has been produced in England in the memory of
young-man. The ‘ parched-pea ’ ballerina with limbs like
a champion rooster-bikist has been warned off to rest the
corns on her toe-tips, and the tasty, harmonious dresses
almost excuse what they do not disclose. The final
scene, representing ‘The Firmament’—whatever that is
—is, lam ready to believe, the most striking, artistic,
and elaborate display ever made. The ’99 team will rush
this ballet and the bicycle-polo at the Empire. Exciting
and clever, the two-a-side teams battle nightly on their
merits, and betting is an institution in the grand-circle.
“ Ven.”: Remember, when a youngster, staying at the
Cornwall Hotel, Launceston. The divisions between the
rooms in that ancient hostlery were so thin that you
could hear every word spoken by your neighbors. And I
heard —much, I recollect, to the prompting of my pity
and sympathy—a solemn voice say, “This half-crown,
my dear, is all we have in the world.” I wonder if George
Coppin remembers 1
“ The new ‘ Savoy ’ opera,” writes The Bulletin’s Lon-
don correspondent, “ is laid—hang on to the car going
round corners!—in a mining camp in Australia! A

“ The latest sensation in theatrical circles,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ is the managerial
triumvirate of the Georges. George Edwardes, George
Lederer, and George Musgrove, of the Gaiety, Casino
(N.Y.)andShaftesbury, an Anglo-Yanko-Austral shamrock
with a very solid stalk. The triplicate have taken the
Prince of Wales Theatre, Arthur Roberts’s late home, and
will open shortly with ‘ Revue, 1899,’ an American form
of entertainment. The events of the day, or the past 12
months, are reproduced in burlesque. Politicians, soldiers,
professionals generally of note, represented in doing
something they have done or ought to. Each character
will be copied to the life, and full face masks worn accur-
ately moulded. It will be really a superior topical panto-
mime, and contretemps of the Randy-Pandy type may
intervene.”
Oscar Girard, the long, thin comedian of the “ Belle of
New York,” seems to have come to Australia in pursuit
of health, poor fellow ! His quaint, effective method of
talking his songs was forced upon him by his failing voice.
Melb. Age’s musical critic committed himself to the
statement that Amy Castles is “ not yet fully grown.”
Which accounted perhaps for a letter in a subsequent
issue of the Age commencing with “ In view of the great
sensation caused by the wonderful child vocalist .” The
only sensation experienced by outsiders, who paid their
“bob” or two shillings in a spirit of enquiry, was a feel-
ing of mild disappointment.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent : —“ Geo. Mus-
Strove tells that New York Belles and London Beaux may
come or go, but the ‘Belle of New York’ goes on for ever,
and in the tenth month to the tune of over £2OOO a week.
The Shaftesbury theatre is now like nothing but the old
days of the Gaiety, when the ‘sacred lamp of burlesque’
took a ‘bunker’ of champagne and an ‘eight o’clock
rush’ of suppers to feed the giddy wick. It has been a
marvellous performance of G. M.’s to lift the curse off
that theatre. It generally takes a good fire and a subse-
quently-solvent proprietor. But the Amurkan girls have
fetched and held the whole of the Johnnieocracy. With
eye-tooth wisdom the ‘ tired ’ ladies are transported back
to New York, and fresh paint, blood, and limbs of the
curviest replace them. As a bookie, I’d lay odds the
‘ Belle of New York’ will be here to push M'llwraith’s
Maori off London Bridge—or the Q.N. Bank.”
“For She is the Belle of New York ” now puts the nose
out of joint of “Sons of the Sea ” as the current whistling
and la-de-dah nuisance of Melb. What is the occult in-
fluence that makes a melody “ touch the electric chain
with which we’re darkly bound ” ? It rumbles in the
railway wheels, and those of the trams, puffs with the
smoke from the locomotive funnel, is heard in the very
rollers of the sea upon the beach, and you can’t tinkle
your knife against your plate without producing it. Even
the electric telegraph raps it out. Just as everyone is
going frantic with tune in the head, worse than the hum-
buzz of the influenza, there comes a refreshing change,
and the “ Belle of New York” is gone.
The toss of a penny, when he was a hobbledehoy in
London, decided Coppin on going to Australia, instead of
to America. He landed first in Sydney. Soon he found
his way to Hobart, as comedian and manager. From
Launceston he brought his company, including Rogers
and Mr. and Mrs. Ohas. Young, to Melb. Queen’s. He
had the first carpet laid on the Melb. stage—for a per-
formance of the “ School for Scandal,” himself as Sir
Peter. The Queen’s and the pub. next door were owned
by John Thomas Smith, Melb.’s Whittington, seven times
Mayor. Coppin concluded his engagement with Brooke
behind the scenes of the Birmingham theatre, in the
time when Mrs. Rignold, the manager’s wife (mother of
George and William), was so popular in Hamlet and
Romeo—but it cannot be verified that she played lago,
as has been alleged. The news of Brooke’s death reached
Coppin while he was chatting in Caple’s book-shop,
Colli ris-street.
“ Bun.”: Veteran Geo. Coppin, who recently arrived at
the 80th milestone of his journey, gave an Argus reporter
a brief sketch of his career. In this he mentioned that
Rogers, old-time comedian, and father of Horace Rogers
of the S.M. Herald, was the son of a clergyman and had
never performed on the stage until he came to Australia.
This is correct. Rogers was a soldier in the 51st Regt.,
when stationed at Hobart. His brilliant acting in
garrison amateur performances led to the purchase of his
discharge by some admirers. He immediately took a
leading position ori the Australian stage. That was before
Coppin’s time.
Apropos veteran George Coppin’s 80th birthday, Satur-
day, April 8. So long ago as 1848 he built a theatre in
Port Adelaide (S.A.), where he was most popular as a
comedian and hotelkeeper 50 years ago. His first wife is
buried in Adelaide cemetery.

“0.G." : “ Round the World in 80 Days” has a geo-
graphical twang about its title which would be un-
pleasantly reminiscent of one’s school-days if the manage-
ment did not thoughtfully obviate this by making the
details as inaccurate as possible. Thus we have a Hindu
princess in Turkish costume, and a pale-faced Hindu priest
who looks like a Druid in an Eight Hours’ Day procession.
Likewise we have a barmaid in America, where the he-
drink-slinger is universal ; also, in the same land of the
Stars and Stripes, a barrel-bodied Frenchman prances
around declaring himself a “ man of de bull-dog breed
dat made old England great.”

Westralian wire : “ Copper is attracting a good
deal of attention in the Kalsroorlie district. Application
has been made for a reward claim. The Warden will
inspect the find to-morrow.” It is much easier to find a
copper show than to float one —in fact, writer is well-ad-
vised from London that copper mines are only floated
there once in about 14 years, and no sooner did the metal
run up to £7O than London was infested with mines from
the uttermost ends of the earth. Up to date few have
been taken, and many left—perhaps to come up again 14
years hence.

The Kallenite Explosives Company (capital
£160,000 in £1 shares) has been formed to acquire process,
specifications, and patent rights of the series of explosives
known as “ Kallenite,” and to manufacture and sell the
same in N.S.W. and elsewhere. The explosive Kallenite
was discovered by Messrs. Alex. Fraser and John Calla-
ghan, by whom the invention was matured and perfected
with the advice and co-operation of Major Quinby, of
San Francisco, a well-known explosives expert. It is
claimed for Kallenite that, while practically smokeless
and fumeless, it is stronger and cheaper than any other
nitro-glycerine compound, becomes safer, instead of more
dangerous, with keeping, and will probably be in Govt,
requisition for defence purposes. Offices, Imperial Cham-
bers, 16 O’Gonnell-street, Sydney.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Dredging schemes by the score are in process of
incubation in Sydney. The same old tribe that bobs up
serenely in every boom is at the game, but this time they'
will reckon without their host. The public are not biting
freely at the tempting bait dangled before them by certain
prints. The man who puts his money into any dredging
scheme without subjecting it to the closest investigation
and getting independent opinion as to its merits deserves
—to be fleeced. There may be difficulties as to titles and
riparian rights, and heavy royalties to pay, a M.L. syndicate
having applied for patents covering bucket-dredges—that
is, they are doing in N.S.W. what they would not dream
of attempting to do in their own country.
* * * »
Queensland and South Australia have shown no
sign of joining in the dredging-boom, but they may be
depended on to come along later. For Westralia to start
would be funny enough to awaken a general sense of
humor, and that always nips a boom in the bud when
floating is in the air. Over two years back the writer,
after putting in a week examining John Wallace’s dredges
at Yackandandah, Vic., anticipated a dredging boom
someday. It has come, and with it the time for antici-
pating its “ bust.” Two things are essential in dredging
—a little gold in the stuff, and conditions favorable for its
cheap extraction. In many of the schemes, incubated
and incubating, there is enough gold, but not enough
water; in others, plenty of water, but too little gold.
There are two systems—the bucket, and the centrifugal-
pump dredge. One works in water, the other beside it.
In some of the schemes it is apparent, to anyone who
understands the business, the natural conditions are such
that neither can be effectively or profitably used. Gener-
ally the idea is to dredge the public for cash. One man
wants £6OOO to erect a dredge and half the property, and
is himself quite satisfied that the wash will go as many
dwf. “to the load” as grains are wanted to pay.
Another man, representing an engineering firm, is
also satisfied that the ground is suitable for
a dredge, and naturally supplies an estimate of the cost
of one. Old alluvial co’s ground is to be operated by new
dredging co’s, for a heavy consideration in paid shares—
and the privilege of taking over old liabilities is thrown
in. The success of Garland and other N.S.W. people,
who have never yet had a clean-up, is anticipated and
grossly exaggerated ; one Vic. co. has been six weeks at
this little operation (cleaning-up)—no joke where the
bottom is bad. Men who have never seen a dredge work-
ing in their lives have become dredge-experts ; and, in
one notable case, it is as clear as the noonday sun that
the daily publication of fictitious sales at fancy prices is
resorted to with the view of drawing the public in and
providing something for the Stock Exchange’s attention.
Maoriland’s dredging success is quoted every day, but not
a word that the united capital of all the co’s working
there would not make up half the capital of the co’s pro-
jected here—that the conditions there are generally
favorable—there are no dredges working where they are
not—and that the returns don’t average 40oz. each dredge
a week, anyhow, and, no matter how cheaply dredging
things can be run, 40oz. a week won’t pay divs. on a
heavy capital.
* * * *
In favor of one of the N.S.W. dredging co.’s
now on the market, at least one thing can be said. One
of the reporting experts admits that the gold starts a
little below the “ grass roots.” In every other prospectus
writer has seen the gold start right “from the grass
roots so mixed up together, in fact, that he expects
soon to see a scheme for extracting large quantities of
gold from the roots themselves.
• * • *
After all the yabber of the great good—and no
possible harm—dredging was going to do Vic., one Lynch
is out with a terrible indictment against it. He cites in-
stances of rich river flats ruined by silt and sludge, of
farms abandoned, of deep, continuous waters turned into
a chain of lagoons, and from the clearness of crystal to the
color of pea-soup ; of streams gradually silting up with
many other horrors, and is amazed at the blindness of
the Vic. farmers to the great and irreparable dangers of
the dredging boom.
* » * *
A fair-sized library might be stocked with
learned monographs and papers on the Broken Hill Sul-
phide Problem ; and tbe most substantial shelf of all
should be given up to the Ashcroft process. Mr. Ash-
croft himself lately sent out a couple of massive pam-
phlets, in one of which he reviewed the whole history of
his connection with the meteoric business. Much of the
150 pp. or so consists of correspondence. It began with
gushful notes to “Dear Ashcroft” from “Kintore;” it
ended with letters to “ E. A. Ashcroft, Esq., Sir,” in
which “ J. Butterworth, secretary,” intimated that if
Ashcroft wanted law the directors were ready for him.
This massive volume was thrown at shareholders of the
Sulphide Corporation at their last annual meeting in Lon-
don ; the meeting replied by knocking the thrower’s
name out of the co’s title. Ashcroft summarises his case
thus: The ore contents of the co’s mine (the Central)
weren’t so good as the co’s own mining experts had re-
ported ; he (Ashcroft) had never had proper control and,
therefore, a fair show'; and the final trial-run was ter-
minated while the process was working successfully. Still,
in his final words, he confesses that “the low grade of the
ores at Broken Hill practically put wet processes,
such as Ashcroft’s, out of court.” And this is, ap-
parently, the end of the most expensive metallurgical
experiment which Australia has known. In one way and
another it cost a trifle over £lOO,OOO.
Ashcroft was electrical engineer to the B.H.
Proprietary Co. at a very comfortable salary. In an evil
hour he would be an inventor; and after using up his
spare moments and coin for some years, he produced what
Prof. Schnabel declared to be theoretically the “ best pro-
cess for the treatment of sulphide ores.” And (this is
told for the encouragement of inventors), according to his
own account, this is how he came out of his successive
rounds with Fatman, Esq. : First time he ran short of
cash he had to hand over twd-thirds of his interest for
£lOOO. Then, on Mr. Schlapp’s glowing report, a
quarter-share in the whole thing was bought by
the Gibbs, Bright and Co. people. Experimental
plants, &c., were wanted ; when 500,000 shares in
the Sulphide Corporation were paid to the ven-
dors, Ashcroft’s “end” was 56,000. His “financiers"
took the balance of his parcel ; and he still owed £15,000.
Since then the £15,000 has been paid ; but he has had to
surrender his entire interest in the co. In other words,
it has cost the inventor about £lO,OOO to produce the
theoretically-" best process of sulphide-treatment yet
made known.”

Birthday mine, Parkes, has had another nasty
fall. Resurrected under the name of the Avoca Reefs.
Great were the prognostications as to results from cyanid-
ing. The results, however, have launched the co. prac-
tically into insolvency. Further calls are to be made to
sink the shaft and pay off all outstanding liabilities,
“ with the exception of the bank overdraft.” What grim
satire is this ?

Geologist Pittman records the interesting theory
that the White Rock mine (Drake, N.S.W.) is the filled-up
neck of a volcanic vent. But he carefully avoids the
more interesting problem : is there any pay-ore in the
mine? It was the sensational million-mine of 12 years
ago, but it killed two local companies and the third is
likely to share a similar fate.
« ifc * *
What is the trouble with the Lake George
mines? Returns low, but remarkably consistent; large
quantities of ore mined, and, judging from reports, taken
fairly from all parts of the Workings, profits, according
to official statements, nearly £4OOO per month. Were
copper to decline to £5O per ton, there would still be a
good profit. Reported that the co. is now out of debt;
yet, despite all these things, shares are buyable at
9s. —less than two years’ purchase on present output.
Stated in Sydney that all the selling orders come from
Melbourne, the headquarters of the co. On reports, Lake
George, at £78,750, is an incomprehensibility when com-
pared with some W.A. stocks, the market-values of which
run into seven figures. Still, the fact that big lines are
always available in Melbourne must be accepted as an
indication that things are not all they seem. The market
talks.
• * * *
At Nutherungie, way out in Wilcannia wastes,
there is said to be a large deposit of copper-ore “superior
to anything found in Cobar.” The warden thinks that
there is enough ore in sight to warrant the erection of
smelters. The owners of the thing, apparently, think
otherwise. They are waiting for English capitalists to
come along and take the risks. If the place is superior to
Cobar why isn’t it worked for all it is worth ? Cobar was
a div.-paying mine, despite the handicap of over 200
miles of road-carriage, in its early days. Nutherungie is
within 70 miles of permanent navigable water.

New Bushman’s mine, Parkes, granted another
lease of life. Taken on tribute. Will history repeat

* * * *
A sharp lesson touching the folly of depositing
trust-money in private banking accounts has been taught
by Judge Tin O’B. P. Gibson at Broken Hill. An agent —
a man well known in Melb. land-boom circles—sold a
cottage, took £lBO for it* and lodged the cash at a bank
where he was overdrawn. He alleges that he lodged it
thus on the understanding that he could draw it, less his
commission, to pay the vendor. Just then bank-managers
changed, the arrangement was denied, neither deeds nor
cash was forthcoming—and the sentence was two and
a-half years’ hard.
* * * *
Paddy Lackey Deep Levels, N.S.W. : A side-on
show. Shaft started two years ago to pick up reef on
underlay, down about 350 ft. Five thousand shares were
issued at £1 some months back, £5OOO more was raised
by debentures, and a good cut has been made into this.
Should cut the reef between 500 ft. and 600 ft., but, if they
drive north on it they will soon find themselves in the
parent mine’s ground, as it runs at an angle across the
lease, and the underlie is not nearly so rapid north,
whereas, in driving south, one of the Paddy Lackey 5-acre
blocks, running past the boundary easterly, will be met
with. As writer has said, it will be “The Very Deep
Levels ” before it is much good to anyone.
• * * *
Manager Stewart has taken possession of the
Chillagoe Co.’s mines, and many people expect the shares
to boom. Others, having no shares, but large interests in
surrounding and other blocks, expect these to “ go off
like wildfire.” Up to date, they show no sign of
going off, and unless something tangible is done by
somebody in London shortly, played-out boomsters
will begin to kick themselves vigorously, and want to
know what use are rich copper-mines, anyhow. Chillagoe
has had its little boom—it was short-lived, because the
public soon realised that three years must elapse before
the parent mine could get things going in good shape,
that meantime the copper-market may collapse, and no
matter if it didn’t—outside shows that could not work
without a railway could never weather the storm of wait-
ing for one. The surface copper deposits of Chillagoe
district are probably the richest in Australasia—it is also
admitted that generally they are in limestone —a ffirma-
tion in which the richest of nearly all metals is found,
and by the natural order of things the richest has the
shortest life. There are one or two celebrated copper-
mines in limestone, but exceptions prove, &c. So far no
attempt has been made to prove the Chillagoe mines in
depth ; that must be done sooner or later. If they go
down, and are permanently rich, there will be nothing in
the world to equal them ; if, like nine out of ten other
limestone deposits, they peter out, and are otherwise as
treacherous as they are rich—well, it won’t take long to
exhaust all the surface shows of the field. Stewart does
not deal with this phase in his report—probably because
he has had no experience of exactly similar formations in
other parts of the world to warrant him in doing so, and
Stewart is admittedly a verra cautious man. With copper
at over £7O a ton there is no sign of another Chillagoe
boom, and there won’t he till permanency and richness in
depth have been established beyond all doubt.
* * * *

* • * *
Castlemaine, Vic., is having a little boom —a big
one compared with the depression long hanging over that
camp. Spring Gully Mine still running over an ounce,
reef big and well-defined, and gold run so long that other
mines are prospecting for it. Adding 20 stamps ; divs.
regular. Also has one mine dredging at a profit; others
threatening to sluice away about two-thirds of the place,
which seems quite prepared to be washed away in the
interests of the boom that makes it feel good.
Controversy has been raging i n Melb. as to whether
Prof.M'Coy, or any other geologist, did say manyyears ago
that gold in quartz would not live or pay below 300 or
400 ft. All the living members of the Royal Mining Com-
mission of ’56 deny that any such statement was made,
but admit that all the evidence went to show that richness
decreased with depth—on Bendigo particularly. So it is
the world over. Sometimes rich things go down, but
mostly they don’t. As a rule, the first 100 ft. contains
more gold than the next 1000 ft. Nor has the old order
of things changed on newer fields. Westralia is a striking
example of the golden rule. Bayley’s got much more
gold above 100 ft. than in all the levels below. London-
derry had thousands of ounces in a big, solid, rocksalt-
looking reef within 6ft. of the surface. At the Wealth of
Nations, “ Big John ” and other stones containing hunks
were lying on the surface, but nothing of the sort was
found below it. On any new camp, the writer, in ex-
change for all the surface-gold, would allow other people
the pleasure of driving tunnels into mountains and sink-
ing into the bowels of the earth to find the balance left.
• * * •
A man was recently fined at Ballarat for re-
moving the top-dressing of the streets at Little Bendigo,
a suburb of the “Golden City.” He and two mates had
been in the habit of sweeping the streets at night, and
panning-off the result. They were captured at 3 a.m.
with brooms, shovels, a cradle, and a wheelbarrow (latter
presumably to carry away the gold). Yield not stated.
» • * *
The Miners’ Association of Vic., which includes
Tasmanians also in its membership, has just over 9000
names on its rolls, though presumably there are at least
30,000 eligible? in the two provinces. Comparatively
Zeehan (Tas.) is the strongest union centre ; it has 820
members, and about £'3ooo in hand. Bendigo has 2000
members, and £2609 ; Ballarat, 1600 members, and £1720.
Financially Stawell is strongest—26s members, and £2651
funds. The average cost per member in benefits last
year was 255. 6d.

Another famous Berry (Vic.) mine nearing its
end. The manager of the Berry Consols is “ hopeful that
the ground cut up for blocking will maintain profitable
results for the current six months,” the significance of
which is shown in the further statement that “ prospect-
ing has been kept well advanced, but unfortunately with
indifferent results.” Last half-year’s record: gold,
£38,455 ; divs., £16,500. Record to date : gold, £810,589 ;
divs., £313,000 ; royalty paid, £60,815. Called-up capital,
£76,000.
• * * *
An English co. having acquired the land be-
tween the Great Southern Garden Gulf mine (Bendigo)
and the boundary of the cemetery, we may look for a re-
vival of the burying-ground scheme of 28 years ago. A
syndicate of speculators bought a number of contiguous
grave-yard allotments and seriously contended that they
had the right to sink a common grave and that it must
be immaterial to the trustees whether the grave was sunk
6ft, or 1001 ft. The collapse of the boom stopped the
scheme in the syndicate stage.
* * * *
Extended Hustlers (Bendigo) struck very rich
quartz at 2800 ft. —the second deepest point at which pay-
ore has been found on the field.
Gilfillan and McCreery, mining engineers,
metallurgists and assayers, 434 Collins-st., Melbourne.
Reports on mines, prepares plans and specifications for all
kinds of mining plant. Complete plant for bulk tests.*
* * * *
Vic. miners are being enticed to Broken Hill in
considerable numbers with (he bait of 9s. per day. But
they don’t take long to find out, when on the spot, that
day-labor for miners is practically unknown on the
Barrier now, and that at contracting (often in places
where local men have kicked against working) they make
what they can ; though if their earnings should happen
to pass 9s. per shift for a week the contract is promptly
terminated.
* • * »
The Austral Otis Engineering Co. Ltd., South
Melb., has completed and delivered a very fine set of
winding engines for the Cobar Gold Mines, N.S.VV., capable
of hauling a load of two tons from 1500 ft. at a speed of
1000 ft. per minute. The engines are a pair of 14" cylin-
ders, geared to 7ft. loose drum, and are fitted with link
motion reversing gear, vertical indicators, andpoweiful
brake gear. The pair of engines are set to one side of the
drums, and all the driving levers are brought behind the
drums. The co. has also just delivered a fine set of
pumping plant for the Paddy Lackey mine for taking the
shaft down to 1000 ft.
* * » »
No mining co. ever had a more appropriate
name than the Big Blow, Limited. The only improve-
ment possible was perhaps a modification to the Big Blow
and Bluff Amalgamated and Unlimited. The original co.,
the Big Blow Amalgamated, was registered in ’94 with a
nominal capital of £150,000. The modest vendors took
£25,039 and 99,961 pound shares. In Aug., ’96 a new co.
was registered—just the Big Blow this time ; nominal
capital, 1200,000. Two major-generals and a brace of
captains were on the board to lead it to victory. By the
end of ’97 they had actually got a crushing—23o tons stone
yielded 740 z. Since then there have been more crushings,
until this gorgeous mine has yielded 805 oz. from 3029
tons. Apart from what the vendors had been paid, it had
not cost more than about £40,000 to produce this much.
The new co. in 12 months or so swamped £13,689 on the
mine and £4917 on “ London administration ” ; besides, it
owed on the latter account about £l5OO more. At about
this point, Boulder Hamilton was invited to report on
the show ; and his verdict was—too poor to pay. So the
machinery has been sold for £2OOO, and an official receiver
is waiting for somebody to offer something for the mine.
Apropos the value of metals exported, and
especially of the Westralian gold, as to which some
remarks were lately made, when it was said that the
figures officially published were not reliable. In the case
of the Big Blow, shareholders complained to the official
receiver that they had been led to suppose that the gold
was worth nearly £4 per oz., whereas some of it actually
realised £1 11s. 6d. per oz. and other of it £2 10s. To
which the directors’ only reply was that the value of the
bullion could never be known until it went to the mint,
and was reduced to standard value. That is, until the
local mint Is in operation, the value cannot be known
until long after the official returns have been published.
Tho value of Lake View Consols output for
March was £33,000, and the cost of production £lO,OOO ■
the latter not including development work, which is’
charged to capital account. The profit on working
account was £23,000-equal to £276,000 a year. The out"
put, it is said, will be doubled when the sulphide plant is
going. Suppose that the working costs are not increased
there will then be a profit of £562,000 per year, or 13-8
per cent, on present market value of the shares. The
buyer at £l6 who expects a profit of 6 per cent, on his
investment at compound interest would have to wait ten
years before he gets his principal back.

* * * *
Refreshing to hear the managers of Kalgoorlie
boss mines, at the Exhibition-opening banquet, on the
alluvial digger. Said Hamilton, of the Boulder : “ No-
thing more immediately benefits a country than a good
alluvial field. The alluvial miner does not send the money
he gets for the gold out of the country in the form of
dividends. You should remember the alluvial miner,
then, when framing further mining legislation.” And
Callahan, of Lake View : “ The alluvial man is the path-
finder. We follow afterwards. There is no capitalist
equal to the man who has the muscle to make the
country. The residents of the Kalgoorlie district could
have made the country what it is to-day without a dollar
of British capital.” And these things were said, not at a
miners’ shivoo, but at a dinner to J. Bull’s representative,
Jerry Smith.
They made big efforts in the West to kill the
uneasy feeling with regard to local banks by heaving at it
chunks of ridicule. The most-favored yarn of the genesis
of the mild scare was that a local pub.-keeper, whose
cheques had been declined, took the first opportunity
that offered to say, when the bank’s notes came in—
“Jlere, just wait a minute till I send round and get gold
for this. Safer, you know ; and, well — ” the rest being a
wink. The most feasible part of the yarn is that it was a
pub.-keeper’s cheque that was returned ; for Perth pub.-
keepers are about the stone-brokest crowd in Australia
just now. The statement that somebody presented a
note to pay for a drink in Perth casts grave doubt upon
the whole fabrication.

Gov. Tennyson (says The Bulletin’s
Adelaide correspondent) is fat, florid, and flutter-
some in appearance, with a half-bald head and a
closely-trmnned beard. He is evidently most
anxious to please, and has, so far, made a very
favorable impression. “ There is as much meat
in his talk as on his bones, and I think he is a
very good fellow,” was Premier Kingston’s com-
ment after a few snatches of conversation with
him on the day of his arrival. His speech at the
swearing-in was rounded in phrase, and given in
a full, orotund voice, and its only fault was, per-
haps, a too-plenteous quotation from his father’s
poems. He reminded the audience incidentally
that Arctic-navigator John Franklin, once Gov.
m lasmania, was his uncle. Lady Tennyson is
too charming for words, and so is the French
governess, Mdlle. Dussan, who accompanied the
vice-regal party to the platform, and smiled with
sweet benignity upon the delighted spectators,
who caught exquisite glimpses of her shapely
ankles as she tripped up the steps. Gov. Buxton
was impossible to the verbatim reporter, who was
reduced to despair by his sentences, but his pre-
sent Excellency recites his piece in a way which
is balm and nectar to the stenographer. Indeed
he gets along so deliberately that he could almost
be written up ”as he goes.

As predicted, Coolgardie Exhibition has
turned out a miserable fiasco. According to pre-
sent outlook, the show will come to a speedy and
inglorious termination. Mining-magnate Mor-
gans, the president,, is wroth with the Easterners
for their apathyJn the matter, and threatens to go
dead against federation in consequence. He
considers that N.S.W., Victoria, S.A., and Q.
Govts, should each have voted ,£lO,OOO or so
apiece in recognition of the splendid markets that
Westralia has opened up for them. But then
the other provinces have all had some previous
experience of Joubert, and his mode of run-
ning shows. John Forrest was reluctantly dragged
into the concern at the eleventh hour, and foolishly
gave £5OOO of public money towards boosting it
up. Then, when fairly committed to the wretched
affair, he had to give another =£sooo, and Heaven
knows how much more will be needed to get out
of the mess. In this “exhibition” the W.A.
Premier has a particularly ravenous wolf by the
ears.
Consider the J.P., how he “acts the goat.”
At Carlton (Melb.) again—of course—on Monday
week, H. Gresham was charged with a “cowardly,
unprovoked and brutal assault ” (Age report) on a
young woman whom he met the previous Satur-
day afternoon in Carlton Gardens. Coming up,
he, without warning, struck her, blackening her
eyes and rendering her insensible. He was gently
and reluctantly fined ,£1 or 48 hours, and the
kindly magistrates allowed him a week in which
to raise the cash !! ! Having thus nobly dis-
charged their duty, these two awful beaks next
proceeded to consider the case of a man and woman
who had been locked up since the preceding
Saturday on a charge of common or public-house
drunkenness. They were promptly fined £2 or
one month each ! ! ! All three had been previously
convicted. Truly, the Carlton J.P. is a hopeless
case.

A correspondent, on the Bourke Camel
Carrying Co. trouble
Dear Bulletin.— The camel-drivers’ trouble at Bourke
seems to be much a question of interpreting. Stated that
Abdul Wade, the camel co.’s manager, put aside N.S.W.
Govt, interpreter, and himself translated the agreement.
The Govt, interpreter does not seem to know whether
Abdul Wade’s translation was right or wrori g. These
men, if really Afghans, speak a language quite different
from Hindustani, though some of them may understand
a little Hindustani or Panjabi. In fact there are four
languages spoken in Afghanistan, and an interpreter to
be efficient would have to know them all. In view of the
above-mentioned statement about the translation of the
camel-drivers’ agreement, a little more precise informa-
tion about the Govt, interpreter’s linguistic attainments
would be interesting.—D.G.

Melbourne seems to have made up its
mind that phrenologist Medor, the much-tor-
mented man who committed one murder and two
lots of personal injuries at the Eastern Market,
shah not be hanged for his frenzy. Perhaps he
didn’t attack the right people, and certainly he
erred in—horrible deed !—gouging out the eye of a
third party; but when a man’s business neighbors
are either leagued together to persecute him “on
their own,” or to laugh at the practical “ jokes ”
which madden him, nobody can reasonably
wonder if his vengeance run its course. Judged
by a tribunal of nervous subjects, capable of
imagining themselves in the murderer’s place,
Medor would get six months imprisonment and an
address of sympathy. Tried at Melb. Criminal
Court he is sure to get something rougher than
that. All the same, he can reckon that he
has helped to humanise the Eastern Market.

Melb. Herald let itself loose over the
Eastern Market murder, and its report was lurid
melodrama with coagulated gore all over the shop.
Comic relief was provided in the shape of slabs of
bald and awful bathos: And down upon the
awful scene in the shop carpeted with blood
. gazes Her Majesty the Queen in serene dignity,
from a cheap print upon the wall.” Ye gods!
The average reporter would seem to have the art
instinct of the carroty small boy who illuminates
stables and hen houses with the assistance of a
clay puddle and a big stick.
♦ * * *
As soon as Melb. police authorities re-
covered from the shock of astrologer Medor’s
Market murder, they set hurriedly to work to
clear all the fortune-tellers, palmists, and bump-
gropers out of the place, bundling them neck-and-
crop into Little Collins-street. These people have
been at work in the market for years, but it
needed a gory horror to make the powers officially
aware of their existence, and the distempered sud-
denness of the clearing-up suggests an agonising
dread on the part of the police that all the
astrologers and mahogany-stained gipsies and
other peddlers of horoscopes were liable to break
out suddenly and shed gore over the Council’s
property. But the tribe will be back on the
job again within three months.
“S.”: Astrologer Medor, the Melb. Market
murderer, evidently had the most sublime faith
in his calling, and held it in such reverence
that the customer who dared to display the
slightest cynicism was haughtily shown off the
premises. He is just the sort of man who would
rather declare in the dock that he knew all along
he was going to be the prime-mover in a No. 1
tragedy, and let it be imagined the crime was pre-
meditated, than have people believe for a moment
that the stars had kept this important fact dark
to him. I can imagine his lawyer saying, “But
if you talk like that they’ll hang you,” and Medor
replying loftily: “May be, but you forget I have
a reputation to maintain ! ”
* * * *
The question as to whether one or two
policemen behaved invalorously in the Melb.
Eastern Market tragedy excitement very soon got
settled to the satisfaction of the Eoorce. One
Goodison was alleged to have made a statement of
police backwardness in coming forward, or for-
wardness in keeping out of the way. Therefore
four detectives and inspectors came along to
Goodison and asked him if he intended to accuse
the police of cowardice, and Goodison not un-
naturally said “No” with a capital N. Also he
threw in the remark that Constable somebody
showed “ coolness, courage, and discretion,” after
which the regiment of ’tecs felt more than proud
of their comrades, and went away, leaving Goodi-
son to breathe again.

When a party of Christchurch (M.L.)
aldermen lately took an outing to inspect the
municipal reserves, the little bill for the dozen
persons totalled .£l4 4s. Gd.—made up of drag-
hire, £2 10s. ; edibles £5 55.; one doz. whisky,
£2 14s. : one doz. ale, 13s. 6d. ; one doz. cider,
12s. ; one doz. each soda-water, lemonade, and
ginger ale, Bs. ; 100 manilla cigars, £1 12s. ; 250
cigarettes, 10s. One of the reserves visited was
appropriately named “Bottle Lake.”
* * * »
Memo, for Premier Turner, who is draft-
ing an Old Age Pension Bill. Old man Alfred
Rhodes was brought before Heidelberg (Vie.)
court—-vagrancy. Pound in a stable, starving—
which is about the meanest crime in the calendar.
Taken to the station, he spied some crumbs on the
cell-floor, and instantly devoured them. In the
intervals of difficult mastication, the old chap told
of his woes. Had been well-to-do. N Bix children,
all married. All right till his wife died —and
then “strangers came in, and robbed me of my
home. Ah ! there was no woman like my dear
old girl,” and he wept. The bench sent him to
gaol for six months. “I’d rather die,” he whim-
pered, “ than go to gaol.” “But at Geelong it
isn’t like a gaol,” said a sympathising constable ;
“ You’ll get the sea-breeze, and be treated tip-top.
I wouldn’t mind going there myself for a holi-
day.” The old fellow gazed at him through his
tears. “Ah, well,” said he, “ mebbe it isn’t as
bad as I thought. Can you give me another
crust V ”
* * * *
Silverton, which bears to Broken Hill
much the same relation as that into which Cool-
gardie is drifting with regard to Kalgoorlie and
The Boulder, still retains a municipality, chiefly
because there aren’t enough people left there to
wind it up. For some years after Silverton’s
glories had faded, its chief business consisted of
removing itself into Broken Hill. Most of the
houses were of wood and iron ; some were pulled
to pieces first, and others were laboriously hauled
1G miles over bush-tracks. Even that industry
has failed these last three or four years, and its
occupation has since consisted of swapping horses
with passing stockmen, showing occasional visi-
tors the scene of the departed pub. where the boys
used up 20 doz. gold-top one evening, and
exchanging perjuries about “ slugs ” and
“ jumping ” cases. There hasn’t been any muni-
cipal election since the light failed, and nobody
pays rates ; when the aldermen want money to
buy stamps for a letter to the Govt, petitioning
for a special grant, they go out and pound some-
body’s goat. _ The other day the clerk, or ex-clerk
(this paper isn’t committing itself), sent in a bill
for =B4O, accumulated screw of several years. The
aldermen weren’t very sure whether there was a
clerk or not; in fact, they aren’t sure whether
they are aldermen or not. But on the off-chance,
and insufficient goats being available, they pulled
up some galvanised-iron fence and sold it to raise
funds to fight the clerk with in the courts. And
yet you can find Silverton printed in big letters
on some maps.

The delusion that jurors if they! agree
without leaving the box will be kept in the box
for the next case was “exploded” by N.S.W.
Judge Backhouse the other day. It is probable
this “ popular delusion,” as the Judge called it,
originated in seme whimsical Judge ordering a
jury who so agreed to stay for another case. Which
shows that Judges should take no liberties with
juries. For instance, ’twas once a popular delu-
sion with many jurors that if they found a map

guilty of murder and recommended him to mercy
the man’s neck would inevitably be spared.
Judges and Executives, however, took liberties
with juries in this respect—in fact, like Judge
Backhouse, exploded the popular delusion, and
now jurors go a far way around (as in the Flynn-

slaughter alone, thus saving the man’s neck with-
out giving the Judge a chance to interfere.

THE PAGAN.
I saw my Love, for whom my heart had ached,
And all my fierce and love-mad body thrilled.
And, ah —he loved at last! His heart awaked
And found itself with answering' madness filled.
And afterwards, when I was quite alone,
I looked above me, where the white moon trod
Her sweet, smooth path that is with star-flowers strown,
And said, “ Dear God, I love you—dear, dear God ! ”

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
ecclesiastical courts” is absurdly weak in face*of
the facts that the fidelity of even Archbishop
Temple himself has been questioned, and that
more than half his fellow bishops are as “ High ”
as they make ’em. The Australian episcopate is
similarly affected, and here the bishops have a
looser hold on their clergy. Primate Smith, of
course, is opposed to ritualistic purple and gold,
and crosses, and incense, and Gregorianism, but
there are at least three churches in Sydney that an
R.C. stranger might easily mistake on occasions
for temples of the Papacy.
Apparently mining-engineering, as a pro-
fession, is supplanting medicine and law in popu-
lar favor in N.S.W. At Sydney University this
year 37 students entered the first year in the
Engineering school, among them several graduates
in other faculties, including a gold-medallist in
classics. The average enrolment in this year has
previously been about ten.
* * * *
It is stated that 105 cases of lead-paralysis
have occurred at the Broken Hill Smelting Works
at Port Pirie, S.A. Among the latest sufferers is
Councillor Connolly, one of the most useful mem-
bers of the local Town Council, whose arms have
been so badly paralysed that he cannot lift food to
his mouth.

How these medicos love one another ! In
a Sydney suburb there are two doctors, X and Y.
As X was being driven on his rounds he was
accosted by a passer-by: “Beg pardon, but
aren’t you Dr. Y?” “No, thank God! Drive
on, coachman ! ”

Verily Sydney Sunday Times is real
cabbage-tree ! The Bulletin (keenly alive to
the fact that a paper only gets the sort of letters
it wants) reprints this from a half-column howl
in S. Times aforesaid about the urgent necessity
for plenty of flogging in Australia :
Flogging at the cart’s tail is generally supposed to have
ceased after 1827, but several instances are on record of
that punishment being imposed up to 1836. This vanished
system not only inflicted deep physical suffering, but also
great public degradation, and it is to be hoped that the
Government of any country will not be under the painful
necessity of renewing public punishment of such descrip-
tions.
“It is to be hoped ” Govt, will not be under the
painful necessity of—nailing anyone’s ears to the
pump ! What a lovely idea for the year 1899 .
By the way, the S. Times office is situate in the
old cart-tail-flogging district, ihe punishment
for purloining oars from a waterman s wherry was
flogging at the cart’s-tail down Druitt-stieet to
Darling Harbor. , , *
Shocking little Melb. boys have discovered
Lord Brassey’s falling sickness, and now when
H.E. rides abroad, or walks, or drives, or sails, or
propels himself on a machine, he is greeted with
shrill yells of “ Fall off, Governor ! ’
Be N.S.W. “Lieutenant-Col.” Neild’s
contention that the letters to his superior officers
which have been adjudged insubordinate were
not written in his military capacity, but upon the
basis of his Parliamentary position, The Bulletin
supposes that if, some day, in actual resistance to
invasion, some Parliamentary warrior or other
should be charged with prematurely retreating,
he would explain that he was running away
merely in his civil capacity, and strictly without
prejudice to his military intrepidity.
The German communities in S.A. are dis-
tinguished by their frugality. It transpired in a
court case, the other day, that for 3d. a week one
can be a member of the Kranken und Begrabniss
TJnterstiitzungs-Kasse—which in itself is a good
deal for one’s money—and command the services
of a specially-selected German doctor. Also it
was mentioned that members called in their medi-
cal adviser for consultation upon agricultural and
other problems, and then forgot to make any pro-
vision for him in their wills—which, indeed, was
what this law-suit was about.

“ sojer ” told me t’other day that before he went
to recent Sydney encampment he knocked up two-
dozen coffins for stock, but business was so brisk
that he had to return two days before breaking-up
and resume casket-building.
George Burgess, the
well-to-do baker of York
(S.A.) who went suddenly
demented, and butchered his
wife and children, and then
committed suicide, stuck
closely to the traditions of
his kind in one respect. He
got up early in the morning
and did the slaughter. Most
demented people who butcher
their families or commit sui-
cide do it early in the morn-
ing—provided, that is, they
quietly develop into the re-
quired mental condition. If
their incipient madness is
brought to a head by sudden
excitement, as in Napoleon
Lis son’s case, this rule
doesn’t apply, "*|jyt if Lisson
hadn’t been hurried into in-
sanity by external events he
would also, most probably,
have developed quite silently
till he also became ap early-
rising homicide. Apparently
the voices that moan in the
ear of the man with the tot-
tering intellect moan their
loudest in the early morning
when there are no passing
carts to drown the row. Pos-
sibly insanity leads-to, early
rising—or perhaps early rising
leads to insanity. Anyhow,
the old man who is found
hanging in the barn, and the
old woman whose remains are
discovered in the drinking
water, mostly get there al iout
6 a.m. in the winter or 4 a. m.
in summer. And in fully half
the cases where the whole
family is obliterated with an
axe the horror is first dis-
covered because some other
early-rising person (who will
probably murder his own
family later on) sees blood
under the front door when he
comes with the milk, or the
early meat, or the paper.
There was much unreported fun at a re-
cent swell banquet mainly through the vagaries
of a wealthy and ancient squatter. He had paid
£2 2s. for his ticket, and was determined to make
a profit on the transaction. His feet were sore,
and he staggered the august company by appear-
ing in carpet slippers and pepper-and-salt tweeds.
When the eating began he was observed to be
“ pouching ” heavily, and he informed the scan-
dalised importer on the right that he would enjoy
“ them” when he got back home. The carcase-
butcher on the left drew his attention to a large
and beautiful jelly, and later on he induced the
old man to hold his pocket open, and flopped the
jelly in. Later on the ancient cursed that carcase-

butcher to his face. When the show was over he
sat on the steps of the banqueting-hall, with the

of assorted fruits that the carcase-inan had poured

the Federal capital, and walked down street in
grey socks trying to comfort the poor girls with
repeated assurances that he had a “ bootiful
sooper ” for them. And he owns several flourish-
ing stations.
• • * *
Bad policy for a member of “cronk”
society to wag a loose tongue in court. A young
larcenist, at Melb. Criminal Court the other day,
asking for lenient treatment on the first offence
ticket, was told that “his cross-examination
showed him to be intimately acquainted with the
language, customs, and resorts of abandoned
women and thieves.” He had practically been
owning up to previous offences all the time that
the man in the big wig was taking notes.
* * * •
A new and flourishing industry has
sprung ,up in Victoria— viz., cat-farming. The
place of operations is situate at Christmas Hills,
and bears the title of “ Waratah Cattery. ” The
practice is to breed the animals for their skins,
retaining the finest to sell as pets. When skinned,
the deceased Tom or Tabby is fed to his live
relatives, economy and utility being thus beauti-
fully combined.
• • • •
All classes are represented in the First
Australian Horse. This was exemplified at the
recent encampment, when a fatigue party of eight,
under a sergeant (a shearer) was detailed to dig
trenches and clean up generally. It was found to
consist of two squatters, two bank managers, a
schoolmaster, a solicitor, a country editor, and a
blacksmith.

It is a woman, if you please, who sends
this to The Bulletin
The young men of Albury' are to be congratulated on
their exclusion of the fair sex (including clergymen) from
their debates. The ruination of every debating-society
dates from the evening when some weak-eyed softy gets
up with a grin and begs for “ the refining influence of
the ladies.” When a young man joins a debating-society
he does serin order to debate ; when a woman joins, nine
times out of ten, it is to look at the young man, and from
thence the society degenerates into gigglings and “ l-do-
thiriks” (which is woman’s nearest approach to argu-
ment), till the young-man part of the society 7 dies out in
a thin fringe of amorous youths waiting to see their girls
home. The Salon of a century ago was a capital institu-
tion because it consisted of many men and a few picked
women, but we can never hope for the Salon again. The
commonplace women would swamp it.

The pneumatic postal despatch-tube, so
long in use elsewhere, is shortly to be experi-
mented with in Sydney. First try is to be be-
tween G.P.O. and Exchange.
One Australian gaol flagellator is
an enthusiast. He approaches the tri-
angles with an athletic thrill. When he
draws blood, he gyrates with uncontrol-
lable joy and trails the wetted thongs
in the surrounding sand. Then, with
demoniacal appetite for work, he lashes
savagely at his victim’s lacerated back.
If the subject whimper not at the tor-
ture, the fury of the flagellator is cyclonic.
And such things be at this period of
civilisation ! ”

Awhile ago many a stranger who
dropped in upon Perth unannounced had
veil- satisfied with a shake-down on the
billiard-table —no ordinary four-posters
being available. But to-day, according
to the humble prayer of the local Bung
Association to the Licensing Bench, you
could march an army in and get bunk
accommodation on the spot. Bung’s little
list is supplied to support his plea that
he has too much competition already;
and to that extent it may be sniffed at
suspiciously. Nathless, the association
secretary has sworn to it more or less, and
has deposed that he found one pub. with
25 beds, all of which were empty ; others
with 22, 7, 12, 12, 18, 9, 26, and so on in
the same unprofitable state. Of 1300
beds in the Perth district pubs., less than
400 were occupied when this “census”
was taken.
Perhaps, in late years, and in Austra-
lia, there never has been a greater
scramble for beds than there was on the
Tas. West Coast when Zeehan and Dun-
das began to be talked about, and when
the thousands who had 4 ‘ just missed it ”
at Broken Hill trekked across to pick up
a few of the scores of bigger plums with
which the bleak Coast was, of course,
stuffed. The landing-place then was at
Trial Harbor—a magnificent port out of
which the miserable cockle-shells scudded
on the first signs of an approaching storm.
Writer was offered, at the only pub., the
choice between the bar-form and the
coach standing outside; he preferred to
bunk in the latter, with two others.
First thing in the morning (it was driz-
zling, as usual) the boss came round col-
lecting; half-a-crown a head for “accom-
modation.” It was part up or get down
in the drizzle. Later on, at Zeehan,
writer was, to begin with, No. 20 on
j r, 7
the dining-room floor. No. 19 was a Yio.
M.L.C., now an M.L.A. ; No. 21 one of the
best - known mine - managers in Australia, sup-
posed just then to be representing Lansell.
No. 7 died next day, and all below him went
up one. Eight went out prospecting, some
more returned to the mainland, and in three days
No. 20 was No. 3. Two days after that he was
promoted to a bed.
SKETCHED AT A BUSH “DAHNCE":
“ THE MUSICIANER.”
A LAUREATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When ev’ry warbler’s tuneful voice was heard ;
When fields were vocal all with bee and bird ;
When every rippling brook was loud and clear ;
When Night was big with Music soft and dear;
When ev’ry bell was beating out a chime,
And ev’ry wind was wafting rippled rhyme—
He wandered near the Monarchs of the Mire ;
He twanged in haste a broken harp for hire;
He bent him down in dear congenial Dust,
And sang how chaste the regal brood of Lust;
He glorified the seed of Rapine old ;
He called the Purple Butcher “ kind and bold,”
And when the Despot died we heard him sing :
“ I weave a shroud of lies to lap the King !”
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.
EVE.
It was a picture large and fair
Of Adam’s wife^—and daughter !
Clad only in her flowing hair,
Beside the rippling water.
The artist scorned the fig-leaf blind
That precedent had taught ’em.
I wrote a title to my mind,
’Tis, “Mother Eve in Autumn.”
Melb.
S.
SUNRISE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The maiden morn, full-lipped and tender-browed,
Rich in the promise of a perfect day,
Now paces, joyous, on her sun-flecked way,
Charming the camp-smoke into crimson cloud,
To float in majesty, serene and proud,
About her veiled feet. And far away,
Around some mountain’s verdured crest to stay,
Low-ling’ring o’er a forest golden-boughed.
The bells chime clearlj 7 from an echoing creek,
The hobbles jingle musical and low,
The fairy cobwebs, diamonded with dew,
Tremble with joy divine as poets seek.
The morning breezes whisper as they blow ;
Up bursts the sun, and day begins anew.
WILL. M. FLEMING.


Amy Castles, the much-paragraphed Australian
song-bird, will, no doubt, wing her flight to Europe to
perfect her voice by the highest artistic training. Just
as cultivation perfects the harmonies of song, so do P.D.
corsets perfect the harmonies of graceful curves and flow-
ing outlines of the female form divine. A pair of P.D.’s
cultivates the figures as effectively as a Marchesi cultivates
the voice. Borsdoff and Co. are agents for this invalu-
able aid to feminine attractiveness.*
“Lead Kindly Light,” the beautiful poetic in-
spiration, was written by Cardinal Newman. The light
that leads all other earthly lights is given by the “ Alba-
tross Lamp Wick.” They “ require no trimming,” have
no smell, give a greater light, prevent explosions and
broken chimneys, and are the woman’s friend.*
