A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 17, 1899.
My Dear Myee—
Never have I heard the word “ Magnificent ” so
flayed upon for all, and more than all, it was
Wprth, as at the great Army meeting last week,
when General Booth told the story of his life.
Lately, Magnificent has seemed to be getting a
little rusty. Everything to-day is Great. The
play was “great.” The ball was “great.” The
supper was “great.”- The cricket was “great,”
which does not mean Great any more than the
frightful “colonial” horror, “not too stinking,”
means that something didn’t smell bad.
In most people’s youth, Magnificent took a hol-
low sound from the unrealities of history learnt at
school, and never lest it. But last Tuesday there
was a revival, and Chief Justice Darley said the
General’s speech was Magnificent, and G. R.
Dibbs said “such a Magnificent speech I have
never heard. ” And plain Mr. George Reid shook
out a lot of tidy sentiments through which Mag-
nificent leaped and bounded wherever a place
opened for an epithet. Such a run on one word
might mean sincerity. Or it might mean that the
seventy-year-old General had not struck off in-
spiration enough to suggest a word apiece to those
three great minds.
On his seventieth birthday, he who may be the
man of the century next century, looked shrunken
with illness. His long, yellow-grey beard fell flab-
bily on his chest, and his long, red nose was pinched.
The eyes were not too dim to show the hollows
round them. He twitched nervously and looked
round constantly for draughts. When he got up
to speak I wondered how on earth he was going to
get through five minutes—much less 70 years. His
voice was a sort of growly husk without any
quality. He built it up as he went on, and pre-
sently there he was shouting and shooting humor
and humanity right away to the back rows of the
gallery. It was a hot and stirring cry from high
levels. But the cold earth-word ‘ ‘ collection ” came
in and spoilt everything. Why don’t the orators
realise that ?
“The Story of My Life” was not what the
General told. Nobody regretted, though. It is
only the man of 20 who legitimately tells the story
of his long and wicked and extraordinary career.
Thirty tries it, and ends in—well, perhaps in
kisses. Forty feels that the story wouldn’t be in-
teresting from middle-aged lips. Fifty begins to
remember. Sixty keeps on reviving. Seventy has
got it all by heart. But, just then, nobody wants
to listen. With a sublime knowledge of human
nature—perhaps including his own—the General.
kept the most part of his story to himself.
Sydney Liedertafel is flourishing. It opened
its new season last Wednesday to what is con-
sidered a record house for the first night of the
new season. The V.R. patronage swelled itself
out with Judge Real from Queensland, Mr. Henry
Dilke, and a big party of Burdekins, who always
come out strong at Liedertafel concerts.
But why do some people go to concerts to do what
they could do just as satisfactorily at home? For
instance, you see a young, pretty girl come in with
her party. With much spread and ceremony she
lays her cloak out over the seat in front, fingers
its fur edge, pats, strokes, gazes ; inspects her
gloves ; switches her skirts first one way, then the
other; finally, fastens her eyes on her jewelled
corsage. From beginning to end of the concert
she sits and contemplates the loveliness of that
little chiffon bodice. Only one thing in the hall
exists for her—the frock outside of herself. But
why pay four shillings to come and look at it in
publie?
The programme only ran off the usual Lieder-
tafel lines when it announced that “Roberto ” was
“Toi que j ’Odoro.” Otherwise, it was punctuated
with the usual type of orchestral numbers, and
choruses where rather less robust tenor voices than
of yore made themselves heard. Madam Helen
Roze was the only lady-warbler. She hails from
the Carl Rosa Co., and sang with much culture
and an agreeable quality of voice. Perhaps she
would have made more of a hit if her personal
appearance had been more becomingly accen-
tuated. Mr, Richards, the male soloist of the
evening, was a little man with nerves and huski-
ness. M. Stael’s violin solos were entirely de-
lightful, finished, and thrilling. It seems that
this fine fiddler is getting into his work more and
more of the warmth one has sometimes missed.
A delightful fete last week was the Old High-
School Girls’ re-union. For this, the big, bare
building went into ecstasies of Chinese lanterns
and laughter, and decorations artfully turned out
of palms and waste-paper baskets. Mrs. Garvan,
the head-mistress, received the several hundreds
of mostly fresh young guests. Looking round, it
seemed as if the Old Girls, who turned up in large
are not to be comprehended by that
term. Ethel Turner was there in a white silk
frock with pink satin sleeves. Florence Mack
warbled. Miss Mercy Murray, in clinging yellow,
recited charmingly some of “The Lady of Lyons,”
which shows that school-room recitations are alto-
gether reaching up.
Ada Evans, the much-talked-of Sydney grad,
who is now going pluckily through our law-
schools, is an old High School girl. In appear-
ance she is ; a fragile-looking little woman, with
naturally.-fnzzy hair, a delicate complexion, and
bright, vivacious eyes. At school she was thought
very pretty—that may help her if ever she gets a
jury but not the sort to blossom (or wither) into
a lawyer.
A rest and luncheon room for shop-girls was
opened on Monday afternoon by Mr. James Fairfax,
supplemented by a few words from the Rev. Carr-
Snnth. A bright, sunny room in the new markets
is the chosen haunt. Working-girls, who now have
to take their bread and tea among the cockroaches
at the back of their ‘ ‘ establishments, ” may have
a quiet hour here, with white tables and chairs
and magazines. Tea for a penny is the special
feature. The girls may bring their own lunohes
if they like, and it is actually whispered that there
won’t be any attempt to cultivate, reform, or in
any way interfere with the privacy of the indi-
vidual with needle-worn fingers who likes to wash
down the gilded doings of noveletted duchesses
with her pen’orth of tea.
Next Friday, April 28, is the date fixed for the
French ball. It comes off at the Paddington
Town Hall under V.R. patronage, and ought to
be a brilliant success.
One of the prettiest brides seen in Sydney for a
long time was Miss Nina Church, married last
week to Mr. Ernest Wilshire, S.M. The wedding
came off on Wednesday afternoon at Christ
Church, and created quite a stir up Haymarket
way. The charming bride-—and the first word
isn’t used simply because it is followed by the
the second, as this bride is a black-haired, blue-
eyed beauty with a profile—was arrayed in decol-
lete white duchesse, draped with real lace, no
orange blossoms, but plumes in the hair and a veil
falling backwards. Some tiny boys in black satin
page suits, and tinier girls in white muslin frocks,
followed as bridesmaids and pages. After a brisk
interval of speeches, Mr. and Mrs. Wilshire went
qff on a secret honeymoon which is to continue
itself later at The Australia for a few weeks. The
bride’s going-away dress was a grey coat and skirt,
and braidfed heliotrope vest.
The history of one young couple, who were mar-
ried last month at Randwick Presbyterian Church,
was spiced with romance by “ Tattersall.” They
became engaged last year, but had little or no
prospect of being speedily wed. In October,
however, they approached “ Tattersall ” with a
modest 55., and drew the winning horse in a Cup
sweep. Their investment netted £4OOO, with
which tidy sum they have set up housekeeping.
Yet another naval engagement. This affair
belongs to the daughter of a lately-deceased soli-
citor and a smart young officer. Most society
girls declare that naval men are nice to talk to,
charming to dance with, but to marry —oh, dear
no ! Too much away from home, too little money.
But, as someone once sapiently observed some-
where, “asking makes all the difference;” and
the name of rejected British officers is certainly
not legion. When, as in this case, the bride-elect
is well dowered, the latter objection does not hold.
A quiet little wedding that came off last week,
at St. Augustine’s, Neutral Bay, was that of Miss
Mayne, daughter of Alec. Mayne, the popular
theatrical manager, and Mr. Harry Martell, of
Melbourne. The bride wore white muslin and a
white hat, and her two pretty sisters in pink
muslin and pink hats followed as bridesmaids.
Emily Soldene’s farewell matinee comes off at
the Criterion Theatre on Tuesday, May 2. The
snappy programme includes Rignold, Mr. and
Mrs. Brough, Phil Newbury, and the Soldene
herself as Serpolette in “Les Cloches de Corne-
ville. ”
Pattie Browne went off to Melbourne last week,
to receive a benefit matinee, given to her by Mr.
Harry Rickards at his own theatre. This does not
look like a quarrel between Rickards and Pattie.
In fact, I have it from the only Harry himself
that there never was the slightest ground for any
such rumors.
Coining over the water lately a lively member
of an imported co. shared her cabin with a lady
missionary. The missionary bore in silence for a
long time the peculiarities of the latter’s toilette,
but one day she couldn’t help herself and broke
out, “Oh, my dear, let me beseech you, do give
up that dreadful paint. If I used it / should be
as beautiful as you are, but you see I wouldn’t. ”
“Why don’t you,” said the frisky one, “Gawd
must know you need it. Come, do have a loan of
my rouge-pot! ”
The fact that Mr. James Arthur Pollock won
his appointment as Professor of Physics after
“open competition with the world” speaks
volumes in favor of the Sydney born and bred
fraduate, and of the educational power of the
Iniversity. Prof. Pollock will, undoubtedly,
receive an ovation on Commemoration Day. Mr.
Henderson, who is filling Prof. Wood’s position
during the latter’s 12 months’ leave of absence, has
had the title of Acting Professor conferred upon
him for that period. There are rumors of another
appointment to be made at the University, but
whether this is only idle rumor or a foreshadowed
fact it is yet too soon -to say.
Other appointments lately made in connection
with science and engineering at the University are
those of Mr. Basil Turner, A.R.S.M., as lecturer
for one year in metallurgy ; and Mr. T. Brearley,
B.Sc., as senior demonstrator (under Prof. Pol-
lock) and Mr. G. Harker, 8.E., as junior demon-
strator. Mr. Brearley is, perhaps, most widely
known at the University in connection with the
dramatic society of which he has always been a
conspicuous member. Mr. Harker is a promising
young scientist, whose sister also won high laurels
at Sydney University ; they are closely related to
the “Money-Millers,” of Melbourne. Mr. Basil
Turner, as his degree implies, has lately arrived
from England.
Sydney University is' very proud of its medical
professors. _ Dr. Anderson Stuart is quoted as an
authority in various works of medicine in addi-
tion to being at the head of “My Medical School.”
Dr. Has well is part-author of a work on Zoology
widely used as a text-book: while Dr. Wilson is
splendidly educated and perhaps the inost broadly
scientific of them all. But this, it seems, is not
enough. Their wives must be learned us well.
Mrs. Haswell (a Maorilander) is also a B.Sc., and
now it is said that Mrs. Wilson (nee Miss Mabel
Salomons) is entering upon the three years’ Science
course at the University. Mrs. Wilson always
had a leaning for the higher education and it was
while studying anatomy that she met her future
lord. . Mrs. David also takes a thorough and tech-
nical interest in her husband’s scientific pursuits.
The many friends of M. L4on Caron are work-
ing up a testimonial matinee for this deserving
director, who is going away, like a good French-
man, to see his mother at Boulogne. The date of
the matinee is fixed for April 28.
The latest news of the Fancy Fair in aid of the
Consumptive Home fund is that the fair will open
on Thursday, August 10th, at the Exhibition
Building.
Some newly-arrived pieces of music are : Jones’
second Barn Dance (Paling and Co.), which bears
no visible relationship to the other Barn Dance
and comes on the scene a little late, as the Barn
Dance is commonly supposed to be dead; “Love
of My Life, ’tis You,” with words and music by
Charles Kenmngham (Allan and Co.), a simple
uneventful little ballad that does hot try to be
flashy, and owes much to Pat Bathurst, who sings
it m the “ Geisha the “May Queen Waltz,” by
Violet Guiseppe Truda (Palingand Co.), a change-
u , rr } easure with a lot of con sentimento about it
which ought to make it and its' waltzers go well in
a ball-room.
I have also received a little book called the
Household Manual,” by Mrs. Theo. Winning
from Moree. Something fresh and wholesome
attracts one to this nice little publication ; it
seems to have got the gum-trees into its hints
and recipes. . Someone who has tried them tells
me that the jams and preserves are especially ex-
cellent. The book isn’t all recipes—it covers the
ground from Mutton Broth and “What to Do
till the Doctor Comes,” to “How to Look Your
Best at a Ball.” I filch the latter for you. “ Take
a warm bath with a little ammonia in it, drink a
cup of hot milk, and lie down for an hour before
dressing. Just before starting take a spoonful of
sal volatile.”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Amy Castles, whose Tuesday concert drew a
second crowd and who was applauded and
blarneyed to the top of her bent ivhen she sang at
ot. Vincents Fair, is a product of Carlton (where
the fool ‘ beaks ’ come from), transplanted to
Bendigo, four years ago. In private life the
warbler is a naive, countryfied chit. On the eve
of her Town Hall debut, she seemed chiefly con-
cerned lest her feet should look big in the white
shoes prescribed for the occasion by a feminine
board of advice. Ma Castles, one hears, wastes no
love on certain doubting Thomases of the
Metropolis, who persistently credit the 17-year-old
Amy aforesaid with having tasted her 20th birth-
day-cake. ‘Things are come to a pretty pass,’
complams the fond parent in question, ‘ when
11 i} lts °f boys ” down-face a mother about the age
of her own offspring ! ’
. “Amy Castles was ‘discovered’ in the first
instance by a Melbourne lady during a recent
visit to Bendigo, where the damsel’s voice has
been asserting itself, more or less, for the past five
years.. An elder, or younger, sister also had a
big voice until she warbled the bulk of it away.
The Melb. lady, reckoning that she could work a
boom for Amy, made overtures to the girl’s
parents, toqk her to Melbourne, rigged her out in
appropriate style, and entered her for an ‘ under
21 ’ musical scholarship, in which she was
understood to finish second. A boy violinist, Foley
Westwood, got the scholarship. Then the entre-
preneuse introduced her protegee to the Austral
Salon crowd, who took her up on the understand-
ing that they were discovering her. Much para-
graphing followed, with the result that the indus-
trious real discoverer thought she couldn’t go
wrong in running her little Amy at the Town
Hall. She didn’t go wrong, for (with her co-
guarantors, if any) she takes a half share of the
handsome profits. The story of how Amy Castles—
a singer of some years’ practice—came to be sprung
upon Melbourne as a 17-year-old novice is very
interesting.
“Pianist Muller, another prodigy of the mo-
ment, played at the Austral Salon (Monday, April
10th). Said Fritz is a small-sized, 11-year-old,
with wistful, elderly face, and straight, thickly-
growing hair, worn longish. As a juvenile
exquisite, he is not to be compared with the
Salon’s former musical protege, blue-eyed Grainger
of the golden curls, snowy collar and Eton jacket.
Instead of discussing Art in the abstract with
Salomtes of all ages, after the manner of the ele-
gant Percy G. in his day, little Fritz, in the
intervals of his performance, steadily ate cakes
and skipped round the rooms with a girl of his
own size, in the wake of the lump-sugar. To the
programme the urchin contributed much Schubert
and Chopin, all without the score and rendered
with feeling, though his tiny hands handicapped
him somewhat as an executant.
That was an extra-festive Salon gathering, by
the way. Lovely flowers, tea scented like a nose-
gay,. professional entertainers. Ida Osborne,
smiling as ever in blue suit and toque, surpassed
herself in IVta Babbie, ’ that motherly, hush-a-bye
croon of some soft, fat, caressing negress. Bertie
Zelman fiddled, the hostess’s pretty daughter
sang, elocutionists Osmond and Morris gave
bright, unhackneyed recitations. The hostess
that afternoon was Mrs. Horace Wills, formerly
of Springsure district (C.Q.). Hubby Horace
Wills and his brother Cedric were boys at school
in England when their father, with 17 others, was
murdered by the blacks on a station near Spring-
sure. ‘ Murder Scrub ’ souvenirs the tragedy
thereabouts to this day ; and also apropos thereof,
more than one sane, sober old resident of that
district swears to a phantom blackfellow, said to
ride Springsure road at certain times of the year.
“The wedding of the week (April 12th) was
that of Dean Vance’s daughter Ruby to Dr.
Charlie Alsop. The victims made an ideal couple.
She—fair, plump, petite and smiling. He—tall,
dark, slight and perturbed. Clerical circles get
well initiated into ecclesiastical pomps and cere-
monies, and never was a marriage celebrated more
‘ decently and in order ’ than this particular one.
The portly Dean led his daughter to the sacrifice
m the usual white satin, court train, transparent
sleeves and chemisette, pearl necklet, &c. Bishop
Coe, in fatherly tones, joined the young things in
the bonds. Five pretty maids gowned in white
silk, hatted with violet velvet, and poised with
purple orchids and autumn leaves, stood in grace-
ful attitudes and saw that not a fold of the bride’s
train fell ungracefully. The Cathedral interior
looked its best, choristers sang, organ played,
chimes rang out, presents were plentiful, all
parental authorities delighted. ‘lf that’s not
bliss, what is ? ’
“ Orating recently at the National Mutual Life
meeting, Chief Justice Madden (where doesn’t he
orate !) said that every wealthy person he knew
personally 21 years ago was either in the Benevo-
lent Asylum or had died in the odor of poverty.
At that long-past time, Jack Madden was just be-
ginning to get a foothold in law, but a few years
previously, when his degree was newly-fledged, he
fell under the ban of a Melb. matron who had
jumped to the erroneous conclusion that the pro-
spective C. J. was matrimonially-inclined towards
her daughter. The old lady freely denounced
Madden as a man ‘ with nothing in him, ’ and fore-
told that he would never make his way in the
world —being thus much beside the mark in the
latter statement, however accurately she may have
hit it in the former. The damsel, bundled off to
another province, made, financially, a much better
match, and was afterwards very near catching a
Premier for a second husband.
“ The ‘ Belle of New York ’ goes on her way re-
joicing rather more than heretofore. She might
show her lovely teeth oftener than she does, with-
out giving spectators just cause to call her ‘proud.’
A domestic and etiquette encyclopaedia, revered
by our mothers in their youth, says : ‘lf your
teeth are fine, unostentatiously turn the conversa-
tion into sprightly channels ; if otherwise, the
serious rdle will prove more suitable to your style.’
“Belle Bucklin and most of the N’Yorkers
seem dentally gifted. S’pect their pearls groxved,
though such wonders are told of American den-
tists. Over thar, ’tis said, if a tootsie is the teeni-
est mite irregular, you part with him in the sure
and certain hope of a-glorious artificial substitute.
On this side, Nature as an ivory-manufacturer
still makes hacks of the field.
Ihe most pathetic incident of the Service
funeral was the utter collapse of poor old Duncan
Gillies. The hero of the boom-time has always
been regarded, even by his warmest supporters, as
a coldblooded, unsympathetic person ; but when
the body of bis old colleague disappeared into the
grave, the Gillies that nobody had seen before was
revealed. People approached to sympathise, but
the old man would have none of them, and walked
off by himself with his head bowed and tears
streaming down his cheeks.
The Loretto Convent, at Ballarat, has been
housing a German Countess for the past few
months. The lady is young, charming, wealthy,
and travels with an elderly duenna. Having
letters of introduction to the'Superior of the Con-
vent, the Countess looked her up, took a fancy to
the Convent, and elected to pass a few months in
the cloister. She even studied for matric., with
the idea of taking a degree at Melb. University,
but changed her mind and left a week or two ago
for the land of lager, intending to rush back to
Loretto nunnery ere the end of the year. Many
things, however, may happen to curb the Coun-
tess’s piety before Dec. 31.
V ours affectionately,

The late Archdeacon Stretch, of Mel-
bourne, was not the father of Bishop “Jack”
Stretch, as many people supposed. “ Jack ” will
address the venerable octogenarian as Uncle when
they meet in the place where the good parsons go.

Melb. Argus lately recorded the death of
one Louis Bilstead, and went on to mention with
becoming awe that deceased had in his youth
attended a Copenhagen school whereat the present
king of Greece was also a pupil. After which it
is sad to know that poor Bilstead’s remains were
fossicked out by a common milk-boy.
“ Bill ” Forrest, eldest brother of W.A.
Premier Forrest, who was jammed to death be-
tween two timber-trucks last week, had little in
common with his brothers. He lived at Bunbury,
followed bucolic pursuits, and was unknown to
politics. Occasionally he visited Perth, when he
made things merry. He owned large stations in
the North-West.
James Martin, M.L.C. (S.A.), who com-
pletes his 78th year on St. George’s Day (April 23),
opened a one-man blacksmith’s shop at Gawler 51
years ago ; and now on the same spot he has
factories employing, even in slack seasons, 500 men.
His evil boast is that he never paid for an
advertisement in his business career. He has had
heaps of free advts., however, and here is another.
In a recent interview, the possibility of
death was mentioned to “ General” Booth by an
inquisitive pressman. The old warrior remarked
that, in case of his death, a cable would, in the
course of a few hours, announce his successor —a
matter which had been arranged some time ago.
. . . Note the air of absolute monarchy in this.
Hereditary kingship or popedom ; right divine for
the Booth family; no absurd nonsense about
popular election or democratic rights among the
blood-and-fire warriors.
“ O. J.” : First met Medor (who ran amok
in Melb. Eastern Market) 18 years ago, in the
National Hotel, Bourke-street, Melb. He wanted
to work my bumps. Wouldn’t permit it; but
paid the fee (half-a-crown). Medor asked me why
I objected to be examined. Told him that, if
there was any truth in his science, I was a scoun-
drel ; if there wasn’t, there was still some evi-
dence in that direction. He handed back the
half-crown: “You know more than I,” he ob-
served, sadly. “ Let us have a drink.”
Peter Phillips, who for nearly 20 years
has been sec. of Bendigo Miners’ Association, and
the most prominent man in the crowd, has re-
signed his office, alleging that for three or four
years past friction between other executive offi-
cers and himself has been increasing until it has
become intolerable. But, after an almost even
contest, the committee has elected him to hold
office till the annual appeal to the members ; and
then it is more than likely that the old one-armed
battler will triumph again.
A reporter was interviewing “ General ”
Booth three years ago in Perth (W.A.), re the
proposed dumping down in that province of
several thousand English ex-oonvicts. Scribe
asked: “Stripped of the military title you have
given your following, what would you call them?”
“A Mosaic democracy,” was the answer. The
reporter looked at the Hebraic features of the
General, and remarked, “ Then you are Moses? ”
“I am,” was the calm reply. And it is true;
with only this difference, that whereas the other
Moses destroyed the golden calf, Moses Booth
embraces it.
“T.”: Be “Locust’s” paragraph on the
good times Tyson might have had. Stayed
at Woorooma once with squatter Loeky
M‘Bean, who had been expressing a desire
to travel and see things generally. Said
the writer : “ Why don’t you do it ? Your
money is no good to you—your’re living
here like a blackfellow.” “Eh, mon, awin
too auld, there’s the gettin’ tackets, an’
lookin’ after yer luggage, an’ arthot.”
“ Get some smart young fellow who
wants to see the world ; he’ll attend to get-
ting the tickets, and so on—and you can
no doubt get one at a very small salary.”
The old boy listened to it all, then laid a
shaking finger emphatically on the palm of
his outstretched hand and replied : “ Aye, and
pay his expanses, mon ! ”
Hosie, the recently-dead Melb. caterer,
started business in the long ago with big, fat,
highly-seasoned mutton pies, for which he charged
3d., and as the great Australian bank-note-sand-
wich-and-champagne era was then only just dying
out, the amazing cheapness of Hosie’s pies struck
the popular imagination a big advertising blow in
its stomach. Everybody ate Hosie’s pies. Eminent
counsel even came into court with crumbs of Hosie
clinging to their whiskers, and stains of mutton-
fat on their briefs. Hosie’s pies were the first
indication that the Melbourne “ d—n the change ”
era had passed, and that the despised tray-bit had
re-acquired an appreciable value.
Rev. Dr. Lang, who is to be com-
memorated in Brisbane for his herculean
immigration labors of the early days, had
three ruling ideas which he rung-in on
almost every theme he discussed. One of
these was antipathy to Sydney Govt.
House. Fitzroy, the first Australian
Governor-General, lived there then, and
one of Lang’s many allusions to the place
was—“ There is no slum in London that
stands more urgently in need of a thorough
clean-out.” Another ruling idea was that
more Scotch immigrants should be im-
ported to counteract the Irish, as he be-
lieved there was a conspiracy to make
Australia an appanage of Rome. That was
why he chartered the Fortitude for Bris-
bane. Third, he believed Australia should
cut the painter from Britain and start at
once as a Republic. He emphasised these
several beliefs with the energetic banging
of a fat umbrella.
Julius Vogel, the man of many
vicissitudes, was particularly fortunate in
his domestic relations. His wife, a lovely
blonde in her youth, and a woman of regal
beauty in middle life, was thoroughly de-
voted to him, and during his gout-stricken
years of physical helplessness attend d on
him with untiring watchfulness. Before
Vogel attained to the Premiership he was
regarded in official society at Wellington
as a somewhat under-bred adventurer, and
his young wife did not, in consequence,
receive the recognition which Vogel con-
sidered her due. The great social panjan-
drum of the windy Maorijand metropolis in
those days was the wife of the senior
puisne Judge, and she had taken a lead-
ing part in—well, not encouraging the
Vogels. But when Julius became the
popular Premier, whose Public Works
policy was to give the Midas’ touch to
Maoriland, he had his revenge. The Chief
Justice, Arney, dying, the senior puisne
naturally expected the post, to which his
position and his commanding ability cer-
tainly entitled him. His friends danced
attendance on the Vogels for weeks, and
much mud was eaten to propitiate the
Premier, but eventually the vacancy was
conferred on Prendergast, a comparative
mediocrity.
David Syme is probably less sus-
ceptible to humor than any other man on
the Australian press. David rarely smiles,
and when he does it is merely a physical
effort, a spasmodic contraction of the mus-
cles of one side of the face. Nearly all the
members of his staff have at one time or
other had a go at the alleged humorous
columns in his publications, but the effort
to satisfy David’s sense of humor is like
feeling out in the dark for the bottom of
the abysmal void with an umbrella-handle.
The “Wise and Otherwise” department
in Saturday’s Age is personally edited by
David, and resembles a procession of leaden
tombstones in a barren land.
Captain Roger Turpie, who is being
lassooed, per Louis Becke, into the authors’pen in
London, should tell many thrilling stories of the
South Seas, as well as of the Australian coast in
the early days. Bor 50 years he cruised about
these quarters, retiring from time to time to his
cottage at Woollahra, Sydney. He was connected
with the John Williams missionary vessel for over
40 years—most of the time as chief officer.

WEABY DIOR: “ Well, I’ll be Mowed!“
Those Australians who wax wroth over the
alleged favoritism Shirley Baker showed the Ger-
mans in his days of Tongan power, and over Hig-
ginson, of New Caledonia, and M'Leod, of the
New Hebrides, taking oaths of allegiance to
France, should remember that Queen Victoria’s
second son, Edinburgh, similarly renounced his
allegiance to England, and that her third son
Connaught, announces himself as ready to do the
same.
The Bigsby gold medal for his Funafuti
achievements reached Prof. David, of Sydney
University, after much circumlocution. The Geo-
logical Society of London forwarded the medal to
N.S.W. Agent-General, who forwarded it to
N.S.W. Acting-Governor, who laid it on the
Executive Council table, thus forwarding it to the
Govt. Then Govt., represented by the Premier,
officially forwarded it to Minister Hogue, who
sent it to the Professor.

to go back and smoke in comfort in the Father-
land. Others remained here—among them Mr.
Muecke; for years past a director of the co.
There is technically no hangman in the
employment of Vic. Penal Department. He is
apparently not on the pay-list, and there does
not show in the expenditure column of the
balance-sheet any casual item such as “ strangula-
tion, <£s 17s. 6d.” But the man-choker is there,
nevertheless disguised in the designation of
“ Gaol Messenger ” at £lOO a year. The sheriff
would look foolish some fine morning if the
scragman should refuse to act, on the ground that
he is not a scragman, but a messenger.
Gov. Tennyson, in mentioning his interest
in Australia in his first .speech at Adelaide, does
not seem to have alluded to his uncle Horatio,
who spent some years in Tasmania. He was
(vide the biography of the poet) “ sent off to try
his fortune in Tasmania,” after wanting to be .a
soldier, and that career being disallowed by his
mother. Horatio is still living in the Isle of
Wight. But there is only this brief reference to
him in . the Tennyson Life, and Gov. Tennyson
drops him. Why ? One hopes that Horatio was
a good boy. Is anything known of him in Tas-
mania? - . .
When Rear-Admiral Pearson was captain
in charge of the gunnery-school at Whale Island,
Portsmouth (Eng.), he was known by the men as
“Doggo,” from the way in which he followed
them about and kept an eye on their behaviour.
One of his fads was to keep the grass on the island
as level as a billiard-table. Shortly after his ap-
pointment he forbade the wearing of hobnail boots
by anyone on the island; and later on he would
not allow boot-protectors to be worn, as they were
supposed to cut-up the grass. Disobedience to
this order meant a spell of “ten A ” for the cul-
prit. Even the solitary horse on the island was
shod with specially-made boots to prevent him
disturbing the sward.
“Old Jack”: Sullivan, the Maungatapu
murderer, was not shipped to America. Went to
England, and in London was so shadowed by ’tecs
that his life became unbearable. So he took, ship
for Melbourne, where he was promptly arrested
and brought before Call, P.M., on some conve-
nient charge. Saw the brute in Court. He had
dyed his hair and beard jet-black, which served to
give him away. He stated that he was hunted
wherever he went. Discharged, he made his way
to Wedderburn —where at one time he had kept a
grog-shanty, wherein, as may be supposed, bloody
work had oft been done. The Wedderburn people
wouldn’t sell him food or shelter, and when he fell
sick he lay in an old shed, where a compassionate
woman brought him victuals. When he left the
district he made for N.S.W., and finally landed in
Wagga or Gundagai, where he pegged out in
hospital.

Aboriginalities.
“A.JS.G.”: Knew a 14st. woman who
wasn’t afraid of the wildest bullock. Saw her
once in a small yard with one. Bullock charged
and charged, but she simply stepped aside, and
fetched him one over the nose with a waddy as he
passed. Then he sulked in a corner until she got
her gun. Killed and dressed him, and had car-
case caitud away. Also, once saw a N.S.W.
squatter fight a bullock with an axe. Bullock
charged ; man stepped aside and slung axe at
head. Axe stuck in jaw. Bullock rushed again ;
man got back, snatched axe out and waited for a
“knock-out.” Bullock came again, was chopped
behind the ear, and went under. Put that
squatter in an arena with a bull, and writer's
“ stuff ” wouldn’t go on coo. [The bull-fighting
of Spanish entertainment isn’t done with an axe,
and the squatter would find the toreador’s lance
as awkward a weapon as would Garcia the
squatter’s little chopper.— Ed. B.]

“ Heimsdal ” : How many of your readers
have eaten “ goanna ? ” A surveyor of my
acquaintance always cooked any he shot, and de-
clared the flesh butter than fowl. An erstwhile
Rylstono publican also would give half-a-crown
for a good fat one ; said he preferred it to any
other meat. Re “goanna’’-killing sheep. Have
seen them tackle a ewe which was down and
couldn’t rise ; and 1 believe they will go for any-
thing that can’t get away, even if it were an ele-
phant. II ive often seen them kill lambs and
half-grown hares, which they sneak up to when
asleep.
“ Eateu-Out ” : “ J.P.M.” (B. *25/3/’99)
says “he never found a twin in or out of kanga-
roo’s pouch.” Twin ’roos are certainly very rare,
nevertheless, I saw two joeys taken from one
mother a few years ago. Had several poddy ’roos
and added these to the number. They grew up
together, precisely alike in every respect, color,
marks, shape, size and sex (male). They left and
took hi the bush when about 10 months old. All
poddy ’roos do this at age of from 6 to 12 months
if given a free run. Only exception, to my
knowledge, was a female of the “blue flyer”
species, which always remained in the company of
the station horses. Came up to the yards with
them when run-in every morning, and would feed
about with them through the paddocks. Would
go under their bellies and round their heels, yet I
never saw one attempt to kick or bite her, and
there were some unbroken snorters amongst them

too. The ’roo once followed an old mare out with
a cart for 7 miles, got with a boundary rider’s
horses there, stayed with them for a week, and
then came home. The station dogs never inter-
fered with her, though a strange tyke coming
along often gave her a big run—but the “ flyer ”is
too speedy for the average bush dog—she never
came to any harm. A ’roo taken young is an easy
animal to poddy— except the wallaroo, which will
sulk, refuse milk, and clear out first chance. Also,
it is easy to teach a kangaroo to box. A poddy
takes to it quite naturally, and when a few months
old will shape up “on his own”—in fact, comes
round looking for fight. Uses only his “ hands,”
too, very rarely lifting a hind leg to rip, as in
tackling a dog.

“ Ben Sun ” : “ C.M.Gr.” says any female,
deprived of young, welcomes any means of reliev-
ing milk-pressure pain. Should have said “ most.”
Sheep and goats never mistake their young, and
will butt away the counterfeit, even when the pain
of milk-pressure must be excruciating.
* * * *
“ \Yerrimee ” : A selector, living on the
bank of a lagoon, had a merino ewe that used
regularly to swim across the water to feed on the
other side. When called, she would plunge in
and swim back. Have often noticed sheep swim-
ming round in a tank. Some say they do it to
cool themselves ; but as the merino, as a rule, is
chary even about wetting its feet, the dip is prob-
ably an accident. Being too thick-headed to
swim straight ahead for anywhere, the jumbuck
paddles about till it drowns. Old boundary-
riders will tell you “ they go in for a cooler and
the weight of the wet wool drowns ’em.”

They were an aged German couple occupy-
ing one of M.L.’s Government “small farms,”
and they came in to Wellington to complain to
somebody. Laid on to the right department,
they burst in on the chief, and the husband began
(without preliminaries) : “De stoads and de
veesels dot you put my farm to gill de rabbids mit,
dey live and breed mit dem in de same ’ole and let
de rabbids ead me up.” Then wife : “ Und’t der
veesels und’t der rrabidds und’t der stoads.” He
(taking a breather): “ I dry and geep young duck
and der veesels dake dem.” She (keeping up the
staccato): “ Und’t der rrabidds und’t der vee-
sels .” He (warming up): “I go send my poy
get egg ad nide, and a veesel bide his pluddy
finger off. Bud dey don’t bide der tam rabbids,
dey live vid ” She (sitting down on the type-
writer) : “ Und’t der stoads ” He (placing a
inanurey boot on a bundle of new Grown lands
reports): “I go oud mit my dog to chase rabbids
und he go into der ’ole, he come oud vid von eye
oud.” She (crying): “ Und’t der .” But here
a powerful messenger intervened, and, as the old
pair wouldn’t leave, they had to be assisted.
They have yet to interview Seddon, and if he
can’t stop the rabbits, etc., “living in de. same
’ole ” they are going to tackle Gov. Ranfurly.

“S.S.” : Know an old miner on the Tas-
manian field who had never heard of Smiley or
Mark Twain, but who had a pet frog which he
had reared from a small, flat, green orphan, about
the size of a sixpence, to a monstrous, warty
creature with the sage expression of a fat deacon
after a hearty dinner. The old man kept his
queer pet in the folds of a wet sugar-bag, and fed
him on flies and other insects. The frog would
hold a pipe in his mouth, put up his fore-paws as
if in prayer, or yawn when bidden. Some-
body bet the proprietor a pound that his frog
would not return to him if put in the dam at the
Chum; but it did, hopping into the crown of his
hat, and settling there in a fat, contented way.
The old man wondered could I tell him how long
frogs lived. “ I’m sorter’fraid he’ll die soon, an’
leave me lonely,” he said ; “I've tried to rear
others in case, you know, but Jim, here, he’s
jealous, an’ he always kills ’sd-”
Steele Rudd ”: Have likewise seen ’roo
swimming in a flood- —also dead cows and hay-
stacks and big logs, but none of these ever put in
a stroke. Reared in a district where ’roo was
thicker than the stars —where one could fringe his
clothes-line with the ears of those that died quietly
round the humpy, and where, sometimes, ’twas
necessary to build a big yard and fill it full of ’roos
and wade in for days with prop-sticks and pitch-
forks to lay out those that wouldn’t starve, but
never saw one swim. Have seen them drown
frequently, and once sa w five go down at one gulp
Don’t know anything about ’roos of other
provinces, but if they swim, how’s Queensland
going to federate ?

“Widdin”: “The monotony of the
bush ! ” Bandicoots ! Have been lost in it and
heard its weird voices ahead and followed them •
behind, and gone back ; above, and found them in
the friction of interwoven branches. Its startling
shapes have stood out in the gloaming and mocked
me; its soldier-ants have put periods to my re-
flects ; its snakes, if not in my boots, have been
around them; its spiders have crawled over me
and its ground leeches have fattened on me. In
storm, its falling timber has frightened me; in
calm, its stillness has awed me—but its monotony
has never meddled with me 1
PATTIE BROWNE (her latest photo.).
Photo., Alla, Strand, Sydney.
“Wallaroo”: Astonishing is the topo-
graphical ignoranceof N.S.W. bucolics. Carrying
in New England district lately, noticed that every
settlement pointed a directing finger-post at
Kangaroo Camp. Began asking distance thither,
and nearest I got to it was— “ Dunno ; but father
says it aint far.” This from a youth topping the
twenties. Was exasperated into asking one be-
whiskered young fellow if he had ever been out-
side his own paddock, and was gravely informed
that he had “been to Elsmore a few miles
away. Asked another bushie distance to next
township, and was told, after much consideration,
“ about nine miles.” Found it was 30 !

“ A.L.W.” : Any- reader know how cat-
fish’s (or jew-fish’s) nests are built? Numerous in
head-waters of Mary River, Q. The nest is a
perfect ring of stones (some weighing 51b.) averag-
ing 3ft. in diameter, embedded upon a submerged
sand-bank, no other stones, as a rule, being within
20 yards of a nest.
A wealthy turfite of many jamborees
was once ingeniously “played upon” by
his trainer. They had set out for a country
fixture with two good gallopers, and all
went well until the day before the meet.
Then the stately owner “broke out.”
Trainer and jockey knew that it would be
the same on the morrow : the boss would
make'a show of himself—the good thing
would be given away to all and sundry.
That night they carted him to bed, tacked
down the window blind, fixed a blanket
over the blind, so arranged the curtains as
to preclude the admission of any light what-
ever, left four big bottles of beer, a long
candle and matches on the table, annexed
his timepiece and departed. On awaking
next morning the sleeper fossicked round,
lit the candle, “ took a pull,” and relapsed
again. “Ditto, repeato,” during day.
Meanwhile, both his neddies had won, and
the trainer arrived just as the boss had got
downstairs to yell about his watch having
been stolen, and was being congratulated
all round. “What the is the meaning
of all this ! ” asked the bewildered owner.
“ Don’t know, sir. I was wondering why
I didn’t see you all day.” Classic language
passed on discovery of the trick, but, of
course, the trainer disclaimed all know-
ledge. “I was beginning to think they
had some d long nights in these parts,”
muttered the owner, as he made for the bar.

“ Timi ” : A couple of years ago
Mrs. Richards, wife pf a selector on
Buckingong station, near Narandera, N.S.
Wales, found a nest of wild duck, and
reared two of the ducklings until they
were 12 months old. They became very
tame, frequently coming to their mistress’s
window and tapping on the pane when in
want of food. Mrs. Richards presented
her pets to a Vic. squatter, who took them
home, clipped their wings, and they be-
came apparently quite reconciled to the
change of location. A few weeks ago Mrs.
Richards was awakened by a familiar noise
at her window; her ex-proteges, their
wings having grown, had flown back to
Buckingong, many hundred miles, after a
year’s absence. Correspondence with their
Vic. owner showed that six days before
their return to Buckingong the ducks had
been suddenly missed. These facts were
narrated to present writer by a recent
visitor to Buckingong, but he has unfor-
tunately forgotten the address of the Vic-
torian from whose homestead the ducks returned.

“Nitfc.”: Re Australian slang. “Ringer”
and “good iron ” are both derived from the game
of quoits ; a ringer being a quoit which rings the
peg, while “‘good iron ” corresponds to “good
ball” at cricket. “Straight wire” is obvious
enough, but not so “straight griffin.” Melbourne
man told me it came from the proverbial relia-
bility of the local detective sergeant Griffin, and
spread from the criminal class there to the
“ cronks ” elsewhere in Australia.

“ Ercildoune ” discovers a new blis-
ter :
Entomologists would delight in a certain little
W.A. specimen known as the “ cocktail.” The name
must be understood literally, for an intimate ac-
quaintance with the insect will at once show that
it is able to take its own part several times over. It
is always found in the neighbourhood of dead gums,
old stumps, &c., is about 3-16th of an inch long and
of variegated color, mainly peacock-blue ; its tail
is, however, a bright red and cocked-up, hence its
name. In defending itself it ejects a very blis-
tering secretion. 11 Cocktails ” can be gathered by
the quart, and might probably be utilised medici-
nally as Cantharides Occidentalis Australis.
* • * *
Harry Stockdale on aboriginal mar-
riage customs :
Strange indeed are the aborigines’ marital regu-
lations. A black will gather his friends and invade
the country of an enemy for the sole purpose of
stealing a bride ; and when he has got her, some-
times at life-risk, the party assisting him to obtain
the woman are rewarded by being the first recipi-
ents of her favors. This is custom, whether the
husband likes it or not, and is in vogue generally
throughout Australia. Women who are taken after a tribal
fight by the victors, as the spoils of war, have an uncom-
monly rough time, being the common property of the tribe
until such time as they are chosen by leading men of the
tribe or by some special fighter with whom none care to
dispute title. Unfortunately, not only aboriginal women
of other tribes, but white women have been on different
occasions waylaid and abducted, or obtained wandering
in the bush as survivors from wrecked vessels. These
women all had to undergo the usage of “tribal
custom ” as related above. It has always been
found a matter of great difficulty to effect the release
of a white woman once in the possession of the blacks.
There are authentic records of a number of white women
having been carried off by the blacks—from squatters’
wives and daughters down to women of the poorest
class. A remarkable instance of a white woman fall-
ing into the hands of the blacks, and ultimately getting
away, was that of a woman who escaped from a wreck at
Wide Bay, on the Q. coast, and was taken possession of
by a neighboring tribe. After enduring much hardship
and outrage, news as to her whereabouts reached the
settlement, where was a convict who had been for some
considerable time with this particular tribe. He was
despatched to rescue the woman, which he heroically did,
swimming rivers with her on his back. This woman took
THE SCORCHING “ NINETY MILE .”

long- to recover from the effects of her sojourn amongst
the Wide Bay natives, but eventually got well enough to
be shipped to England.

Apropos of aboriginal Jimmy Solomon s
breaking gaol at the Gilbertian township of Dan-
daloo, which has provoked some very common-
place comments in the press, The Bulletin rises
to remark that the love of liberty is the noblest
instinct implanted by Nature in the mind of man,
and no one who breaks gaol without violence
should be punished for it. Anyhow, the low-
comedy incident was laughed out of court, as the
offending blaekfellow explained that he had the
toothache, and had taken French-leave in order
to procure, in a paddock near by, some gohanna
gall—the aboriginal remedy for that complaint.
In the case of the aboriginal woman, aged 39, who
was going blind, and appealed to the N.S.W.
Aborigines Board for an allowance, let it be
remarked that the black native female of 39 is 10
years older than her white sister —and Heaven
help the white woman of 49 who is going blind
and has no friends to help her.

“ Centralian Kanga-
roo swims all right. Have
seen one, chased by dogs,
jump into a flooded creek
and swim across with ease.
Used to think a hen would
not take to water, but was
recently astonished to see
one walk deliberately into a
flooded gutter, get washed
off her legs, go under, come
up, do a wild splash, and
land on the side she was
making for. Anybody seen
a wallaby drinking? Camped
at a waterhole out-back dur-
ing a dry spell, and ob-
served that kangaroos, cock-
atoos and plain turkeys come
to water at sundown and get
away while it is still light.
Large pigeons always waited
till dusk before drinking
and small pigeons came at
sunrise, but never saw a
wallaby come to water all the
time, although they were
plentiful.

wide, and had tuft of fur remaining on it.
Full-grown hare, too.

The M‘Cooey expostulates :
Re Australian snakes and their mode of biting. Let
me say that there is no analogy between the English
viper and Australian snakes. No greater bosh was ever
written about the latter than appeared in last Nov.
Queensland Agricultural Gazette (official), although
from the pen of editor Boyd, F.R.G.S. The English
viper’s fangs are movable, and, when not in use, lie flat,
points backwards, being erected when the snake is about
to bite; whereas, in all Australian snakes—even in the
death adder (which is one of the viperidw)— the fangs are
permanently erected and immovable. There is, there-
fore, in the Australian snake no such pre-mordant erec-
tion of the fangs as Mr. Boyd refers to. The fang of an
Australian snake is firmly fixed in an anterior horizontal
jawbone, hinged to a shorter posterior bone ; and when
the snake is about to bite the anterior bone is pressed
down posteriorly, thus projecting the fangs forward, and
making them appear to be erectile. Mr. Boyd is also
wrong in saying that “ a snake does not bite —it strikes!”
The case is exactly the reverse ; and, if an Australian
snake didn’t bite and cling on with jaw-pressure, it
couldn’t force the poison from the glands, through the
fangs, into the wounds. The ignorance of the bushman
and the “ blackfeller ” is pardonable ; but Boyd, F.R.G.S.,
should know better.
* * * *
The natives of the Gilbert Islands have
petitioned the Commissioner to pre ventmissionaries
coming their way. Say they are quite content
—plenty to eat, both vegetables and human
nature. Also, that things socially are ripping
along most satisfactorily—no young men dying,
only the old ones ; who probably make good soup-
stock. There is something pathetic in their
appeal, and it will be a cruel thing if it prove
ineffective. For, without doubt, wherever the
missionary sets his foot, the old order changeth.
And the young men die quite as much as the old,
and laughter and song are heard no more.
As most people know, caoutchouc is obtained
from what is called the india-rubber tree, by
piercing through the sap-ring and collecting the
milky discharge in gourds. The visoid fluid is
then boiled in pans, and the residue, rolled into
handy-sized balls, becomes the raw rubber of
commerce. Last week a Sydney importer, upon
cutting open a portion of a consignment (from
Papua) of such india-rubber balls, foundnoless than
two out of 12, or about 16 per cent., were simply
handfuls of wood-chips with a caoutchouc coating
—valueless rubbish which had been dipped into
the pans and then rolled to resemble the solid
balls of india-rubber for which full market price
had been paid. The missionaryised nigger has
just enough nous to add the white man’s vices to
his own, and treat the combination as a business
asset.

Generally believed that the elastic Law of
Victoria was again stretched to an unlawful
extent, the other day, when the Full Court was
cleared of public and press, to admit of certain
financial matters being discussed in camera.
Johnny Wallace, M.L.C., gave rise to this extra-
ordinary proceeding. Truculent John is under
the Chief Justice’s order to retire from the
management of millionaire Monahan’s estate, and
the chief clerk has named the Equity Trustees
Co., as a fit and proper substitute for John afore-
said. The angry old man objected to the appoint-
ment of the co. named, on the ground that
barrister Higgins, who fought for the winning
side in the original law-suit, is a director of
said co., and will be unconsciously biassed
against him a ridiculous objection, seeing
that Wallace himself has no claim upon the
property they are wrangling about. Failing in
this objection, Wallace appealed to the Full Court
and embellished his appeal with a suggestion,
seemingly, that one of the other Melbourne trus-
tee companies, in which there is no Higgins con-
cerned, would be safer than the E.T.E. and A.
Co. The idea of having the facts and figures of
sundry financial concerns discussed before the Boy
(i.e ., the Public) was appalling to Judge Williams,
a’Beckett, and Hodges, so they decided to keep
the arguments dark. Whereby they have possibly
given an opening for Johnny Wallace to appeal to
the Privy Council against the finding of an irregu-
lar Full Court. At present, Johnny is dismissed
with costs, much to the disgust of a hiccupy ad-
mirer, who assured their Honors that it would be
all right if they put back the old ’un as trustee.
No finer combination of cricketers than the
present Australian XI has ever visited England, says
Ranji. Their bowling displays pace, method, and variety.
Much the same compliment might be paid to the London
and American Supply Stores (Melbourne). No finer com-
bination than London and American styles can be found.
The pace at which we (the L. and A. S.S.) execute orders
is wonderful ; our methods are rich in craftmanship, and
the variety of excellencies, quality, reasonableness of
price, &c., is a matter of common talk. Write for cata-
logue to London and American Supply Stores, Bourke-st.,

through the nose to keep the wool-trade of the Riverina
district out of its natural channels. The Anti-Federalists
(who all pretend to favor “ Federation on fair lines”) are
quite content for the province at large to lose thousands
annually in differential rates as long as Sydney pro-
fits by the swindle and Federation is kept at bay.

dime at the leading agricultural shows of Great Britain. He is to get such produce admitted to France at
minimum rates, to open up new markets in the great provincial centres of England, and to bring about an
improvement in the methods of handling and selling Australian products in London. Curious duties these
for the leader of the colonial Bar.’” _ 5 - -

THE “GOANNA” CURE.
“ Jimmy Solomon, an aboriginal, was in
Dandaloo lockup charged with some offence, and
left it without permission to hunt for an iguana’s
gall to cure his toothache. Having found this, he
returned to custody next day, and was pro-
ceeded against for having
been illegally at large.”
Next time you have tooth-
ache try this and send
result to Bulletin
office.

COL. NEILD UNDER OPEN ARREST.
“ ‘ Open arrest,’ in general terms, means that
the officer so placed must not appear at any pub-
lic function or place of amusement, and that he must not present
himself at the barracks or its precincts without uniform, a so,
that until relieved from the embargo he must not wear eithei
sash, belt, or sword.” . ,
The Con.: “ They have taken my sword, but, thank heaven,- 1 still have my,
Jawbone and my pen !” (

The death at Devonport (Tas.) of ex-Dis-
trict Constable Quinlan recalls that the nona-
genarian always maintained that the wrong man
was hanged for a certain old-time murder. At
Kitty’s Corner, near the Black Tier, in Oatlands
district, a settler named George Sturgeon was
murdered. Richard Innis, alias Long Mickey
who was “wanted’’for a minor offence and long
eluded the police, was eventually captured, made
to fit the crime, and died protesting his innocence
Years afterwards Quinlan, who took a prominent
part in the capture of Long Mickey, stumbled
across a bit of evidence which convinced him of
the innocence of his prisoner and fixed the guilt
on another person. But the evidence, though con-
clusive enough for Quinlan, was, owing to the
peculiar administration of Y.D.L. law in those
days, hardly good enough to establish a sure coh-
viction. Also, proceedings would have involved
a number of innocent persons, so Quinlan held his
hand. In his very old age his tongue worked
loose, and he declared to cronies that he knew
Long Mickey was innocent of murder. “ But ’tis
along time ( since the throuble,” he always said
“and there’s wheels within wheels, so we’ll lave
it at that.” And, so far as writer knows the
secret of the crime is Quinlan’s still.

horse-owners, and bowlers ; the latter simply be-
came enthusiastic in their praise of Zetz Spa Water
and maintain had they butanother week on “Zetz”

Though carefully nursed, the Miners’
Association, membership of which was formerly
compulsory upon all men working in the Broken
Hid mines, has never recovered the knock-out of
the ’92 strike. Its membership is never disclosed
now, and all its proceedings are strictly secret ■
but it is announced that last year three of its
members were killed through mine accidents. As
there were 19 fatalities altogether, the proportion
of unionists doesn’t seem to be satisfactory or
encouraging. For the association is better now
than it was ; apart altogether from the money
benefits provided, its rules have been specially
drafted to prevent ill-considered strikes.

Business, Robbery, etc-
Nothing like official correspondence for arriving
at the truth. A Vic. country resident sent to certain
friends a crate of fowls, which had to be transferred at
Swanston-street station to another train after a four-
hours’ wait. On arrival at their destination all were
found to be dead. In reply to official enquiry, the guard
of first train advised that he had watered the fowls on the
journey, and handed them over fresh and well at the
terminus. Guard in charge of waiting-room reported—
“ Fowls here four hours—well watered and fed, and sent
away in good order.” The third guard was quite unable
to account for the fowls arriving dead, except that it was
a terrible hot day. Upon which the Commissioner writes
to consignor, regretting that “he is unable to account for
the fowls arriving dead, as every care and attention was
given them during transit.” He received this reply:—
“Am quite able to understand the circumstances, as the
fowls were killed and bled before being sent away.”
* * * *
A well-known Sydney business-man used to
keep in his desk a small Bible which a J.P. friend of his
found handy for casual affidavits. One day the J.P.
came along with an intending deponent, and “George”
carelessly handed out, as he thought, the Bible. But
after the volume had been duly mumbled over, and the
others had departed, “ George ” found that the book was
a copy of—Don Juan ! “Nevermind,” he said to him-
self as soon as he had recovered from his consternation—
“it’s a good book in itself, anyhow, and not half as
* blue ’ as the Old Testament.”

Wire from Wyalong to Sydney warehouse,
signed by drummer : “No hope here. Six travellers in
front of me. What shall I do?” Reply: “Keep going
like blazes. Twenty travellers behind you.”

“P.A.”: Watch wouldn’t go; took it into a
gorgeous shop with £2OOO worth of jewellery and dia-
monds in the window. “Ah ! very dirty—seven-and-six.”
“ I had it cleaned a month ago for four and-six.”
“ That’s it; too cheap; must have it done properly.” I
tried another Aladdin’s palace. “Yes,” said the man
with the dice-box in his eye, “ there’s a lot of long hairs
in it wound around the safety-valve ; seven-and-six.”
“Then it wants a barber,” I replied; “ what do you ad-
vise—hair-cut or shave?” He looked at me in a pity-
ing, superior way, and I went further on till I came to a
shop with two Waterburys, a card of brass wateb-keys,
and a sheet of fly-paper in the window. I thought,
“ This maq looks poor enough to be honest.” “No ; I
can’t see nothing wrong.” “Not dirty?” “No!” “Not
got long hairs wrapped around the ball-bearings? ” “No ;
it wants a drop of hoil on the linch-pin is all I can see.”
The oil went on, and she started like a 20-head stamp
battery. “How much?” “Sixpence.” “ Have a drink ?”
“No, sir; never tetch it. Good-day, sir. Thank you.”
Now, is honesty the best policy? It may be, but appa-
rently the holder of that policy will have more dead flies
than diamond-rings in his window.
* ♦ * *
A Melb. undertaker’s way (per printed circular)
of calling upon clients to pay up : —“ street, Mel-
bourne, and all suburbs. January 2, 1899. Dear friend, —
Courtesy prompts us to wish you 1 the Compliments of
the season,’ which we heartily do, and hope that the
incoming year may bring every prosperity to you and
yours, and keep and leave you all well in health', full in
purse, and free from debt. At the same time, we feel-
ingly sympathise and condole with you on the hard times
we have all passed through, and we are convinced that
nothing but the direst misfortune could compel you to
let such an account as that for funeral expenses—per-
formed by us for you (and with which you kindly ex-
pressed your satisfaction) —remain so long unsettled. We
trust that with improving times you will now be able to
instruct us to write off the balance, or at least some por-
tion of it. For a settlement we are willing to assist by
making a liberal allowance. We will be glad to acknow-
ledge your kind recommendations either by cash pay-
ments or by reducing the standing account. If, however,
it may strike you that the account should, by effluxion of
time, have paid itself, see us and obtain a clearance, as
we have no wish to quarrel with you, or to let an old and
a bad debt stand in the way of our doing any business it
may be your misfortune to'require in the future.—Yours
respectfully, Smith and Sons, High-class Economic Under-
takers.”
* * * *
A certain Sydney business-man rarely sends out
a debt-collector. Should a client be dilatory in dealing
with “account rendered,” the smart trader will let
him slide for a while, and then, after adding from £1 to
£5 (according to original amount), send in account
again with a polite note hoping that no harsh measures
will be necessary. As to how it works, hear the wily one
himself: “ Veil, I always preddy soon gids a rebly ora
call from de party, mit hayment for der first account and
a brotest about der monish I added. Then I take der
dollahs and apologises for mein clerk’s error. Ha ! ha ! ”
* * » *
Much in evidence lately in Sydney
is the lydy who has just dropped her last 6d. down a
street grating, and asks a passing boy, “ whose eyes are
sharper than hers,” if he can see the coin. When boy
stops other boys stop, and crowd soon gathers, out of
which some benevolent old gent is pretty sure to bestow
upon the bereaved one the equivalent of the never-lost
coin. Saw one dame recently play the game twice
within an hour, and when a policeman eventually moved
her on with the remark that “ the ole gent gave you
sixpence,” she indignantly replied—“ Yes; but that
wasn’t the one I lorst.” A woman always has an answer,
of some kind. Even for a policeman.
* * * *
No less than seven leading hotelkeepers filed
their schedules in Perth the other week. There is scarcely
a solvent publican in the place.
* * * *
The latest dodge-joke (an old trick revived). A
smart young man waits upon an unsuspecting house-
holder and represents himself as travelling for a firm of
tailors. He then solicits an order for a suit of clothes.
The bagman’s eloquence overpowers all objections, and
an order, say, for a pair of trousers is given. Sample of
cloth selected, deposit of ss. paid, and scientific measure-
ment laboriously effected. If the victim is corpulent, the
traveller perhaps complains that his firm will be a loser
in cloth by the transaction. Finally, the tailor’s client
is told that the goods will be ready on such a date at such
an address. He calls per arrangement, to find the pre-
mises long occupied by an undertaker. This “ fake ” has
of late been much worked around Sydney.
* * * *
The other day in a Vic. criminal case it trans-
pired that accused had pawned articles in an assumed
name. Then the sapient Justice Hodges, who never
pawned a thing in his life, made this assertion to the
jury .-—“The pawning of any article in an assumed name
is a certain indication of dishonesty.” How little the
average Judge seems to know of the world !
* * V *
“ Face ” : A swindle, whereby one can get, at a
comparatively small outlay, enough collars, neckties, &c.,
to last him half a century, is worked by individuals versed
in the haberdashery business on big soft-goods firms in
dear old England. Send £1 to one of said firms with a
request that you be put on their sample-list, and in two
or three months you will receive a large box packed with
many variegated neckties, numerous silk and other hand-
kerchiefs, together with a fair supply of socks, collars,
gloves and other useful and necessary articles. Many
backblock storekeepers get their supplies in this way. Of
course the British draper won’t be had twice by the one
individual, but said individual can always get a fresh
supply by changing his “ custom " to another shop.
* * * *
An attempt is being made to coerce the Austra-
lian Railways Commission into allowing the privileges
accorded commercial travellers to be given only to mem-
bers of the C.T. Association. Which simply means that
a C.T. who does not wish to be a member must become
one if he desires railway privileges. Or, if the Railway
Commissioners fall in with the C.T.A.’s views, an ob-
noxious drummer who wishes to preserve his rail
privileges, may be blackballed and deprived of what is
his public right. Cheek !
• * * *
“S.S.”: There are many ways of making a
living, and I have discovered one of the meanest, fol-
lowed by a bottle-nosed person in the suburbs. His
game is to take up small shops in poor localities, and
start greengrocery or pin, cotton, and tape businesses,
keep them going for a week or two, and then advertise
A business for sale in a populous suburb, suitable for
widow or wife of workman. Stock and goodwill, £25.”
there is always a demand for businesses of this kind, and
the bottle-nosed person, being gifted and plausible, suc-
ceeds in trading off his twopenny-ha’penny biz at an
enormous sacriflth, my tear.” Heard him boast of
having sohl three in one week, out of which he cleared
AMO. Only one of his ventures he has failed to sell within
a month or so, and that developed, to his enormous sur-
prise, into a profitable concern. Fortune favors the
Knave.

(( Smuggled or stolen tobacco is always easily
placed,” but that “honor amongst thieves” does not
always hold good a Sydney tobacconist lately discovered.
Had been buying caddies of tobacco from an unrecognised
hawker (no questions asked), a £5 caddy costing him only
A 2. Hut last call the hawker made he brought the caddv
in a gunny-sack as usual ; and, the shop being full the
shopkeeper motioned him to leave it behind the counter
and handed over £2, as per usual. Afterwards, however’
upon examining the swag, he found that he had this time
parted up for a choice lot of brick-bats.

* * * .
The newchum, or t’othersider, at Klondyke is
known as a “ sucker,” and the most payable local indus-
try is euphemistically called “sucker-bumping.” Those
fortunate enough to get back to the coast without being
absolutely fleeced, are advised by friendly experts to re-
plenish their purses by “bumping a few suckers.” The
term indeed is a succinct description of the mission of the
Canadian-Pacific Railroad and the Hudson’s Bay Co in
connection with the Klondyke.


“The White Man’s Burden.”
Mr. Rudyard Kipling has recently been telling
the people of America, in a series of magnihcent
verses, what “ The White Mans Burden •
shows the responsibility which has been
on their shoulders by recent conquests, and in
soul-stirring cadences, bids them lake up the
White Man’s Burden.” Whilst these verses are
addressed to the Americans in particular, there is
another “White Man’s Burden of far more
universal character, namely, that particular y
sensitive organ, the liver. This great organ is the
regulator of our bodily health. If impaued, ot ler
organs, such as the kidneys, become affected, ana
ill-health results. And such was the experience
of Mr. J. Waller, of 82 York-street, South Mel-
bourne. His story of suffering for years from
long-standing liver complaint, and subsequent
cure by Bile Beans, was narrated to a repre-
sentative of the Spobtsman as follows
“I am glad,” he began, “to give my testi-
mony as to the very great value of Bile Beans.
After five years’ intense suffering from liver and
kidney disease, Bile Beans have cured and made
a new man of me. It is, in fact, over five years
since I first began to feel bad, could not properly
digest my food at times, and, on other occasions,
would suffer from too much bile, . and endure
agony. Doctors said it was the liver. I tried
various remedies, but without benefit, and
gradually got worse, could scarcely look at food,
lost flesh, and became a miserable wreck of
humanity. Two years ago the complaint was
aggravated by kidney troubles, with touches of
rheumatism. Yeu can scarcely imagine how I
suffered. I became gloomy and morose. The
world seemed black to me, and I did not care
whether I lived or died. Hearing so much about
Bile Beans, and knowing people who had bene-
fited by them, I decided to give them a trial, and
did so. The effect was immediate and pleasantly
surprising. Two boxes made ine feel a very dif-
ferent man. I continued to take them, and am
now, as you see, cured, all the credit being due to
Bile Beans. I can now quite understand how that
medicine works its cures, for it must act on the
liver and bile. I hope others will benefit by my
experience.”
Shot Through the Head.
An elderly gentleman while walking down one
of the principal streets yesterday was alarmed at
experiencing a sharp pain that shot right through
his head. He knew immediately that a headache
was threatening, and, being a man of great good
sense, he also knew that the best possible remedy
was Bile Beans for Biliousness. These Beans,
acting directly on the liver, give almost _ in-
stantaneous relief in all cases of constipation,
headache, indigestion, debility, biliousness, and
similar complaints, which all have their origin in
liver derangement. If the liver gets out of order,
as it often does in a climate such as ours, the whole
digestive system goes wrong, and as the proper
digestion of food is the most essential thing in the
sustaining of life, the consequence of bad digestion
must be most disastrous. Bile Beans will keep
you well during the trying wintry weather
that will soon be upon us. If you get sick, they
will put you right. They are the greatest specific
of the age, and, as such, they have positively
cured many cases where other remedies for which
much is claimed have utterly failed.
The Newsboy’s Question.
It is a well-known fact that among the little
street arabs who vend the daily newspapers
about the streets of all large cities, the possessors
of remarkably keen intellects are frequently found.
A case in point occurred only a few days ago. The
youngster, after having effected the sale of a
paper, requested the purchaser to answer him
which was the quicker, heat or cold ? Bailing to
receive an answer, the boy volunteered the infor-
mation that heat was the quicker, as you can catch
cold. And now as this is the weather when colds
are most easily caught, it would be well to fortify
ourselves against such possibility. Colds are
caused by the congestion of the blood, which is
most prominent in the head and throat. In-
fluenza can be traced to the same cause, and
irregular or insufficient liver action is in most
cases responsible for both. If the proper and
effective working of the liver be restored, the
dangers of contracting colds or influenza will be
reduced to a minimum. Bile Beans for Bilious-
ness have been proved to be the most effective
known remedy for ensuring the safe and thorough
discharge by the liver of its natural functions, and
is, therefore, the surest preventative for colds, and
where they have been contracted, Bile Beans will
speedily remove them. Their price is only a shil-
ling and three halfpence per box, and they can be
obtained from all chemists. If a course of this
remedy be taken, it will minimise the danger of
your experiencing personal proof of the newsboy’s
statement that heat is quicker than cold because
cold can be caught. There is a heap of solid
advice contained in the newsboy’s witticism, and
advice which, if followed by older foks, will bring
in its train far greater benefits than may at first
sight appear.

" They Didn’t Know.”
Many people, when detected in the commission
of some wrongful act, attempt to excuse them-
selves by the plea that “ they didn’t know it was
wrong.” Of course, the law of the land absolutely
refuses to excuse any wrong-doer upon such a
ground, and the moral law should be as equally
just in refusing pardon to all such as do wrong to
themselves by neglecting the care of their health,
particularly when they advance the untenable ex-
cuse that they were unaware of anything which
would remove the causes of their trouble. Ninety
per cent, of the ills which afflict the people of
Australia of to-day are directly the result of im-
paired digestive organs. The liver, stomach, and
kidneys are the three great regulators of human
health, and when those organs become affected
the effects thereof are quickly felt in the system.
Now, it is plain that if the sure and effective
working of those organs be secured, the causes of
the ill-effects, which, when impaired, they are
the known producers of, will be removed. For
promoting the thorough working of those organs
Bile Beans have been proved to be the most effec-
tive known preparation, as well as being the most
potent remedy for all ailments resulting from any
derangement of the digestive organism. They
are obtainable from all chemists and storekeepers,
and the price is only one shilling and three half-
pence per box. Thousands of sufferers have been
benefited by their use, and patients have been re-
stored to health when hope of recovery had been
abandoned by their medical attendants and
friends. From far and wide testimonials are
being daily received from grateful persons who
have been cured by their use. If you are suffer-
ing through any ailment resulting from deranged
digestive organs, you have a simple but most
effective remedy at hand. This remedy is Bile
Beans for Biliousness, and you should not do
wrong to yourself by neglecting to avail yourself
of their curative powers. If you do there is abso-
lutely no excuse for you, and you cannot shield
yourself behind the “I didn’t know” hedge of
defence which seems to be so eagerly sought by
persons now-a-days in their efforts to excuse or
hide the results of their folly and negligence.

Midnight Executions.
In Australian gaols the sentence of death which
has been passed upon a condemned criminal is
customarily carried into effect during the early
morning. But it may not be generally known
that midnight executions are taking place in
thousands of Australian homes every night of our
lives, with impaired digestive organs acting as the
executioner. It is at night, when sleep should be
reinvigorating our bodies, that a deranged
digestive organism gets in its evil work, and lays
the foundation for the ruin of health, home and
happiness. Now, something is required to check
these wholesale midnight executions, and Bile
Beans for Biliousness will be found to be the
required article. They exercise their beneficial
effects and curative powers upon a sufferer whilst
he is at rest, and thereby counteract the insidious
attacks of a deranged liver, stomach or kidneys.
Their action is gentle, there is no griping or
purging, and no evil effects. Their formula is
purely vegetable, and such drastic ingredients as
aloes, calomel or mercury, as found in most purga-
tives, are emphatically and positively not em-
ployed, even in the smallest degree. For the year
ending February 28, thirty million doses of Bile
Beans were consumed in Australia alone. They
are sold by chemists and storekeepers generally,
price one shilling and three halfpence per box.
They have, in thousands of cases, proved their
efficacy in restoring sufferers to health, and they
are universally acknowledged to be the most
potent power known to-day in Australia in pre-
venting those midnight executions which are
almost nightly occurring.

“I Took Them.”
The laws relating to the unlawful taking of
other people's property are somewhat stringent in
the colonies, but their administration is occasion-
ally the cause of much comment. It has fre-
quently been noticed that the unfortunate, who,
when perhaps pressed by want and hunger, appro-
priates to his own use a small sum, is sentenced to
a long term of imprisonment; whereas the well-
connected wrong-doer, when charged with the
larceny of perhaps thousands of pounds, has fre-
quently, in reply to his “ I took them,” received a
sentence far from commensurate with his crime.
The ill-gotten property of such actions never re-
sults in benefit, but there is a preparation now
being sold throughout the Australian colonies—
Bile Beans for Biliousness —which, when used,
causes people to joyfully say “I took them.” These
Beans are a purely vegetable preparation, and the
result of years of thoughtful research on the part
of an American medical man. The use of
any, even the slightest deleterious substance, is
most carefully avoided. They have been com-
pounded with the especial object of assisting the
liver, stomach and kidneys to thoroughly and
effectually perform their natural functions. If
any of the foregoing organs of the digestive
system are impaired, ill health is the result ;
and, in fact, nine-tenths of all the ills which
affect mankind to-day are directly traceable to
deranged digestive organs. As Bile Beans will
restore those organs to their proper working
order, they will prove efficacious in removing the
causes of a great majority of the ailments from
which men and women unfortunately suffer to-
day. They have done this in thousands of cases,
and innumerable men and women to-day find
themselves in a position of great benefit through
being able to say “ I have taken them.”

Scientists tell us that in this nineteenth century
of ours about the greatest strides made in the dis-
covery world have been in medicine. We have to

of a certain vegetable substance that has the effect

bile. The discovery is revolutionising all the pre-
sent methods of treatment for digestive ailments.
The old theory of pepsin, bismuth, starving,
purging, &c., &c., is to be done away with. This
great vegetable substance is compounded with
