General Failing Health.
REMARKABLE CURE AT EPSWIOH,
QUEENSLAND.
BILE BEANS ACHIEVE A WONDER
FUL VICTORY.
Usually when a person is attacked by General
Failing Health his or her life is regarded as fast
drawing to a close. The fears in such an event
are based upon a stronger foundation when the
sufferer is one of advanced yeais. In like manner
the restoration to health of such a person so
afflicted, should attract much more than passing
wonderment. And such a cure would indicate
that the means by which it had been effected,
must have been some extremely efficacious and
life-sustaining remedy. The wonderment at the
cure would be naturally heightened also when it is
known that it had been effected by a proprietary
medicine after hope had been abandoned and doc-
tors and many other medicines tried in vain.
Usually patent medicines are received with mis-
givings, but the following details of the remark-
able case and cure of a Queensland lady will
silence any misgivings, even supposing such to be
entertained, in regard to the marvellous curative
properties possessed by Bile Beans eok Bilious-
ness.
The lady’s name is Mrs. T. Wooley, of East-
street, Ipswich, Queensland. She is 53 years old,
and has resided in Ipswich for twenty-six years,
being well known in the district. Mrs. Wooley
says she has not the slightest objection to the pub-
lication of the facts connected with her case and
cure. “I consider,” she said, “it is for the sake
of humanity, and also that I may be able to show
my gratitude to the makers of Bile Beans. The
facts of my case are as follows : —I commenced to
suffer severely from headaches and continual
pains in the chest about eight years ago. The
pains, which were of a most acute nature, usually
attacked me after meals. I did not at that time
know from what kind of illness my pains arose,
but I have since thought that they came from in-
digestion. As I have already said, the pains first
assailed me about eight years ago, and never left
me until a short time back. During this time I
consulted many doctors in turn, and it is a sin-
gular thing but they each and all said that the
pains in my head were the result of worry, and
advised me to take a change of air. I took this
change, and after having given it a sufficient time
within which to work some improvement in me and
it failing to do so, I became convinced that some-
thing else was needed. It was then that I, in
turn, tried all the patent medicines so largely
advertised. Notwithstanding that I persevered
in taking those medicines for a time, extending
over some years, I found myself in the end
worse instead of better. I was now thoroughly
disgusted, and thought that the remainder of my
days would be spent in suffering. My headaches
and pains had by this time so increased that they
were almost intolerable. My appetite left me,
and my sleep became very poor ; and, as a conse-
quence, my system was wholly run down. I have
now reached the turning point in my story. A
Bile Beans pamphlet having been left at my
house, I read in it a testimonial from a person
giving particulars of the cure by the use
of Bile Beans of a complaint which I
thought was similar to my own. Although
I was tired of taking medicines, and had but little
faith in the Beans, I thought I would give them a
trial. You may imagine my surprise when, al-
though I had no confidence in the Beans, I began
to feel better after a few doses. My pains and
headaches began to be less severe, and my health
continued to improve. This improvement gradu-
ally went on until now my former good health
has wholly returned, and I feel as I felt before my
illness first attacked me. I have now been taking
the Beans for three months, and during that time
I have not had even a slight return of my former
complaints. My neighbors are surprised at the
change which has taken place in me, and say that
they never saw me looking so well; and, as I feel
as well as I look, I cannot say too much in praise
of Bile Beans. It will certainly be a hard day for
me when I have not a box of them in my house.”

A Parliamentary Marine Board—N.S.W.
Legislative Council.
A lie has nine legs ; and when it wants
to put its best foot foremost it uses the “ leg ” of
Sydney Day-lie Te-leg-raph.
Sydney D.T. heading :—“ Denouncing the
Bill. Mr. John Nobbs, M.P., Speaks.” Which
unfortunate caption made all who know Mr. John
Nobbs, M.P., think of Balaam’s Ass.
Gr. Republican Dibbs derides Reid’s 12
nominees to N.S.W. Leg. Council as “ a seedy
lot.” But not so seedy as the crowd who lately
supported Dibbs at Sydney Town Hall.
Sydney Sunday Times has now sheered off
the capital clause, and is using as a scarecrow the
Braddon Blot and the prospect of a Protectionist
tariff. Yet the Times has hitherto posed as a
Protectionist organ.
FOR N.S.W. LEG. COUNCIL.
They either fear their fate too much,
Or their deserts are small,
Who dare not put it to the touch
To win or lose it all.
Sydney D.T.’s' latest dilemma is to please
the Labor members in the Lower House by de-
riding the new M’sL.C., and, at the same time, to
keep in with the working classes by approving of
the recognition of the Labor-party's right to be
represented in the Upper House.
Affleck, M.P. for Yass, and Wilks, M.L.A.
for Balmain, are recent converts to the Federal
Bill. Sydney D.T., which so often has printed
the glowing periods of these mighty legislators,
made not the slightest reference to the Assembly
speeches wherein they announced their apostasy.
Premier Reid deserves credit for holding a
stiff back to those urging him to imitate Seddon in
offering a contingent for service in Samoa. N.S.
Wales General French’s hint of “ a thousand men
who would be very pleased to go ” fell as flat as a
hundred other invitations to play butcher without
precisely knowing who owns the calf.
In the ’76 ructions in Samoa, then also
accompanied with considerable loss of life, it was
the British and Americans, not the Germans, who
were at loggerheads. On that occasion Capt.
Stevens, of H.M.S. Barracouta, arrested the
masterful Col. Steinberger, and nearly precipitated
hostilities between John and Jonathan.
‘‘Willie” Stephens, M.L.A. for S. Bris-
bane—all the Willie-boys have a weak spot some-
where—now joins theanti-Federationists, although
he declared for Federation before the elections.
It was this quaint politician who, two years ago,
openly accused the Boodlewraith (his own) party
of bribery and corruption ; but he still sit I and
votes with it.

N.S.W. Mines Minister Joe Cook is as
doleful an example of YeS-No as. his boss, Reid.
For instance, as Minister for Agriculture he is

holds ready for presentation a Bill for stringent
local option—in the Cook sense, for drink suppres-

chelor are being hard pushed at West Adelaide by

S.A. Laboritesare “ working” for all it is
worth the characteristic speech of the National
Ass magnate, S. Tomkinson, M.L.C., at the re-
cent Moonta and Wallaroo Co. meeting. Moonta
miners are notoriously ill-paid ; yet Mr. Tomkin-
son, talking of “ill-advised” promises of ad-

increase of wages is undue and unnecessary.”
A pity that Crick’s offer to resign his com-
fortable West Macquarie seat and help to carry
Federation through N.S.W. Leg. Council was not
accepted by Premier Reid. Crick is the sort of
man who could do a heap of good in the Council
by waking up J. Lucas, and speaking candidly
to Benjamin Backhouse, and the Honorable
Blanksby, and acting the genial bull-ant gener- .
ally in that somnolent Elysium. The Council has
not possessed a man like Crick since the advent
of “ Responsible ” Govt., and that vitriolic genius
has himself been so slumbrous and dispassionate
of late that his migration to “ another place ”
might help the gods to laugh again.
All over Maoriland now flaps the gaudy
election banner of the Tory party with the device
Let us reduce Taxation.” It is about
time someone kicked a hole in that ban-
ner large enough to throw a goat
through. Maoriland is a cheaply-
governed country—in proportion to
population it is run at less expense
than N.S.W., Queensland, S.A. or
Westralia, and it would also be run
more cheaply than Victoria but for
the fact that the old Tory Party
left it a far heavier debt and a
far bigger interest bill than Vic-
toria possesses. There is little
room for retrenchment in Maori-
land. Therefore reducing taxa-
tion only means more borrow-
ing, which means more taxa-
tion in the future; or more
land sales, which means less
land revenue, and consequently
more taxation in the future.
Premier Braddon, of Tas-
mania, has dreams about a land-
tax based on unimproved land
values only, and the necessary
assessments are in preparation.
Tasmania’s present land-tax is Jd.
in the and if a strong Premier
would double or treble this im-
post, and use the proceeds to open
up the West Coast with roads and
railways in place of leaving the
work to gridiron syndicates, he
might live to see Tasmania well on
the road to a population of a million.
It is a solemn thing to reflect upon
that almost the only decently-watered
part of tliis horribly dry land is prac-
tically inaccessible because a tired Go-
vernment won’t make the necessary
roads.
According to his annual report,
“ Boss ” Creer, of Sydney Govt. Labor
Bureau, continually finds himself confronted
by the problem of what to do with that portion
of the unemployed that is too old or otherwise
incapable of facing competition. Private em-
ployers will not engage the aged, and Govt, has
to either find them relief-work at which they
can do their little best to earn enough to buy
food with, or take the responsibility of let-
ting them starve. And as Govt, hasn’t got
the courage, or the brutality, to say it will
let them starve outright, why not have an
old-age-pension system and do the. thing
decently ?
Gut of three Q. sugar-towns—Bun-
daberg, Mackay, and Cairns—two now re-
turn anti-kanaka legislators. A nasty knock
for the Govt, press, that is continually crying out
that the kanaka is the mainstay of the sugar-
industry. Evidently the people in the districts
concerned don’t think so.
The N.S.W. Anti-Billious leader, is still
unannounced, and the pathetic appeals to Jack
Want to come out and lead the party betray the
fact that he has no likely supplanter. The anti-
Federal following, too, gains few recruits. The
Cabbage-Tree Mob was reduced to placarding
Stephen—who ran Dacey to a head for Botany
seat—as a politician dead against the Bill, and
even he has “turned dog” and supports the
measure.
Kerr, Labor M.L.A. for Barcoo, Q., owes
his seat to the loyalty of his bush mates. 110 of
them were unjustly disfranchised. And Kerr’s
opponent took care to have the backblocks polling-
booths as far apart as possible ; but the disfran-
chised men provided relays of their own horses
every 10 or 20 miles, and Kerr’s supporters tra-
velled 90 or more miles on polling day. No
wonder the Boodlewraith is astounded at the
Labor organisation.
A big weapon in the hands of the N.§.W.
Conservative-party is that members of the Leg.
Council are unpaid. Pay the Council, and it will
be far more amenable to public opinion. Also,
pay the Council, and you will be able to appoint
thereto good and democratically-representative
men from all over the province.' Just consider
the disadvantages under which a miners’ repre-
sentative like Buzacott must labor in coming,
unpaid, all the way from Broken-hill to Sydney.
From circular issued by South Warrego
(Q.) Political Association, run in the Ministerial
interest :
, The result of the last annual revision-court was par-
ticularly gratifying to our cause. Out of 78 objections
lodged to names on the roll 76 were allowed.
And yet Premier Dickson, who was prevented
speaking at Cunnamulla, where the revision-court
was held, by some of the 76 disfranchised electors,
threatened to bring in legislation to stop such tac-
tics at public meetings !
The latest bogey manufactured by the
N.S.W. “Colonists’ Anti-Bill League” makes it
appear that Riverina pastoralists (who, because of
differential rates, have their wool now railed for
about 40s. a ton) will have to pay between £4 and
£5 a ton under the “ virtual monopoly ” of the
Commonwealth. That bogey is too murderous in
intent. It would not alone scare to death the
present, but all Federal Bills for all time. But
the pure bogeyness of its character is shown by
the fact that pastoralists, among them Riverina
pastoralists, are excellent friends of the Bill in
hand Sam M'Caughey, M.L.C., king of the
squatter-ring enters N.S.W. Upper House in
the interests of Federation, and to help keep the
Sydney colonists’ gang from garotting it from
behind. And surely Sam M'Caughey, the man
who started with nothing and has made three or
wet mill,ons ’ knows enough to come in out of the
Premier Kingston is delighted at having
unearthed in S.A. Register of 1855-7, the period
in which the S.A. Constitution was being
fashioned, a series of most radical leading-articles
advocating manhood suffrage for both Houses of
the Legislature, and urging, that the only differ-
ence as to their election should be that the Council,
r by the whole province as one constituency,
should represent general interests, and the
Assembly, elected by districts, should voice local
needs. It is too excruciatingly funny,” Kings-
ton writes, “ the way in which the Register in its
manhood, of nearly 50 years ago disposes of the
pet theories of its present dotage. ”
Selt-preservation is the first principle of
politicians. Scores and scores of “ward” and
lodge statesmen regard Federation as an engine
specially contrived for thei r extermination. Who
can oonceive a Federal Par-
liament handicapped by
the presence of theanti-
Federal hangers -on
of the provincial
Assemblies ?
AT A SILVER- COIN SHOW,
FIRST ACTOR: “Business is ‘dead slow.’ I’m
almost starving.”
SECOND ACTOR: “ Nonsense ! Didn’t you have a
meat-pie one day last week?"
Parkes, when Premier in 1888, moved—
That in future the Govt, of N.S.W. should be
informed of any intended appointment to the
high office of Governor before such appointment
is finally made.” G. R. Dibbs, then leader of the
Opposition, seconded the motion. G. H. Reid
spoke and voted against it, but Parliament passed
There still stands to the credit of N.S.W.
new Houses 1 of Parliament i!100,000, authority to
expend which was duly given under a Loan Act.
Luckily, this sum is still intact. There also exists
a Board Reid, Lackey, Abbott, Lyne, Want and
others —to examine and advise on designs. But
the Board forgets it is in being. The <£loo,ooo
was only a first instalment. The best thing Par-
liament can do is to repeal the Act, returning the
cash and wiping out the Board.
Premier Reid’s agreement to help Henry
Copeland to obtain leave to introduce a Bill to
regulate Sunday trading—principally to open the
pubs.—and prevent N.S.W. Civil Service Co-
Operative Society from retailing whisky at its bar
—is really a timely sop to the King Division
whisky-millers. These gentry are pawing the
ground ominously, so Reid rushes in “to help
Copeland to obtain leave to introduce,” etc. Put
if the Bill be actually introduced, where will Reid
be at the critical division? Where was he last
time?
A TELL-TALE POSTER.
The Alien Restriction Act came into force
in Tasmania April 1. Under this measure every
alien may be required to write 50 words in some
European language before being allowed to land.
S.A. Govt, is still being bombarded by a
syndicate for permission to construct a railway
from Pt. Augusta to W.A. border. The
syndicate says it is willing to spend
£2, 000,000 and asks for no land-grants,
and offers to give Govt, right of pur-
chase at any time, and only wants a
State-guarantee of interest on the
£2, 000,000 to the extent of 2| per
cent. As the country between Pt.
Augusta and the W.A. border is
some of the worst in Australia, this
looks about the most philanthropic
scheme of the century. But such
schemes are generally ioaded some-
where or other.
Curious that Maoriland, of
all places, should be agitated by
such a violent discussion about its
falling birth-rate. . It has a higher
birth-rate than Victoria, or S.A.,
and a lower death-rate than any of
the other six Australias. In ’9B,
for every 1000 inhabitants there were
nearly 10 deaths and nearly 26 births—
a gain of 16, so it had the largest in-
crease by excess of births over deaths in
Australasia. In fact, the only province
which came within gunshot of it was
_ Queensland. Maoriland doesn’t know when
it’s well off.
“Victorian”: The seats of some
Vic. M’s.P. are kept secure in an in-
teresting way. Nearly every cocky
and cocky’s man has a sonin
the railways or civil-service.
and the influence of the
absentee is maintained in
the district by the rates
being paid in his name,
the “ old man ” taking
out an electors- right.
Then, when an election
happens along, the “ rate-
payer” is franked back to
vote. Manhood suffrage and
the recording of votes at
the handiest polling-places
would send a few out-of-date
fossils into long-merited ob-
scurity.
Curious to consider all the
small -ways in which Freetrade
N.S.W. protects its local industries.
In the matter of wheat, for instance,
the railway rate for N.S.W. wheat
coming from the west towards the seaboard
towns is 11s. 4d. per ton on a 200-mile
journey. Imported wheat going inland from the
coast pays nearly double these rates. Victoria
and S.A. charge the same rate both ways, but
they dump a heavy duty on the imported grain.
They are openly and honestly Protectionist on
a large scale. N.S.W. is secretly and dishonestly
Protectionist on a small one.

Missing Friends.
OVERTON, a native of Sydney, about 55
years of age, is supposed to have perished in
the bush near ALPHA, CENTRAL QUEENSLAND, on
or about the 16th day of January, 1889. He is supposed
to have left two MARRIED SISTERS and a NEPHEW,
his NEXT OF KIN surviving him. Should this meet
the eye of any of the NEXT OF KIN of deceased, they
are requested to communicate with the undersigned.
J. BLOOD SMYTH,
Acting Deputy Curator in Intestates Estates,
Rockhampton, Central Queensland.

COUNTRY TRIAL ORP€R RESPECTFULLY SOLICITEP.


The fire and burglar epidemic has come round
again, and is raging briskly in Sydney. During the last
few weeks there have been several fires in which there
has been considerable destruction of valuables, and the
enterprising burglar has had some good jewellery hauls.
In each case this was due to inadequate precaution. If
the advantages of the Sydney Safe Deposit had been
availed of the loss would have been little or none, while
the cost of this insurance would have been infinitesimal.
We are desired to invite Bulletin readers to visit the
vaults in Ash-street, off George-street, back of Paling’s.*
The cure of piles by the new “Canalin”
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
Piles, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert
bharp. Copies, with testimonials, sent free to
any colony from the dep6t, 257 George-street,
Sydney.*
The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society’s
funds now exceed two millions and a quarter, of which a
considerable proportion is invested in Government stock
and other liquid securities. The Bankers’ Magazine
(London) writes: “The Colonial Mutual has not only a
position of great strength to recommend it, but it is re-
markably progressive in its methods, and is oontinually
striving to render its policies more and more attractive.”
The Federal character of the Colonial Mutual’s organisa-
tion renders it an institution of special interest at the
present moment.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Significant of the stage the new: boxing-boom
has reached in Sydney that 1200 people distributed them-
selves between Alhambra A.O. and Golden Gate A. 0., last
Saturday night, to witness novice-competitions—each for
30sovs. cut up. At Alhambra the fighting was lively and
severe, especially bouts between Poet and Harden, and
Head and Jim Hope. Poet gives promise of developing
into a good ’un. He has a daisy left, and never loses his
head. Read, pupil of Tom Nelson, who figured promi-
nently in the days of the old “ White Horse” gymnasium,
made his initial public appearance “ for keeps.” Report
credited Read with being a top-notcher, and much was
consequently expected of him. He belongs to the legs-
and-wings variety’, and keeps up a continual toe-jig
throughout every round. Gallery-sports whistled horn-
pipes for the man on wires, and the man on wires got
angry thereat. Hope, inches shorter every way and
pounds lighter, but game as a pebble, led the long ’un a
merry dance so merry that the judges couldn’t separate
the pair at the close of the fifth round, and another had
to he fought, with the result that Read won, but by very
little. Hope is among the grittiest in the ring to-day.
bix red-hot battles were witnessed at Golden Gate A.C.,
and how some of the pairs did bash each other 1 They
kept the excitement at fever-point from end to end, and
left the rings “busted.”
* * • •
At Alhambra A.C. (Sydney) on Saturday night
things were mixed. Judges slow in giving decisions,
referee slower still. Crowd jeered, boxers made im-
promptu speeches to the gallery’, seconds argued with
officials and brandished bony fists in their fat faces.
Read’s second rushed into the ring—-a palpable breach of
the rules which should have given the fight to Hope, but
no notice was taken of it. Confusion reigned supreme
for minutes.
* * * *
“Old Timer”: Saw Conlon-Burden affair
(won t call it fight) at Golden Gate A.C. on Sat. night
week, and am satisfied Burden won on points ; also satis-
fied that Conlon has a weak streak. Burden got close
in once punched the big fellow in the ribs twice, and
Conlon wasn’t “ taking any ” afterwards. Conlon. is
matched to fight Dan Q Connell, the Captain’s Flat
(N.S.W.) miner, next month, and former will know he
has been up against it—good and hard —when that battle
is over. Daniel’s a fighter through and through.
Provided nothing interferes meanwhile, ’rastler
Harry’ Cansdell (Sydney’s own man of thew) and German
Lorenz will gets in “ holts,” Friday, 21st inst., at Alham-
bra A.C. (Sydney), to contest for the local middleweight
championship. Conditions—Graeco-Roman, first two falls
out of three. Graeco-Roman style wearies one as a rule,
Dpt this bout will be lively, as the agreement provides
that neither athlete is to go down unless fairly thrown.
Ground-wrestling limited to smin„ and if no fall, both
shall be ordered to assume the perpendicular.
* * * *
Though still in the thirties, Mick Dooley,
Sydney s one-time brilliant heavyweight, is now a decided
back-number. He who formed the stepping-stone to
championship honors for Peter Jackson, Paddy Slavin,
and Joe Goddard was, let us hope, seen for the iast time
m a willin go when he met and was beaten by Doherty
at Kalgoorlie (W. A.), Easter Monday night. Poor Mick
presented a very feeble front, and 'received his quietus
fair on the point in the fourth round after a poor
showing, during which not for one moment did he
exhibit any of his old dash or cleverness. Doherty
and Dooley have now met three times within about
as many months, and have apparently been playing the
game in the West for all it was worth. At Fremantle,
/u° c " on in half-a-dozen rounds; at Kalgoorlie,
(the first time) Doherty got home within the same period,
and then know-alls looked wise and said, “ It’s Dooley’s
turn next! But it wasn’t, for Dooley’ went down before
a steam-hammer punch that would have felled an ox.
* * * *

was one Boota Singh, a lanky Hindoo, with an arin like a
chicken s leg and no chest worth mentioning. - Boota—

101 b. to battle with Cashin—formerly’ a middleweight

the turn-up wouldn't last lOsec. But it took Cashin 20

only after a couple of very close shaves for himself. The
mg., who is a handkerchief-vendor in private life, seem-
ingly knew all about the game, even if instructions hadn’t
been yelled at him by half-a-hundred Ram-Jam Sammees
who had turned up to do a barrack. What an object-
lesson those barrackers would have been for the superiah

* * * *
Big negro Armstrong, protege of Peter Jack-
son’s erstwhile hustling manager, “Parson” Davies, and
much-heralded prospective world-beater, met, last month
at Cincinnati (U.S. A.), another “ cullud pusson,” Frank
Childs, a deal shorter but verv stockily built, for the
colored heavy-weight championship of the earth, and
latter won after six savage rounds. Childs made such a
splendid showing that arrangements were subsequently
entered into for him and sailor Sharkev to a-et together
Australian Peter Jackson’s first fight in Yankeeland was
for the colored championship of the world, when he licked
that wonder of his time, “ Chocolate ” Godfrey, with left-
hand jabs over the heart which Godfrey treated disdain-
fully until, as he afterwards explained, their frequency
seemed to weaken the spot, and they became strange
thumps as if a ton weight were behind them. “ I was
fairly parlysed or mesmerised,” said “ Old Chocolate.”
Peter paralysed or mesmerised many others in the same
manner afterwards, until the “ heart blow ” became more
famous than Australian George Dawson’s “ kidney
punch.” J
* * * *
The victory of one Australian in America now-
a-days is a .novelty, but what must be said of two men
from this section coming out on top in one ring on one
night? Tom Tracey and “ Cocker ” Tweedie met Muller
and Reilly, respectively, at ’Frisco, Feb. 28. Tracey won
in 10 of what was to have been a 20-rounds fight, while
Tweedie got away on points after boxing 15 rounds
There was nothing to rave about in’ either win. As a set-
off, news comes by same mail that in one night (March 7)
but at widely-separate places, two Australians went
under—Joe Goddard was easily beaten by Gus Ruhlin in
New York, and Billy Murphy got his quietus from Van
Heest at Dayton (O.).
* * • *
Melbourne “ Sewerage Champion ” Billy Ed-
wards and Paddy Gorman, the whilom dashing N.S.W.
middleweight, who went to the United States with Peter
Jackson in the ’Bo’s, had a hot go in New York, Feb. 23
Paddy made things sultry awhile, but directly the man
with the scented title got fairly to work he knocked
chips off his fellow-Australian until the seventh round,
when the already-satiated crowd cried “ take him away !”
And poor Paddy was carried off, a sorry spectacle.
* * * *
Pathetic item from the land o’ kilts and burgoo.
One-time-all-round-champion-athlete Donald Dinnie, hero
of victories totalling thousands, has had his tools of trade
(dumb-bells, &c.) “ arrested ” for debt at Aberdeen—the
magnificent Scot’s native town. Seems but a few months
since brawny Donald left Australia, after 10 years’ resi-
dence, full of hope and confidently looking forward to an
open-armed reception and replenished purse in the coun-
try which his deeds of strength and skill had advertised
the world over. Auld Donald is 63.
* * * *
Two consultations on Canterbury Park Plate
cannot fail in drawing a big concourse of people to wit-
ness a lot of mediocre horses gallop to-day (Saturday).
Kallatina is rapidly getting form, and if she can beat
Pense-k-moi and Carberra she should land this (if the
sweep-money’s right) rich prize.
* • * *
Brighton (Sydney) 2 miles handicap trot was
concluded on Thursday last, when Acquittal made an easy
win from Shakespeare in 5.28 J. What might have been
an exciting contest with reasonable handicapping, was
completely spoilt by the ridiculous marks on which some
of the better-performed horses were placed. Take Cool-
gardie, placed 40seos. behind. To beat Acquittal, the
horse_ would have had to trot at a 2.24>4gait; and even
allowing the handicap had been framed on a 2.50 basis
she would want to show a 2.30 gait to win—an impossible
task for her. Brighton is certainly the only club which
gives trotting a show in the Metropolis, but the good it
does the sport is discounted by the incapable handicap-
ping.
• • • •
The Doncaster winner, Vigorous, must have
genuinely earned his reputation for inconsistency, other-
wise a cute owner like Sam Fielder wouldn’t part with
him for £3OO, at which price he has been sold for India.
A horse that can, at weight-for-age, run Merloolas (in his
present fettle) to a head, as Vigorous did at Randwick, is
worth several times that amount—provided be
depended upon to always run up to form—and Vigorous
apparently can’t. Vigorous has a big contract before
him to beat the Indian record of another of Sam’s exports
—Vanitas.
* * * *
The Grandmaster filly, Grand Belle, one of the
late “ Dick ” Craven’s, has been put to pony-racing, and
opened her acoount by easily winning Kensington Handi-
cap, last Thursday. She is a peculiarly-built mare—
viewed from the rear she looks a “ 16 hands,” but is a
fair pony in front, and a nice galloper. She was bought
for only £2l.
* * * *
Ed. B.— Re par. as to Fairy Prince run-
ning without any money on him. As a matter of fact,
the horse did a wonderful “ go ” at Moorefield during the
week when the local crowd were away at Brighton—so
good that it was no wonder he equalled Survivor’s record
in the race. Those in the know backed him to a man,
and it does seem extraordinary that his trainer should
not have had a decent win considering the long odds on
tap.—W.
• * * *
Pity, for the prestige of the Australian race-
horse, that Aurum has broken down. When Newhaven
went to England it was thought the Britishers would at
last see the best of Australian form, but he was a disap-
pointment, and now Aurum has collapsed without show-
ing his quality.
• * * •
Ever since young Flanagan’s death at Caulfield
last year, the authorities under the Children’s Protection
Act (N.S.W.) have been waiting for a case to see whether
their regulations could be used as a check upon the prac-
tice of trainers engaging youngsters too small to cope
with the tasks imposed upon them. Last week the De-
partment’s inspector laid an information against E. E.
Keys, owner of Sir Charles, and Callinan, sen., for allow-
ing young Peter Callinan to ride at Randwick. It appears
that the boy, who is only 12 years old, fell off his mount
during the preliminary canter and broke his arm, the
cause being the large amount of dead weight he had to
carry. Sir Charles is a big horse, and the jockey simply
toppled off, top-heavy. The defence admitted the youth-
fulness of little Peter, and also the danger, but denies
that a horse-race is a “ public performance ” within the
meaning of the Act. The magistrate would not decree
the point, and a case is to be stated for the Full Court.

At the recent Randwick sale of yearlings it was
curious to notice that the stock of the untried Chester
horse Projectile fetched higher prices than those of the
consistent winner-getters Lochiel and Gozo.
* * * *
The Great Eastern Steeplechase, run lately at
Onkaparinga (S. A.), seems to have been almost a record
race for Australia. Fifteen faced the starter, and the
whole lot, barring Eclipse, fenced like machines. Domino
humped the crusher of I2st. 131bs. to victory, thus beat-
ing Flashlight’s (12s. lOlbs.) and Mahdi’s (12st. 121b5.)
wonderful performances. S.A., woefully behind N.S.W.
and Vic. in flat-racing, yet not only manages to keep
’chasing up to the mark by attracting good ’uns from
other provinces, but turns out excellent “leppers” of
her own.
* • • •
Old-time ’rastler Teffen shuffled off at Riverton
(M.L.) recently, having “strutted and fretted” 62
years. For over half-a-score of that term he was 9.7
champion of England, and could give more weight away
than any other man in his time.
* * * *
The inevitable forging in connection with Melb.
“ art-union” tickets has happened at last. Two tickets
bearing the numbers of the second and third prizes in the
Druids’ lottery were sold at a heavy reduction on their
face value (in jewellery) to a couple of confiding hotel-
keepers, and when presented for payment were found to
have been “ faked.” The pasteboards were genuine, but
their numbers had been ingeniously altered. When one
considers the cheap get-up of these shilling cards the
wonder is that the swindle didn’t suggest itself to the
spieling intellect years ago.
The management of the Druids Gala, whilst
claiming due credit for the discovery of the fake by com-
paring these wrong tickets with the official counterfoils of
the right tickets, incidentally mentioned that the second
prize had not been claimed. This raises the question
whether it was actually sold. Druid Art Union tickets
could be bought in Melb., anyway, on the day of the
drawing. At what hour was the sale stopped, and what
means are taken to ensure the omission of unsold numbers
from the drawing ? How many tickets were put into cir-
culation? How many of these were thrown back on the
committee’s hands? Are all of the 125 prizes (mostly
paltry) available for capture by public purchasers of
tickets when the drawing comes off ? The system of sel-
ling tickets through hundreds of agents in'Victoria and
elsewhere seems to demand that all returns should be
made at least a week previous to the date of the drawing.
The recent reception of Bill Martin at Melb.
St. Vincent’s Fair shows that it would be almost worth
while for Australian cycling leagues to unite and pay the
renowned Plugger £2OOO a year to stay here and ride.
Boom the sport Bill certainly does, for Saturday brought
a bigger and more enthusiastic crowd around the arena
than has been seen for many a day. When William, bare-
armed and pink-suited, made his appearance, he was
simply roared at. He is thinner in face than of old,
but is very fat about the legs, and, were he not a man
who is always fit, he would be reckoned out of condition.
No doubt it was the green tinge in his blood that in-
duced him to appear gratis at St. Vincent’s show. His
entries, however, being irregularly made, he had no
chance for the prizes had he won them, but he rode four
hard races (only five days ashore) and raked in at least
£lOO for the charity.
* » * *
Pitted against Beauchamp, R. Lewis, and Hunt
in the heat of the mile scratch at Melb. St. Vincent’s
Fair, Billmartin went out at the last lap and won in great
style, and, later in the afternoon, he and Body paced one
another up to the field in the preliminary of the handicap,
for him to win from L. M. Jackson, jerking his machine
around the last turn as he used to do two years ago. Bill
was blocked in the semi-final of the handicap, but, in the
final of the mile, he met Walne, who had scratched him-
self for the heat of the handicap which Bill won. Half a
lap from home Martin shot out, and AValne hanging to
him a most exciting battle took place along the straight.
Walne’s finish was too good for the Yank, and as head to
head they passed the line, Walne’s wheel was a few' inches
in front. Martin is out now with a challenge to Walne
for £2so—while Don Walker wants Bill to lay him £lOO to
£5O and ride three distances in a match.

The main result of last week’s Sydney military
tournament was to prove that at mounted games the
bush-whacker—the real way-back bushy—can badly lick
the city and bush townsmen that comprise the Lancers
and Mounted Rifles. Australian Horse competitors, many
of whom had never seen the events before, got into every
final, and they won one event straight out, dead-heated
with M.R. in two others, and got innumerable places.
In several events said competitors captured the hearts of
the public, but lost the prizes, by going through at a
gallop, whilst the more experienced Lancers and M.R.
took things at a steady canter ; but with more practice
they should be able to give the latter this little bit in.
Apropos: in the sham-fight, during camp, officers had
hard work to stop their men from charging clean over
any of the enemy they came in contact with.

Tom MaJone’s former hostelry, the Gladstone,
at Tempe (Arneliffe) is being taken over by Mr. John
Ferris, who intends putting the running grounds into
thorough order, and starting matters with a handicap, of

War between civilized nations over the
Samoan question would, according to German
minister Yon Bulow, be criminal, “ but the main-
tenance of Germany’s right is a point of honor.”
Which is to say that Germany is prepared, if
necessary, to chance her share of criminality.
“Crowner’s quest” again. Three men
were recently killed through the faulty construc-
tion of a temporary bridge at the Pieman (Tas.).
It was explained that the cables that formed the
chief part of the suspension bridge simply drew
through the clips and deposited the whole struc-
ture,. with the workmen, into the river. The jury
verdicted that “no blame could be attached to
anyone.” A local newspaper passed over the re-
mains of the three men with the barest mention,
but grew eloquently sorrowful at “the loss of the
iron rails.”
Recent Samoan events make it look as if
Kipling’s “ White Man’s Burden ” is rapidly get-
ting heavier. One hundred and ten whites go out
for niggers’ wool and come back badly shorn
themselves. Butcher’s bill : 7 killed and 7
wounded. They had with them 150 “friendly”
natives who, finding they were short of cartridges,
very sensibly bolted. The “automatic Colt”
gun went bung at the critical moment, and on the
whole the niggers had much the best end of the
stick, though it is said (evidence not very clear)
that 30 of them were killed. Three of the whites
killed were officers—Lieuts. Lansdale and Mona-
ghan, of the U.S. ship Philadelphia, and Lieut.
Freeman of H.M.S. Tauranga. The last-men-
tioned officer, who was well-known in Sydney and
whose portrait appears herewith, had seen much
service, and possessed, in addition to several pro-
fessional decorations, the R.HS. bronze medal,
awarded for a gallant life-saving exploit in shark-
infested waters.
Policy-holders in the great A.M.P. Society
will be pleased to learn that the experienced and
trusted Reginald J. Black, who is a retiring
director, will be a candidate for re-election. On
the death of J. R. Hill, the A.M.P’s deputy-
chairman, Mr. Black was chosen by the directors
to fill the vacant seat on the board. Mr. Hill was
president of the Bank of New South Wales, and
the directors of that institution also paid Mr.
Black the compliment of selecting him as being, in
their opinion, from his extensive knowledge of
financial and commercial affairs, the best man for
the position. The investment of the large aggre-
gation of surplus funds of the A.M.P. Society is
the most difficult question the board has to deal
with, and as Mr. Black has retired from active
business, he is able to give ample time and energy
to his responsible duties.
Cabled that Jack Want has resigned his
place as Attorney-General in the Reid Ministry—
which apparently means that, after a lot of con-
sideration, he has made up his mind to sacrifice
his fat salary and fight Federation. So much the
better: an open enemy is far to be preferred to a
treacherous friend-on-a-fence.
Poet Ogilvie has a practical horse-side as
well as a poetical, and when he was handling “a
young one ” the other day it fell and rolled on
him, hurting his shoulder to some extent. The
enforced leisure has been productive of seve-
ral sets of verses which will shortly ap'pear in
The Bulletin. Ogilvie’s charming book of
“ Fair Girls and Gray Horses ” continues to sell
as well as it deserves—that is to say, splendidly.
The price is 4s. 6d. post-free from Bulletin
office.
Stephens, the victim of the Melb. Market
murder, was a witness at an inquest about a year
ago, and was shown over the morgue by the
coroner’s orderly. “ What a ghastly place ! ” he
remarked on emerging from the mortuary. “It
gives me the horrors. I won’t go in there again
till I have to.” And now he has “had to ” —with
two bullets in his body, and with his neck nearly
severed.
The Q. anti-Federalists are beginning to
move. At a “monster ” meeting lately called in
Brisbane one or two more or less defunct
politicians, headed by the ancient editor of that
most miserable of all rags, the local Telegraph,
tried to raise their discordant and feeble voices
against the extinction of Queensland and the
transformation of Brisbane into a grazing-ground
for N.S.W. cattle. Two of the chief speakers—
the president of the moribund local Protection
League and the self-styled leader of the Freetrade
party—created much amusement by flatly con-
tradicting each other, and the meeting, which
consisted mainly of a thousand empty chairs,
broke up abruptly on the vulgar suggestion of
someone that funds were needed to carry on the
agitation.
It takes a bard to catch a bard ; and E. J.
Brady claims to have caught the popular Newbolt
tripping when he wrote that
Drake, he’s in his hammock and a thousand feet below.
Brady points out that Drake was not thrust over-
board in the usual sailor-fashion, but was coffined
in lead that he might be better preserved to fight
Spaniards at the Day of Judgment. But
surely the lead coffin does not exclude the ham-
mock ? Anyway, Newbolt is welcome to pick
holes in Brady’s just-published collection of verses,
“The Ways of Many Waters,” and a copy has
been sent him to that end and others more com-
plimentary. The price of “The Ways” is 5s.,
post-free from Bulletin office.
Archbishop O’Reily is backing Editor
Denny, of the S. A- Roman Catholic organ,
Southern Cross, in the latter’s attempt to slip
into Parliament between Premier Kingston and
labor-leader Batchelor, the sitting members for
West Adelaide. Denny upholds the sphefne
of subsidising R.C. schools with a capitation
grant; Kingston and Batchelor, as in duty bound,
oppose it. The Bulletin hopes that the West
Adelaide Irishmen, who number many good
democrats, will, on this'momentous occasion, bear
in mind Dan O’Connell’s historical caution
against allowing priests to interfere in politics.
Kingston, and the broad-minded men who have
fought under his banner, can give, and have given,
the Irish more than they could ever hope to get
if represented in Parliament solely by men of
their own blood and creed. Even should
Denny succeed in ejecting Kingston from the
Assembly, the cause of a religious capitation
grant would still be hopeless, as the national
vote on the subject taken a year or two ago
clearly showed. The Bulletin calls on all
good Irish - Australians in West Adelaide to
record their votes for Kingston and Batchelor—
for the honest Radicalism which in politics asks
no man his religion, and which believes that chil-
dren of all creeds should study and play together
at the same schools to the end that they may
really know one another and be friends in after-
life.
A few days ago Sub-Inspector Cooperwired
from Cooktown to Brisbane that he had buried 37
bodies (victims of recent hurricane), and that
several corpses of colored men were found 2 feet
above the ground on trees. It’s only in N.Q. that
such things could occur. Also, according to
Hamilton, M.L. A., thousands of snakes, land and
water, were found thrown up on the coast, after
the hurricane. Bodies of porpoises were found 40
and 50ft. above sea-level, while trees were barked
and grass torn up by the roots. A long sandy
beach was transformed into a bare stretch of rock,
the sand having been all carried away by the
violence of the wind.

ABRIDGED PROSPECTUS
OF THE
Kall?Qite Explosives Corupaoy,
LIMITED
(REGISTERED UNDER THE COMPANIES ACTS OF N.S.W. AS A
LIMITED LIABILITY COMPANY).
Capital: £160,000 in 160,000 Shares of £1 Each.
80,000 Shares fully paid up and £lOOO in cash will be allotted and paid to the vendors as payment
(together with a further sum of £3500 to be paid out of profits) for the invention, process, and
patent rights for the manufacture of the explosive compound known as “Kallenite.”
80,000 Shares are offered for subscription, payable 2s. 6d. per share on application, 2s. 6d. per share on
allotment. The balance in calls as required, in amounts not exceeding 2s. 6d. per share, at inter-
vals of not less than two months.
From the money subscribed by the payment of the application and allotment fees, the Vendors are to
be paid £lOOO, as above mentioned, and the balance, less brokerage and flotation expenses, to be
placed to the credit of the Company.
DIRECTORS:
T. H. KELLY, Esq.
Hon. A. J. GOULD, M.L.C.

SYDNEY —Messrs. RANDLE & WILLIS, Martin-place.
MELBOURNE—Messrs. TOLHURST, KEATS, & CUMMING, 56 Queen-street.
ADELAIDE—Messrs. E. L. HEINEMANN & CO., No. 1 Pirie Chambers, Pirie-street.
BRISBANE —Messrs. CORRIE & CO., Queen-street.

Messrs. GOULD & SHAW, The Exchange, Sydney.
SECRETARY: R. N. KIRK.
Offices: Imperial Chambers, 16 O’Connell Street, Sydney.
This Company is being' formed for the purpose of acquiring from the Vendors the Invention, Process, Specifications,
and Patent Eights (as hereafter detailed) of the Explosive or Series of Explosives known as “ Kallenite,” and for
the purpose of Manufacturing, Selling and Disposing of the same in New South Wales and elsewhere.
The Explosive “ Kallenite” was discovered by Messrs. Alexander Fraser and John Callaghan, and the invention
was matured and perfected by these gentlemen aided by the advice and co-operation of Major W. C. Quinby, of San
Francisco, California, who has had an experience of over thirty years in the use, sale, and manufacture of all grades
of explosives. _
The Vendors having carried on extensive experiments for a considerable time with ultimate satisfactory results
now feel confident that they are offering to the country a valuable manufacturing industry, and to capitalists an
exceptional opportunity for investment.
Major Quinby reported : —“ After tireless efforts of myself and the members of the Syndicate, WE HAVE PRO-
DUCED THE BEST EXPLOSIVE EVER YET TESTED BY EXPERIENCED MEN. The discoveries are not only
revelations, but in their effects will be almost revolutionary, especially as far as these colonies are concerned.”
“ ‘ Kallenite ’is the most powerful explosive that I know of. IT SEEMS TO BE ABSOLUTELY SMOKELESS
AND FUMELESS. It can be manufactured in Australia as cheaply as any first-class explosive can be produced in
any other country. A clean, safe, smokeless, and fumeless explosive will be hailed with joy by miners everywhere.”
The following is an extract from the Sydney Morning Herald of 12th January, 1899, giving the result of a Public
Test, made at the Government Works, Glebe Island, by permission of the Minister for Works
“ The experiment was carried out under the direetiom of Mr. T. E. Burrows, of the Harbors and Rivers Depart-
ment, who has charge of the works. Fifty pounds weight of ‘Kallenite’ having been inserted in the bottom of the
four holes drilled downwards from a ledge high up the side of a stone cliff, it was discharged by electricity, and
straightway, with merely a slight incidental report, 200 cubic yards of rock suddenly broke out and fell forward in
fragments. There was little, if any, upheaval. The movement seemed similar to that of a landslip, and no fumes
or smoke were discernible.
“ No. 1 dynamite has 25 per cent, non-explosive material in its composition,-gelignite, 4 per cent, non-explosive ;
and blasting gelatine about 2 per cent, non-explosive ; but‘Kallenite,’it is claimed, is entirely explosive, and that
gives it a great advantage.”
Another test was made by Mr. J. Hutchen, Manager of the Mount David Gold Mine, who wrote
“ I have this day made a crucial test of the new explosive ‘ Kallenite ’ in all parts of the mine, stopes, drives, and
in the main shaft, and am pleased to say that it has done most satisfactory work all along the line.
“ The test made in the shaft was most pleasing, as the men went down immediately after the explosion and
FOUND, EXCELLENT WORK DONE, AND FOUND VERY LITTLE SMOKE OR FUME, OTHER THAN LEFT
BY THE BURNT FUSE.” '
As it is intended to produce “Kallenite” near the point of consumption, there will be NO HIGH COST FOR
TRANSPORTATION ; as it will be stored in the Companv’s magazines, THE ENORMOUS EXPENSE OF STORAGE
WILL ALSO BE SAVED; and as there is NO EXUDATION, NO DELETERIOUS CHEMICAL DECOMPOSITION,
AND CONSEQUENTLY NO DESTRUCTION OF STOCK, this saving alone over other explosives will make ah and-
some profit to the manufacturers of “ Kallenite.” r
Of the keeping qualities—a very important point to be considered—Major Quinby says :
“ The original samples of ‘ Kallenite ’ at first stood a heat test of only four minutes ; but now they stand ope of
twentv-six minutes, or more than six times the amount they did at first.* ‘Kallenite,’ he ventured to say, WOULD
NEVER DETERIORATE, BUT WOULD IMPROVE BY AGE AND KEEPING.
“This was a most valuable fact, and was characteristic of no other powder.
“‘KALLENITE,’ he considered, ‘WAS THE SIMPLEST EXPLOSIVE TO MANUFACTURE IN THE
WORLD.’”
* It is to be noted that the heat-tests above referred to were applied TO THE SAME SAMPLES with the
following results, viz. :
When the samples were newly made... ... ... ... 4 minutes
AFTER HAVING BEEN KEPT FOR 4 MONTHS 26 MINUTES.
Another most important, feature of the process is the fact that what w r as at first considered to be only a
waste product in the manufacture of the blasting explosives was found, as a result of the careful and patient
experiments, to produce the most perfect smokeless powder for sporting and military purposes ever seen by
experimenters, so that, in addition to what it was at first sought to attain, the Company should produce and
supply an absolutely smokeless gunpowder in sufficient quantities to satisfy all demands. The material of which
this fine and valuable pow’der is made is in reality but a by-product of the “Kallenite” process, and conse-
quently can be manufactured very economically, and sold at a large profit.
It is estimated that to purchase a suitable site, to erect the necessary buildings, with the most modern
and up-to-date machinery for the manufacture of all classes of explosives, “Kallenite,” cordite, smokeless
powder, blasting-powder, etc., and to provide materials, and cover working costs for extensive operations,
£40,000 will be more than sufficient.
Briefly summarised, the following advantages may be claimed
“ KALLENITE,” in the various grades in which it can be manufactured, is, considering all the qualities required
or desirable for various uses, the best-known explosive.
“ KALLENITE ” is practically smokeless and fumeless.
“ KALLENITE ”is stronger than any other nitro-glyoerine compound. There is no residue.
“ KALLENITE ” is locally made of Australian products, and can be manufactured cheaper at a profit than any
other nitro-glycerine compound.
“KALLENITE ” is as safe to handle as any other nitro-glycerine compound, and becomes more safe, instead of
more dangerous, with keeping.
“ KALLENITE,” being stronger, safer, and cheaper, should command the entire Australasian trade, with a hand-
some profit on the total capital of the Company.
“ KALLENITE,” by reason of its superiority and local production, should command the Government require-
ments for National defence.
The only contract entered into on behalf of the Company is one dated the 12th day of April, 1899, between T. H.
Kelly, G. W. Bell, Walter Sully, and R. N. Kirk of the one part, and the Company of the other part.
The abovementioned contract may be inspected at the Office of the Company.
Full Prospectuses, with forms of application and copies of Government and other reports, may be obtained from
the Brokers of the Company.
The Company will be considered formed as soon as 40,000 shares have been applied for.
The whole of the money subscribed, less a sum not exceeding £lOOO, and expenses of brokerage and flotation and
legal expenses, will be placed to the credit of the Company.

How American Axes are Made.
The American axe has been the pioneer’s
greatest mechanical ally in Australia. In clearing,
splitting, cutting timber for mills, and in a hun-
dred other operations the light, keen chapping
tool that comes from America has been premier
for many years. The superiority of a first-class
axe is ensured by using superior metal and skill in
its manufacture, and there is a great deal more in
the simple-looking tool than appears to the eye
The Collins Axe is a triumph in this respect and
has earned a great name among bushmen. At the
recent Royal Agricultural Society’s Show in Syd-
ney, J. M. Mackinolty won the champion-
ship (and the special prize presented by
Messrs. M. Moss and Co., of Wynyard
Lane, Sydney, on behalf of Collins and Co.)
with one of the make, and thus add'd to a list of
successes previously placed to the credit of Collins’
Axe. Some details of the manufacture of these
axes are illustrated on this page.
An axe is made in two parts, a “ poll ” or head
of iron, and a “ bitt ”or cutting blade of steel; the
place where these are joined, or welded, may be
recognised by a faint line a few inch' s, above the
edge of the axe. Axe polls or heads are made
either by punching the eyes out of solid iron, or
by welding together the ends of blanks of iron
that have been bent around to form the eye. Polls
with the eyes punched out are the strongest and
best. Collins’ American Pattern axes have
punched polls. Axe bits or cutting blades are
made either by splitting or opening the iron, in-
serting the steel blank (fig. 3), and then welding
the iron and steel solid as one piece, or by welding
a thin envelope of steel (fig. 4) over the iron,
whereby is secured a handsome exterior with a
large display of steel surface with but little wear
or durability.
The following illustrations clearly show the re-
sults of the two methods of manufacture.
Fig. 3
Represents the prepared
steel ready for inserting
into the iron. Weight for
a genuine Collins
axe, 12oz.
Fig. 4
Represents an “ overcoat ”
steel prepared for welding:
over the iron. Weight for
a axe, only 4oz.
Iron.
B
Steel.
Fig. 1
Represents a section
through the middle of an
axe, made with a solid
punched poll, and with
steel inserted in and
welded to the iron. Col-
lins axes are so made.
Steel.
Fig. 2
Represents a, section
through the middle of an
axe, made with a welded
poll, and having the steel
welded over the iron.
“Overcoat” axes are so
made.
Axes made as described in figure 2, having th<
steel welded over the iron, appear to have a large
amount of steel in them, while in reality the steel
available for a cutting edge only extends from D
to E, and is only 3oz. of the 4oz. used, while in
axes with inserted steel, as described in figure 1, a
cutting edge is available until all the steel is worn
from B to A, which in an axe stamped Collins and
Co., Hartford, is not less than 12oz. in a 3£lb axe,
thus yielding more than three times the wear oi
steel applied as in figure 2.

THE ILLUSTRATED LONDON NEWS of
Sept. 28, 1895, says:
“ If I were asked which single medicine I should
prefer to take abroad with me, as likely to be
most generally useful, to the exclusion of all
others, I should say Chlorodyne. I never travel
without it, and its general applicability to the
relief of a large number of simple ailments forms
its best recommendation.”
Melb. Argus reads Minister Taverner a
lecture on what he will learn and unlearn by visit-
ing Britain. Its readers are asked to conceive
what Taverner’s feelings will be when he looks
upon the old country’s activity and notes “ the
infinitesimal interference by the State.” Taverner
probably will be struck, though not quite as the
Argus expects. After his trunks have been over-
hauled by State Custom House officers, and he
has travelled in State-inspected railways, he may
ride in a hansom licensed by the State, and be
stuck-up by a State policeman who is regulating
the traffic. He will stay in a State-licensed hotel,
will go to a theatre where he will see a play which
has been submitted to a State censor and is pro-
duced in a State-licensed building. He will find
in army of €k>vt. inspectors supervising every
factory he enters, he may visit a State-regulated
coal-mine, he will tumble across State health-
inspectors at every turn, he may go to a State
church on Sunday, and look at some compulsory
State schools on Monday, and ride there in a
municipal (i.e., a local State) tram, mix municipal
water with his whisky, and read his evening
paper by municipal gas-light, and wash in a
municipal bath, and go round and see the work-
men’s houses owned and built by the municipality.
And then he can look at the State telegraphs and
the State-regulated Bank of England, and come
home again.
Mr. Wong Tape, educated at Otago High
School, M.L., lately returned after a long visit to
the Flowery Land, and has unburdened his
heathen heart to a Maoriland paper regarding
missionaries
They are a great factor for evil in the relations between
our people and foreigners. Those of our people who
travel see quite clearly that the Western nations require
Christianising quite as much as our people. lam well
acquainted with the lives of our lower classes, and I am
quite certain they live on a higher moral plane than
European people of the same class, and vve want to know
why you don’t cure yourselves before going in for healing
others. There are more vice and immorality in a large
European city than in a Chinese city of an equal popula-
tion. Our faith has sufficed us for thousands of years.
We have held it while you have changed yours several
times. Besides, you Westerns have not yet decided
among yourselves what it really is you believe.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
11. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer 1
of opal in Australia, and opal miners, will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
A severe reproof from wayback :
Ed. Bulletin. —Paragraph (18 2 '99) urging suppression:
of the “ newspaper bludger” reflects discreditably upon
the would-be exclusives of the journalistic press. The
badge suggested as a method of designating duly-
accredited press representatives would, by the writer’s
own showing, merely be an advt. soliciting those ques-
tionable favors shared in by the bludgers aforesaid. Also,
said badge would savor of degrading mendicancy as a
means of participating, “ free gratis,” in the gorge-and-
swill functions characteristic of an age of swinish public
display. And who are the journalistic representatives
claiming thus exclusively all the privileges alleged to per-
tain to the Fourth Estate—specially the right to public
bounty in the shape of bribes from foolish aspirants for
newspaper jam ? According to the par. under notice, the
“ bludger” is a loafer without a license ; the fellow who
needs the badge is the legitimate deadhead. “The ill-
odour in which pressmen are held by some people ” may
be due to bogus journalists ; but the well-merited con-
tempt in which average pressmen are held by many in-
telligent persons is due to characteristics too odiously
prominent to need further mention. Such are the
would-be exclusives who purpose joining an organised
Bohemian Olub ! The present writer was connected
with a newspaper, wrote its leaders, dictated its policy,
built it up into a prosperous concern, and, while
thus engaged in licking the rag into shape, educating its
nominal editor, and acting as bear-leader for its reporter,
who was the proprietor-editor’s brother, the literary
laborer never attended gorges or public functions in a
press capacity. The nominal editor and his brother posed
as the authors of all the original matter published in the
rag ; and many free-lances who are, or have been, in like
case, do not grudge the “bludger” his share of the
relinquished bribe proffered by folly and claimed by
knavery. After all, ’tis only natural that such jealousies
should find open expression. The journalistic press being
mendacious, parasitic, and socially pernicious, the tool
of the quack, the abortionist, and the commercial fraud,
the mouthpiece of blatant ignorance and the veriest slave
of the Mammon of Unrighteousness, it is not at all sur-
prising that its inferior mercenaries regard the “bludger”
as a dangerous rival.— Frank.

Legal Notice.
Notice of testamentary
BEQUESTS.
The Late LAMES WEIR
of Saint Clouds Blackrock in tiie County of Dublin
Esquire by his Will dated 12th of October 1893
bequeathed the following legacies amongst others
namely:—One thousand pounds to Mrs. Rachel
Sandiland ; One thousand pounds to the children
of the late Andrew Brown ; One thousand pounds
to each of the seven children of Testator’s wife’s
sister Isabella Fallow, namely James Fallow,
William Fallow, John Fallow, Gavin Fallow,
Janet Fallow, Mary Fallow, and Agnes Fallow.
One thousand pounds to Mrs. Grace Barr,
formerly Steele. One thousand pounds in equal
shares amongst the children of John Steel son of
Mary Steel; One thousand pounds to the issue
living at the death of the Testator of James Weir
who died in his lifetime ; One thousand pounds to
each of the six children of Testator’s brother
Thomas Weir deceased, namely James Weir,
Francis Weir, Andrew Weir, Agnes Weir, Eliza-
beth Weir, and Margaret Weir.
The Testator recited that some of the persons
who might be entitled to legacies under his Will
were, the Testator believed, resident in America,
Australia and New Zealand, and it contained the
following clause: “ I hereby declare that every
person entitled to a legacy under this my Will
shall within a period of three years from the day
of my death send to my Trustee or Trustees a
claim (in writing) for such legacy and any person
who shall not send in such claims as aforesaid
shall absolutely forfeit all benefit which he or she
might have otherwise claimed under this my Will
and the Legacy hereinbefore bequeathed to him or
her shall be dealt with as a lapsed legacy.” And
the Testator directed his Trustees as soon as con-
veniently might be after his death to cause
advertisements to be inserted in newspapers
requiring all persons entitled to legacies to send in
their claims. The Testator died 30th October,
1898, and said Will and one Codicil were on the
18th November, 1898, duly proved by Sir William
Findlater, Robert Bell, James Anderson Merry
the Executors and Trustees thereof.
Now all such of above-named Legatees as have
not already done so and all other (if any) persons
claiming to be Legatees or to be beneficially entitled
under the said Will and Codicil are hereby re-
quired to send in the said Executors and Trustees
at the office of the undersigned solicitors claims
(in writing) and the necessary proofs of identifica-
tion, and the Trustees will at the expiration of a
period of three years after the death finally wind
up the Estate and distribute the then remaining
assets of the Testator without reference to any
claims except those which shall have been sent
in within said period. The Executors have col-
lected and realised a very large proportion of the
Testator’s assets and they have paid the Testator’s
debts and thus will shortly be in a position to pay
the above mentioned legacies and to distribute
their Testator’s Estate (except of epurse such part
of the residuary property as it may be necessary
to retain three years).
The Executors believe that they already possess
the addresses of the abovenamed legatees but
for extra precaution and in pursuance of their
Testator’s directions publish this advertisement
and particularly request each named or indicated
legatee to send in a formal written claim without
further delay.
Dated at Sydney this 10th day of April, 1899.
W. W. BILLY A RD ANDREWS and MOSE-
LEY, Solicitors, 12 Castlereagh-street, Sydney,
Agents for WILLIAN FIN DLATER and Com-
pany, Solicitors for the Executors, 53 Dame-
street, Dublin.

Statutory Declaration.
I, THOS. MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 327 Elizabeth-
street, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows:
Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried
several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.

mussen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,
which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare COM-
PLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and although
this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since.
The ALFALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true.
Declared at Sydney this 29th day of April, 1898.
THOMAS MUDIMAN.

B. R. Wise on the Cabbage-Tree Mob and
co. :
The opponents of Federation said trust the people pro-
spectively, but in the same breath characterised the
people of the other provinces as plunderers and robbers.
If the angel Gabriel came down from heaven and offered
them a constitution, some newspapers would fight
against it. They had all sorts of faddists and extremists
arrayed against them—single-taxers, socialists, and men
of that extreme stamp. The Domain orators were fond-
ling over Dr. MacLaurin and Mr. Jack Haynes in loving
embrace, and gloating over the discovery of the admir-
able qualities they had just discovered in each other.
The object of these combinations was to prevent
a reasonable basis of union on any terms. They
wanted a union that would give them Sydney as
the capital, that would better all their posi-
tions and cost them nothing extra. It was a heaven-
born Constitution they wanted. If they recalled
the narratives of the Old Testament they would re-
member how an experiment in this direction was tried.
A Constitution was given on Mount Sinai to the great
leader of the Jewish people, but how were Moses and
Aaron received ? . . . Provincialists throughout all time
were of the same type, and used the same language.
First, there w'as an outbreak of rebellion, because it was
given out by Moses and Aaron that the worship of the
golden calf would have to be discontinued, and the idols
destroyed. It was the golden calf image that was stand-
ing in the way of some Sydney people just now.
Dubberke’s friend, Pratt, who scooped the
=£sso reward in the boot-trunk mystery case, is
said to have deft Melbourne for parts unknown.
Anyhow, his'offic-e knows him not at present, and
his solicitor declines to acquaint the police with
his whereabouts.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue. *

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Mai, vox, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir,—For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene,”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Salts,” and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY:—
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy, “ Eczema Capitis,” from
which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M'Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliene,
and after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is defeated, and I am perfectly well. I will only
add that since I commenced the treatment of mvself I have
prescribed it to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours
sincerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

Al!
High, clear and high, the soaring skylark sings
Love ! Love I Love ! the joy of life and woe :
Throbs, throbs his heart, as upward on thrilling wings
Far, far he soars from this dim world below.
Was it a skylark’s voice or a soul’s triumphant song
We heard in the days gone by in the woods of Dande-
nong?
Rose, lovely rose—a fairer rose was she —
Rose, white rose, I kiss your tender leaves 1
Speak, speak, speak, O soul-white rose for me,
Say, say to her ray heart in silence grieves.
Lonely ami sad it grieves amidst the careless throng . . .
Ah, green are the waving trees in the woods of Dande-
nong 1
Star, crystal star, shining where angels be,
Bright,,bright star—yet brighter were her eyes—
Ai! Ai ! Ai! Star of my life was she !
Shine, gently shine where low her bright head lies.
And ah, but the world is cold and the way is dark and
long ;
And oh, that we u-ere once more in the woods of Dande-
nong.
V.J.D.,

THEN IT WENT ON ALL RIGHT.
The writer of the letter which I am going to
copy for you in a moment has a complaint to
make. Rather, perhaps, a complaint to place on
record, as the reason for it is passed away for the
present, and she hopes—and we hope with her-=.
that it may not return. The complaint does not
refer to any relative, friend, or foe, but to her Own
heart. It did not work well. It was weak, and
for a long time she was unable to find means to
make it do better, Which was a serious matter,
inasmuch as the vigor of the circulation of the
blood always depends upon the force wherewith
the heart drives it.
Still, it seems to me wb Ought to be a bit indul-
gent towards the heart in view of the labor it has
to perform. Remember that it never takes a full
minute’s rest at one time, night or day, from the
instant it begins at your birth until, like a muffled
drum, it stops for good and all—life’s funeral
march to the grave being over, Luring all this
While, ten years or a hundred, the heart has got to
keep on pumping blood through your body at the
rate of from 130 strokes«a minute in childhood to
§0 or 60 in old age. If you happen to have ft
mechanical turn of mind, it may interest you to
figure out how much this stands for in units of
horse-power for a given case and time. If not,
you can take my word for it that, merely as a
machine, the heart deserves your respect. So long
as it goes ahead steadily, up hill and down dale,
hammering away softly but strongly, you haven’t
a word to say for or against it; but when it begins
to get weak, maybe skipping a stitch now and
then, you call in the doctor, who puts the tip of
his finger just below the base of your left thumb,
looks wise and solemn (as befits the occasion), and
says, “ Ah, yes, yes ; I see, I see.” But what does
he see ? He doesn’t tell you that ; he leaves medi-
cine, and mentions when he will iook in again.
But as to the letter I spoke of. “For many
years,” the lady says, “ I suffered from indigestion
and weak heart. Very little exertion made me
feel weary and tired. Cold, clammy sweats broke
over me. I had a poor appetite, and after meals
an aching pain at the chest and a miserable sink-
ing feeling at the stomach. I had also much pain
at the left side, and my heart would flutter so as
to frighten me. At length I became so weak I
was barely able to get about, being no longer able
to do my housework.
“ Owing to the trouble at my heart I obtained
no proper rest at night, and often walked about
my bedroom at night. Many times these attacks
were so bad I thought I was dying. During the
day a sense of suffocation sometimes came upon
me, and I was obliged to go to the door for fresh
air.
“Year after year I suffered like this; now a
little better, now as bad as I could be. In No-
vember, 1887, while on a visit to Croydon, my son-
in-law persuaded me to try Mother Seigel’s Syrup.
He got me a bottle, and after taking it I experi-
enced great relief. The pain at my heart was
easier, and I felt better as a whole. I could eat
well, and the food agreed with me.
“ I now felt encouraged to continue using this
remedy. Soon I was in better health than for
years, the heart trouble having disappeared alto-
gether. Since that time when I feel anything
ailing me a few doses of Mother Seigel’s Syrup
never fail to give the desired relief. I have told
many persons of the benefit I have derived from
it, and hereby consent to your publishing this
statement should you wish to do so.”—(Signed)
(Mrs.) William Harrington, near Wickford Hill,
Clare, Suffolk, November 12, 1897.
Now what ailed Mrs. Harrington’s heart ? Why,
precisely the same things that ailed her lungs, her
nerves and her muscles — weakness. Therein she
is right. It was a weak heart, but not a diseased
heart. The heart is a muscle, and (seeing the pro-
digious lot of work it has to do) necessarily a
strong, active muscle. But it will not work with-
out pay any more than you or I will. With all the
rest of the body, it has got to be sustained and
strengthened by food. Here we have the point,
then. The lady was afflicted with chronic indiges-
tion. For this reason her whole body grew weak
—the heart, of course, with other parts of the
engine. Hence all the symptoms she names. Her
immense all-round weakness and puller-down is
that same old dyspepsia. When Mother Seigel’s
Syrup made the digestion of plenty of food pos-
sible, the heart went on all right, like a newly-
wound clock.’'

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline.thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS'S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
