The Case Of
IVBrs. Z. DEAM.
(F, •om the “Ken t ion Independent .”)
Mrs. Dean, of Cowrie-street, Newtown,
Sydney, now wears an exceptional appear-
ance of robust health, and our reporter was
particulaily impressed by her vivacity and
brightness of manner. Consequently, it
was a matter of some astonishment to the
newspaper man to hear from that lady’s
own lips that a short time ago she was
suffering greatly, and in such a serious
nervous condition that she never expected
to live through it.
“Yes,” said Mrs. Dean, “hope seemed
deul. One after another I had tried all the
medicines and pills that are so widely
advertised as nerve cures. They were all
ghastly failures. I believe such things
would be total failures in every case.”
‘ How so, Mrs. Dean ? ”
“ Because I’ve made enquiries, end have
been so informed by people who ought to
know. Once a gentleman who has a special
knowledge of medicines called here and I
took the opportunity of enquiring as to a
very widely advertised medicine which is
guaranteed to cure nerve affections —in fact,
it is warranted to cure every illness.”
“Yes, responded the reporter,” I think I
know what you refer to. I know the game;
they assert they cure everything—but tell
me what this gentleman said.”
“He said, ‘I know what the nostrum in
question is; it is a delusion, and entirely
ineffective,’ and that I should have got just
as much relief if I had taken as many clay
pills. You may guess my astonishment and
chagrins Here I had been taking this stuff
on the faith of the statements put forward
in print, hoping day by day to find an
improvement in my condition, only to find
in the end that I had been grossly deceived,
and was cramming myself with useless
rubbish. And I have reason to believe,
pursued Mrs. Dean confidentially, that all
these new fangled catch-penny medicines
now so widely advertised are all useless—
these people only want your money—as to
your health, little they care about that, for
they are here to-day and gone to-morrow,
to seek fresh fields and pastures new in
other count)ies, after having fooled all the
gullible people to be got hold of here. ”
“ Did you try many medicines? ”
“ Yes, I tried them all. I also went to
see certain people who advertise themselves
specialists'in debility complaints.”
“ How did you get on with them? ”
“ Well, the least said about them the
better—their advice was about as good as
the other things I was talking about.”
‘‘ But doctors, Mrs. Dean the doctors—
it is the business of these gentleman to cure
such maladies as yours ? ”
“The doctors’me icines did not appear
to touch the cause of my complaint.”
“ Now, Mrs, Dean,” said the newspaper
man, “ kindly give the particular symptoms
of your illness —how did it start, for
instance ? ”
“ I was sitting upstairs one day, when
suddenly I felt a most peculiar sensation in
my legs—it was as though they were numb
and paralyzed, and I had hardly any feeling
in them. I tried to get down stairs but was
utterly unable to do so, and I had to call out
to my daugh er to help me. That was the
beginning of my troubles. For many
months after that I was in a perilous state. I
continually suffered from nervous headaches.
My nerves got worse and worse till I
daren’t go out alone. Sometimes this
sensation would come over me , whilst
walking in the streets or riding on a tram.
I should start trembling. 'I hings would go
hazy before my vision and all feeling would
disappear from my lower limbs. It would
feel just as though the whole world were
giving way under me. hy, on these
occasions I posti\ ely couldn’t see straight.
Seeing wasn’t believing then, I can assure
you, for I couldn’t even trust my eye sight.”
“Can you describe the sensation more
fully?”' w =
“No, I hardly think I can. It was a
terribly low, ‘ all-gone ’ sensation —it was
horrible, and I fairly turn cold now when I
think of it. Frequently I turned giddy.
The merest shock would start my nerves
shaking. Any one coining suddenly into
the house, for instance, or anyone slamming
the door, would cause me to go all over »
tremble.”
“ How did you rest ’ ”
“That question is answered in three words
*1 was sleepless,’ replied Mrs. Dean.
Night after night I passed without sleeping,
and at length I got into such a state of
trepidation that I daren’t allow myself to
be left alone anywhere under any circum-
stances. So weak were my nerves that I
was not able to read the paper. I was so
easily excited by anything unusual which
appeared in the paper. The account of a
fatal accident or anything of that nature
would affect me severely. As I have
already told you, I dare not go out by my-
self, but sometimes, when my husband would
return from business, I would cling to his
arm and get a short distance. This
continued debility of my nervous system
resulted in an intense nervous depression.
I became horribly low-spirited. I lost my
usual cheerful spirits entirely, and felt I
didn’t care what happened to me. I took a
gloomy and melancholy view of everything.
I didn’t even trouble to eat. I never
wanted any food. It was placed before me
and I ate what I could mechanically. I had
no interest in anything, and, as I’ve said, I
never expected to recover. My daughter
can tell you that I’ve not overtold my tale
of misery.”
“Indeed no,”saidthisyounglady, who was
present; “all mamma says is quite true, andone
of us had to stay with her both night and
day. It was a bad case of nervous pros
tration, so the doctors told us.”
‘ I changed terribly in appearance,” con-
tinued Mrs. Dean. “My nearest friends
would hardly have known me, and my face
wore a haggard and anxious look. It was a
bit of bad luck that I didn’t take Clements
Tonic sooner than I did, because, you see,
I should have been saved all these months
of suffering, and I should have taken it
sooner, doubtless, but for my husband;
though, to tell you the truth, it was he who
obtained Clements Tonic for me in the end.
You see, explained Mrs. Dean, he had
j taken Clements Tonic, and it cured him of
indigestion in quite a marvellous way, but
he was under the impression that Clements
Tonic was an indigestion cure, and nothing
more. Of course he was wrong, for when
he did obtain me a bottle, and I hazarded a
j few doses, the change in me was astonishing.
! I can’t exactly explain the good that I found
Clements Tonic was doing me, but it felt
something like being lightened up and
I pulled together. It braced me up like a
b »th. I began to feel bright and iapp/
again, and to rejoice in my returning heauai.
I ate my food with a zest, slept peacefully
and long at nights, and once more could
walk the streets without tremor, help or
fear. I kept on taking Clements Tonic till
my cure was complete, and my nerves were
as strong and unfailing as when I was a girl
I tell you I am really grateful, and I have
already written a private note to tin
proprietors of Clements Tonic expressing
my gratitude.”
“ Can we print what you have said ? ”
“You may publish it as you think fit.”

Notes Celestial.
BY AN AUSTRALIAN IN CHINA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tint introduction of railways into China has
provided fresh pastures for the Celestial Bill
Sikes. Accompanied by a screwdriver he will
travel in a train car; and if every screw and piece
of brass-work doesn’t leave that car with him it
will only be because the screw driver was no good.
When travelling on a Chinese railway I never
venture to sleep; if I did I should wake up with
nothing left me but my shirt.
* * * •
It was at an amateur dramatic performance by
the European “ Naicest ” in Shanghai—the Paris
of the East and the wickedest city in the world.
During the course of the play one of the mummers
had to be considerably battered and then killed ;
but while this process was going on, a pretty
Chinese girl in the stalls jumped up and sereanred
out “ Wha’ foh you kill my boss ! ” The amateur
mummer’s wife made unpleasant enquiries, and
even divorce-proceedings were talked of. But it’s
a custom of the country.
» * * *
Some little time ago an eminent Chinese actor
appeared by Imperial command before the Son of
Heaven at the Palace, and wishing to tickle the
Royal ribs, introduced a suit of European clothes
into one of his acts, putting the Vest over the coat
and the seat of the trousers in front, and thus
highly amusing the blue-blooded audience. But
on leaving the Palace the poor mummer forgot to
take the clothes with him, and they were found
several days after by one of the courtiers, who,
enraged, at the idea of having been so long within
a mile of anything foreign, reported the matter to
His Celestial Majesty, who immediately ordered
the actor’s arrest. The culprit was taken to the
Palace and —beheaded !
• * * *
Which energetic proceeding recalls what hap-
pened when the Chinese reformer, Kang Yu Way,,
thought it advisable, for the sake of his health, to
quit Peking, and the Empress Dowager sent a
gunboat in pursuit of Kang’s steamer. The gun-
boat had to put into Ctiofoo to re-coal, and,
failing therefore to catch up with the fugitive,
the captain returned to the capital to report. He
will never “report” again.
* * * *
The lighting system of Peking is worth munici-
pal attention. A certain sum —about <£looo—is
set aside for the lighting of the'city streets. The
places where the lamp-posts ought to be are easy
enough to find—the sites are there right enough—
but if you walked from one end of the city to the
other, you would never find a public light. And
yet the money is regularly set aside from the Im-,
perial Treasury for the purpose.
* * * *
Was living for. a time in one of the Yamens, or
official residences, at Wuchang, 700 miles up the
Yangtze. There were missionaries in the city,
but, being engaged on important business, I, of
course, carefully avoided these gentlemen: because
you cannot do business in China if you associate
with missionaries. The mandarins did their ut-
most to make me comfortable, even going so far
as to-engage a cook (at my expense) who said he
knew all about European cookery. I bore with
this fraud until he put a bottle of lavender-water
on the table, and insisted that it was vinegar.
Then I battered him, and upon one of the
mandarins interfering, 1 bashed him, and had to
leave Wuchang hurriedly.
* • * •
Copy of a circular issued by one of the leading
Japanese drapery establishments in Yokohama
Respected Sir,—We beg to call your attention to our
shirts which we are selling at 20 per cent, below the
lowest prices of our enemies. Our goods are made from
the superflnest best shirtings and linens ; and the work-
manship without lies is unsurpassed. We cannot under-
take orders but our shirts being made on the scale of
prime, principal English and American manufactures, we
can promise to fit anyone of normal dimensions.—Yours
affectionately, Sho-Ri-Tkn.
* * •' *
The Canton executioners are complaining that
unless they get either more pay, or more
“patients” they will starve. They have asked
that in future they be paid 1000 cash (about 2s.) —
instead 500 cash, as heretofore—per head, in order
to’make up for the slump in business. The official
interviewed stated that though he could not grant
their request he was convinced their business
would very soon be brisker.
* * * *
It was in one of the few Provinces of the Middle
Kingdom where nominal Christianity is strong,
and where the ruling mandarin was entirely with
the Lord. A complicated case came up for hear-
ing: Shu Yao-fung said that So Ting-tsin owed
him 600 strings of cash ; So Ting-tsin said that on
the contrary Shu Yao-fung owed him 100 strings.
Shu was honest; his claim was just, and in other
circumstances would doubtless have been allowed.
But at the hearing So claimed to be a mission
preacher, and said that he trusted to the Lord to
bring him out all right. Decision accordingly
given against the man who was not with the Lord.
And yet the world wonders what is the cause of
the occasional attacks on missionaries !
* * * *
Li Hung Chang relates that at Manchester he
dined with a gentleman, who was chairman of a
large company there, and that he (Li), wishing to
be polite in the Chinese way, asked his host what
salary he drew for his services. The chairman
replied that he received absolutely nothing ; but,
observing his guest’s incredulity, added, “ Well, I
occasionally receive a little present; ” and pointed
to a valuable diamond ring on his finger. “ Well,”
commented Li, with the Celestial equivalent for
a wink, “you could sell that for a large price, and
anything that will bring money is money ! ”
• * * *
The reward of virtue ! A Japanese daily news-
paper, the Yorodzu, of Tokyo, is reaping the
recompense for its efforts on behalf of public
morality. Since publishing a list of the concu-
bines kept by men of note in Tokyo, including
prominent Government officials, bankers, and
merchants, it has increased its list of subscribers —
mostly ladies ! —by 4000, the total circulation now
amounting to 80,000. And the editor is over-
whelmed with letters of thanks from wives whom
he has enlightened about the little tricks of their
husbands.
Shanghai, February.
GUMSUCKER.
Through the White Horse
Rapids.
“ OHILOAT,” an Australian, pictures to The
Bulletin some aspects of the road to Klondike: —
The journey to Dawson Oity, through the series of
lakes and rivers, is full of varied experiences and sur-
prises, and a man must look very sharp to escape mis-
adventure in some shape. For instance, in the “ White
Horse Rapids,” four miles in length, the fall of the
river is no less than 800 ft., and even after passing the
rapids we have drifted 100 miles in 16 hours. All the
waters of the lakes and rivers, before arriving at White
Horse, have to force their way through a straight narrow
passage about a quarter of a mile long and only 10yds.
wide, between high, perpendicular walls of basalt; and
so great is the compression that during its passage
through this canyon the centre of the stream is fully five
feet higher than the sides.
Safety depends upon the steerer keeping the course
straight, right on top of the mass of tumbling and seeth-
ing waves—should the boat swerve towards either side it
becomes a spinning-top, and a capsize is inevitable.
After emerging from this canyon, a number of falls have to
be negotiated, the series ending at the White Horse
proper, where, also, the last and greatest leap has to be
encountered, 10 to 13 feet deep, surrounded by powerful
eddies and sharp-pointed rocks. Here it is that so many
have lost their lives.
The miners usually land at the head of Miles Canyon be-
fore attempting the rapids to watch how other boats fare
at that last dangerous leap, and great is the cheering when
a boat comes safely through. I myself sat for. hours on the
rocks anxiously watching each boat as it ran the gauntlet.
While there I noticed a woman standing alone, a little
way from me, wringing her hands and weeping, and as
the roaring of the. water prevented her from hearing my
shout I went to her, but all that my questioning could
elicit was, “My husband ! my husband 1 ’’—pointing at
same time towards the rapids. I was imagining all sorts
of disasters, when she suddenly clasped my arm hard and
with wildly-staring eyes pointed to a solitary boat
approaching the fall. It was all over in a moment, and I
thought the woman would have gone mad with joy when
she saw the boat emerge safely from that chaos of water.
Laughing and crying in one breath, clapping her hands
and patting me on the shoulder—l confess that when she
hurried away towards the spot where the boat was trying
to land, I felt a lump in my throat which took many
swallows to gulp down.
Next “boat” to dare the leap was an extraordinary
structure—a mere raft of logs, cigar-shaped, with a sort
of turret in the centre, in which the provisions- were
stored. At the pointed end of the raft stood a man hold-
ing on to a pole from which a small American flag flut-
tered, and the man steering was dressed in a belltopper
and frock-coat, tails of which flapped gaily behind him,
while painted on the craft in big, black letters was her
name, “ The Holy Terror.” .
Near the rapids are many graves, some marked by
wooden slabs, telling who lies underneath; others with-
out rhyme or name. One grave was pointed out to me as
that of a man who, after losing outfit and boat at the
last fall, barely escaping with his life, returned to Skag-
way, obtained another outfit, and had another try, when
the boat again capsized, and he himself was rescued with
difficulty. After recovering a little, he borrowed a
revolver—and the slab at the head, of his grave is in-
scribed^“ Similia similibus cu/raritur.”
CHILCAT.
“ The election at Hythe, owing to retire-
ment of the sitting member, ” writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent, “was striking
evidence of the growing power of the Jew in
England. It was the first time a seat had Been
contested for by two openly - acknowledged
Hebrews. Sir Edward Sassoon, a long-eyed,
drooping-nosed, fragile, Conservative, Persian-
Bombay Jew, vanquished Sir Israel Hart, a
plump little Radical robin recbchqeked, nasal-y-
-hooked Petticoat-laner. Had either put his hand
over his nose and said, ‘ Vat bart of Ireland do I
come from ? ’ a child would have guessed it in one.
Oh, yes, they were Jews, and with enough shekels
to announce it on the platform to prevent mis-
takes. Sassoon married Baron Gus 'Rothschild’s
daughter, and Hart was married by a lady so
emblazoned with pods of pearls, searchlights of
diamonds, temperance-processions of turquoises,
and conflagrations of rubies that when she ‘ took
the chair’at his meetings the Folkestone voters
went blind—at the adjoining pub. In London
the Jew is now more over-powering and all-per-
vading than ever.”
Perhaps, outside Mt. Gambier, in the
S.E. of S.A., there isn’t an Australian town where
one stands in danger of being tossed out of the
roadway and over a fence by a stray stag. And
it doesn’t happen often even there. One lordly
creature, however, wandered in the other day, and
threw an elderly lady on top of her woodheap
He was, apparently, an escapee from Yallir.u
Park, where there is a large herd which is only
with difficulty kept within bounds.
John Wilkie, one of the three brothers
who made a mint of money out of W. A. railway
contracts, has been selected as Liberal candidate
for Patea against Geo. Hutchison. The latter
has had a varied political career. He has stood
against Atkinson, Bryce, Bruce (Tories), and Bal-
lance (Liberal), caused a hullaballoo against \V hit-
taker and other leading Conservatives over their
connection with the 8.N.Z., and was responsible
for some of the bitter attacks on Ward when he
succumbed with the Colonial Bank of N.Z. Was
challenged to a fight in the lobbies by John
M'Kenzie for his charges of nepotism against Big
John, and was partly suppressed by his own
leader, Russell, for his ridiculous allegation against
Seddon of being alias Bun Tuck. Hutchison a
copyist of Chamberlain, but cant manage the
monocle. Also, he is an unreliable political
friend and a very reliable enemy. When Reeves
was in the House of Reps, he was always told off
to reply to George, and their encounters-eontained
the best speeches M.L. Parliament has heard since
the days of its political giants.
When Ward and Reeves dropped out, and
the Maoriland Government was patched by the
addition of Thomas Thompson and Hyphenated
Hall-Jones, Seddon’s luck was distinctly out.
Thompson and Hyphenated Jones are, two utter
failures. Thompson broke his record of clumsi-
ness the other day. A deputation, headed; by
Opposition member Wilson, wanted something.
Thompson said it couldn’t be granted on account
of .an Act which he himself had carried through
only last session. Was asked name of Act, and
Thompson couldn’t remember. It was a ridiculpus
situation, and' the deputation looked surprised,
and the inflammable Thompson suddenly blazed
up and called the deputation a eub and other hard
names ; in fact, to all intents and purposes, he
offered to spank the deputation on the spot. It
begins to look as if Joseph Ward, of the Colonial
Bank disaster, who, it is printed, has paid his
Maoriland debts in full, and is paying of his
English ones, was about due to rejoin the Maori-
land Ministry. Buckley is dead, and Reeves is
in England, and the Thomas Thompson and
Hyphenated Jones element is weighing the
Government down.

Kidney Diseases do not prostrate a man the first day, possibly not the first year. They
are the most mysterious of all diseases. But, at the same time, as surely fatal as consump-
tion if neglected.
The first approaches of kidney disease are seldom painful. Slight dizziness, sleeplessness,
irregular heart, highly colored or scanty urine, are often disregarded or ascribed to some other
cause. These symptoms, and others, may even pass away, and the patient says to himself, “ I
am all right now.” This is a fatal mistake.
Soon the symptoms return, in a little stronger form, and last longer. Nature is giving
the patient plenty of warning. If he heeds these warnings in time he may save his life ; if
not, death is as certain as it would be had he taken arsenic or strychnine. Will the reader
have confidence in a medicine that’s made to cure kidney diseases ? That is curing them all
the time here in Sydney, and is always endorsed by Sydney people ? Is it not better to trust
the medicine that’s made for Kidney diseases, rather than boastful medicines that say “ I cure
everything, from consumption to measles ” ?

has the following to say :—“ I have been suffering for the past eight years from pains in my back and kidneys, also from weakness
and giddiness. In consequence, I had to neglect my business, and sometimes have been confined to my bed. It was difficult for me
to walk any distance, and I could not do so without the aid of a stick. Even then I was often obliged to sit down and rest at intervals

neglected makes it just that much more so.

Virtues that Pay.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You argue—as sympathy governs your bias —
That Wisdom distributes the capon and crust;
Indulging the sinful, and stinting the pious,
Or starving the wicked, and fatting the just.
You are wrong to the Evil One : hear what I
say—
There are ruinous virtues, and Virtues that Pay.
If your purpose be saving your soul and your
bacon —
Fruition forthwith and a sweet by-and-bye ;
If your definite project stands clear and un-
shaken—
A Fatman on earth and a seraph on high ;
In working this out let it still be your lay
To cultivate only the virtues that pay.
Such virtues are not of the workshop or cloister ;
They test every act by the way it pans out ;
They prompt you to seize on the world as your
oyster,
Inserting your knife with a spirit devout.
For strait is the portal, and narrow the way,
Representing the route of the virtues that pay.
Men as good as yourself, or most probably
better,
Have gone to the rear, after many a try—
A permanent wage-slave—a usurers’ debtor—
Reduced to the motto of “Root hog or die ! ”
But their handicap dates from an earlier day,
When they failed in espousal of virtues that pay.
There is nothing outrd in the man with the
bluey ;
He started, like you, for a goal undisclosed ;
But never in life can he come within coo-ee—
Though he may reach a goal (with the vowels
transposed).
And a similar Sheol gapes fair in your way,
If you turn out deficient in virtues that pay.
You must race, like St. Paul —you must race for
the dollar—
No pause of compunction must ever intrude ;
You must watch, you must prey, never missing a
collar —
The course is severe, and the company good.
You must rev’rence the Thrift-God, and earnestly
pray
To be grounded and built up in virtues that pay.
By this means you will serve the Almighty and
Mammon,
And die in a state of salvation and wealth ;
When the clergy, without a suggestion of gam-
mon,
Will furnish your soul with a clean bill of health.
So you’ll sweep through the gates in your spotless
array,
A shining example of Virtues that Pay.
Yic.
TOM COLLINS.
On Our Selection.—His Father.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mother and Dad talking at the barn. Mother,
bare-headed, holding some eggs in her apron.
Dad leaning on a hoe.
“ I am afraid of him,” Mother said ; “ it’s not
right you should keep him about the place. No
one’s safe with such a man. Some day he’ll take
it in his head to kill us all, and then ”
“Tut, tut, woman ; poor old Jack ! he’s harm-
less as a baby.”
“ All right ” (sullenly) ; “you’ll see.”
Dad laughed and went away with the hoe on his
shoulder to cut burr.
* * • *
Middle of summer. Dad and Dave in the pad-
dock mowing lucerne. “ Cranky ” Jack sinking
post-holes for a milking-yard close to the house.
Joe at intervals stealing behind him to prick him
with straws through a rent in the rear of his
patched moleskins. Little Bill—in readiness to
run—standing off, enjoying the sport.
Inside the house sat mother and Sal, sewing and
talking of Maloney’s new baby.
“Dear me,”said mother, “it’s the tiniest mite
of a thing I ever saw ; why, bless me, anyone of
y’ at its age would have made three of ”
“ Mind ! mother,” Sal shrieked, jumping up on
the sofa. Mother screamed and mounted the
table. Both gasped for breath, and leaning cauti-
ously over peeped down at a big black snake that
had glided in at the front door. Then, pale and
scared-looking, they stared across at each other.
The snake crawled over to the safe and drank
up some milk that had been spilt on the floor.
Mother saw its full length and groaned. The
snake wriggled to the leg of the table.
“ Look out ! ” cried Sal, gathering up her skirts
and dancing about on the sofa.
Mother squealed hysterically.
Joe appeared. He laughed.
“You wretch !” mother yelled. “Run!—rim and
fetch your father.”
Joe went and brought Jack.
“Oh-h, my God [’’—mother moaned, as Jack
stood at the door, staring strangely at her. “ Kill
it—why don’t he kill it ?”
Jack didn’t move, but talked to himself.
Mother shuddered.
The reptile crawled to the bedroom door. Then
for the first time the man’s eyes rested upon it.
It glided into the bedroom, and mother and Sal
ran off for Dad.
Jack fixed his eyes on the snake and continued
muttering to himself. Several times it made an
attempt to mount the dressing-table. Finally it
succeeded. Suddenly Jack’s demeanor changed.
He threw off his ragged hat and talked wildly. A
fearful expression filled his ugly features. His
voice altered.
“You’re the devil!” he said ;“ the devil! the
devil ! The missus brought you—ah-h-h ! ”
The snake’s head passed behind the looking-
glass. Jack drew nearer, clenching his fists and
gesticulating. As he did he came full before the
looking-glass and saw, perhaps for the first time in
his life, his own image. An unearthly howl came
from him. “M e father !” he shouted, and bolted
from the house.
Dad came in with the long-handled shovel,
swung it about the room, and smashed pieces off
the cradle, and tore the bed-curtains down, and
made a great noise altogether. Finally, he killed
the snake and put it on the fire ; and Joe and the
cat watched it wriggle on the hot coals.
Meanwhile Jack, bare-headed, rushed across the
yard. He ran over little Bill, and tumbled
through the wire-fence on to the broad of his back.
He roared like a wild beast, clutched at space, and
spat, and kicked his heels in the air.
“ Let me up —ah-h-h—let gome throat ! ” he
hissed.
The dog ran over and barked at him. He found
his feet again, and, making off, ran through the
wheat, glancing back over his shoulder as he tore
along. He crossed into the grass paddock, and
running to a big tree dodged round and round it.
I lien from tree to tree he went, and that evening
at sundown, when Joe was bringing the cows
home, Jack was still flying from “ his father.”
* * * *
After supper.
“ I wonder now what the old fool saw in that
snake to send him off his head like that ?” Dad
said, gazing wonderingly into the fire. “ He sees
Plenty of them, goodness knows.”
“That wasn’t it. It wasn’t the snake at all,”
mother said ; “ there was madness in the man’s
eyes all the while. I saw it the moment he came
to the door.” She appealed to Sal.
“ Nonsense !” said Dad ; “nonsense/” and he
tried to laugh.
t| “Oh, of course it’s nonsense,” mother went on,
everything I say is nonsense. It won’t be non-
sense when you come home some day and find us
all on the floor with our throats cut.”
“ Pshaw ! ” Dad answered ; “ what’s the use of
talking like that ? ” Then to Dave : “ Go out and
see if he’s in the barn.”
Dave fidgetted. He didn’t like the idea. Joe
giggled.
“Surely you’re not frightened ? ” Dad shouted.
Dave colored up.
“No—don’t think so,” he said; and, after a
pause, ‘ You go and see.”
It was Dad’s turn to feel uneasy. He pretended
to straighten the fire, and coughed several times.
Perhaps it’s just as well,” he said, “ to let him
be, to-night.”
Of course, Dad wasn’t afraid ; he said he wasn’t,
but he drove the pegs in the doors and windows
before going to bed that night.
* * « «
Next , morning, Dad said to Dave and Joe,
Come ’long, and we’ll see where he’s got to.”
In a gully at the back of the grass-paddock they
found him. He was ploughing—‘sitting astride
the highest limb of a fallen tree, and, in a hoarse
voice and strange, calling out—“ Gee, Captain—
come here, Tidy—wa-ay! ”
“ Blowed if I know ” —Dad muttered, coming
to a stand-still. “ Wonder if he is clean mad ? ”
Dave was speechless, and Joe began to tremble.
They listened. And as the man’s voice rang
out in the quiet gully and the echoes rumbled
round the ridge and the affrighted birds flew up,
a gloom fell over the place.
!- tj s no use kein’ afraid of him,” Dad went on.
’ We must go and bounce him, that’s all.” There
was a tremor, though, in Dad’s voice which Dave
didn’t like.
“See if he knows us, anyway”; and Dad
shouted, “Heigh!”
Jack looked up and immediately scrambled
from the limb. That was enough for Dave. He
turned and made tracks. So did Dad and Joe.
They ran. No one could have run harder. Terror
overcame Joe. He squealed and grabbed hold of
Dad’s shirt, which was ballooning in the wind.
“Let go!” Dad gasped. “ Danin y’, let me
go” —trying to shako hjm off. Rut Joe had great
faith in his parent, and clung to him closely.
When they had covered a hundred jmrds or so,
Dave glanced back, and seeing that Jack wasn’t
pursuing them, stopped and chuckled at the
others.
“Eh ? ” Dad said, completely winded —“ Eh ? ”
Then to Dave when he got some breath :
“Well you are an ass of a fellow. (Puff!)
What th’ devil did y’ run f’ ? ”
“ Wot did I run f’ ? Wot did you run f’ ? ”
“ Bah ! ” and Dad boldly led the way back.
“ Now look here (turning fiercely upon Joe) :
don’t you come catching hold of me again, or if y’
do I’ll knock y’r d head off. . . .• Clear home
altogether and get under the bed if y’r as frightened
as that.”
Joe slunk behind.
But when Dad did approach Jack, which
wasn’t until he had talked a great deal to him
across a big log, the latter didn’t show any desire
to take life, but allowed himself to be escorted
home and locked in the barn quietly enough.
Dad kept Jack confined in the barn several
days, and if anyone approached the door or the
cracks he would ask :
“ Is me father there yet ? ”
“ Your father’s dead and buried long ago, man,”
Dad used to tell him.
“Yes,” he would say, “but he’s alive again.
The missus keeps him in there ” —indicating the
house.
And sometimes when Dad was not about Joe
would put his mouth to a crack and say :
“Here’s y’r father, Jack!” Then, like a caged
beast, the man would howl and tramp up and
down, his eyes starting out of his head, while Joe
would bolt inside and tell mother that “Jack’s
getting out,” and nearly send her to her grave.
But one day Jack did get out, and, while mother
and Sal were ironing, came to the door with the
axe on his shoulder.
They dropped the irons and shrank into a
corner and cowered piteously too scared even
to cry out.
He took no notice of them, but, moving stealthily
on tip-toes, approached the bed-room door and
peeped in. He paused just a moment to grip the
axe with both hands. Then with a howl and a
bound he entered the room and shattered the
looking-glass into fragments.
He bent down and looked closely at the pieces.
“ He’s dead now,” he said calmly, and walked
out. Then he went to work at the post-holes again,
just as though nothing had happened.
Brisbane.
STEELE RUDD.
The champion absent-minded man the other
day engaged a suite of apartments with bathroom
attached at the Hotel Australia. He forgets all
about his private bathroom, and is seen impatient-
ly waiting his turn outside a general bathroom,
which has nobody in it, and wondering why the
man who left ten minutes earlier doesn’t come out.

Dear Sirs,—Being a sufferer for years from fibroid phthisis and bronchitis, I de-
sire to add my testimony in favor of your Irish Moss. As a soother of irritating
cough, with much" expectoration, I have found it to be the most effectual of anything
I have tried. It loosens the mucus, which, therefore, comes more readily away, and
the cough is thereby considerably pacified.
I remain, Yours respectfully,
THOMAS STOKES.
